Responding to Refutation by Proof Type
Author explains response to refutation by proof type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether to respond to a refutation by preventing the minor or major premise, based on the type of proof and the scholar's view on conditions.

Core Idea
The correct dialectical response depends on the epistemic status of the proof.

We learned earlier that a refutation (naqd) challenges a proof. But how do you answer it? The author explains that the answer is not one-size-fits-all: it depends on whether the proof yields certainty, is a legal analogy, or is merely a rational indicator. Understanding this nuance is essential for effective dialectical practice in jadal and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof type
Determine whether the respondent's proof is yaqini (certain), qiyas shar'i (legal analogy), or 'amara 'aqliyya (rational indicator). The first two are strong proofs; the third yields probable opinion.
The proof type dictates the available responses.
2
Check the view on conditions (tammat al-'illa)
For certain proofs and legal analogies, see whether the opponent considers conditions and removal of impediments as part of the complete cause. The majority (jumhur) do; the minority do not.
This affects whether man' al-kubra is allowed.
3
Apply the response rule
For yaqini or qiyas shar'i: if majority view, respond with man' al-sughra only; if minority view, man' al-kubra is also allowed. For 'amara 'aqliyya or istiqra': always respond with man' al-kubra, by consensus.
Man' al-sughra denies the counterexample falls under the minor; man' al-kubra denies the universal premise.
4
Test with examples
Work through the author's examples: burning wood (yaqini) and urine ablation (qiyas shar'i) show why the majority prevent the minor for coated wood or istihadah blood. The rational indicator of the horse at the bathhouse shows why all prevent the major.
The counterexample does not invalidate the proof; it only fails due to a hindrance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجاب عن هذا النقض عند الجمهور إذا كان دليل المعلل يقينيا أو قياسا شرعيا بمنع الكبرى) لأن الجمهور يجعلون الشروط وارتفاع الموانع من متهمات العلة بل يجاب) عندهم (بمنع الصغرى) فقط وأما عند من لا يجعلها من تتحتها فيجاب عنده بمنع الكبرى أيضا كقولنا هذا محرق لأنه حطب ملق في النار وكل حطب ملقى فى النار محرق وكقولنا خروج البول ناقض الوضوء لكونه خروج النجاسة وخروج المذى خروج النجاسة وخروجه ناقض له فان الأول دليل يقينى جار عنده في الخطب الملطخ بالطلق وهو دواء يمنع الاحراق ، والثانى قياس شرعى جار عنده في خروج دم لمستحاضة لكنهما ليسا باطلين ليكون التخلف عنهما لمانع وهو الطابق والاستمرار بحيث يعد عذرا ، وأما عند الجمهور فليسا بجاربين فيهم السكون قيد بلا مانع ملحوظا فى أوسطها ، وأما إذا كان الدليل أمارة عقلية كقولنا زيد فرسه في باب الحمام ومن كان فرسه في باب الحمام فهو في الحمام فيجاب بمنع التكبرى أيضا بالاتفاق فانها لوجرت فى عمر و فعلمنا أنه ليس فى الحمام بوجه آخر لا نقدح إفادته ظنه في جق زيد فلا يبطل بتخلف الحكم عنه ، وأما بطلانها عند المعارضة فلحصول الاعتقاد بعدم كون زيد فى الحمام لا للتخلف فتبصر وكذلك الاستقراء.
And this refutation is not answered — according to the majority — if the respondent's proof is certain (yaqini) or a legal analogy (qiyas shar'i) by preventing the major, because the majority consider the conditions and removal of impediments as part of the complete cause. Rather, it is answered by preventing the minor only. But according to those who do not consider them as part of the complete cause, it is answered by preventing the major as well. Like our saying: this is burning because it is wood thrown in fire, and every wood thrown in fire is burning. And our saying: the emission of urine nullifies ablution because it is the emission of impurity, and the emission of madhi is the emission of impurity and its emission nullifies. The first is a certain proof that runs, according to him, in wood coated with talc, which is a substance that prevents burning. The second is a legal analogy that runs, according to him, in the emission of istihada blood. But they are not invalid because the lack of consequence is due to a hindrance (talc and continuity) which is considered an excuse. According to the majority, they do not run in these cases because the condition 'without a hindrance' is included in their middle terms. And if the proof is a rational indicator ('amara 'aqliyya), like our saying: Zaid's horse is at the door of the bathhouse, and whoever's horse is at the door of the bathhouse is in the bathhouse, then it is answered by preventing the major unanimously. Because if it ran in 'Amr, and we know that he is not in the bathhouse by another means, that does not harm its conveying a likely opinion about Zaid, so it is not invalidated by the lack of consequence from it. And its invalidity in case of contradiction is due to the belief that Zaid is not in the bathhouse, not due to lack of consequence. Understand. And likewise induction (istiqra').
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage discusses how to respond to a refutation (naqd) based on the type of the respondent's proof. The author distinguishes three cases. First, if the proof is certain (yaqini) or a legal analogy (qiyas shar'i), the majority of scholars (jumhur) answer by preventing the minor premise (man' al-sughra) only, not the major, because they hold that the conditions and removal of impediments are part of the complete cause (tammat al-'illa). For example, a certain proof like 'this wood is burning because it is wood thrown into fire; every wood thrown into fire is burning' — this proof runs (according to the opponent) in the case of wood coated with talc, which prevents burning. But the majority say the proof does not run there because the condition 'without a hindrance' is implicit in the middle term. So they respond by denying that the case is truly an instance of the minor premise (i.e., the wood with talc is not 'wood thrown into fire' in the unrestricted sense). Similarly for the legal example of urine nullifying ablution, which runs in istihada blood; they respond by denying that istihada blood is included in the minor premise (i.e., it is not emission of impurity in the relevant sense). Thus, they prevent the minor. But those who do not make conditions part of the cause would answer by preventing the major, claiming that the universal statement is false due to the counterexample. The second case is when the proof is a rational indicator ('amara 'aqliyya), like the example of Zaid's horse at the bathhouse door. Here, all agree that the answer is to prevent the major, because rational indicators only yield probable opinion (zann), and a single counterexample does not necessarily invalidate the generalization that usually holds. If it ran in 'Amr but we know he is not in the bathhouse by another means, the proof is still useful for Zaid. Therefore, the refutation by lack of consequence does not defeat it; it is only defeated by a stronger contradictory evidence (mu'arada). The same applies to induction (istiqra'). This nuanced discussion shows the sophistication of Islamic dialectical theory.

Key Terms
أمارة عقلية
منع الصغرى
منع الكبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the majority (جمهور) answer a refutation against a certain proof?
A) By preventing the major premise.
B) By preventing the minor premise.
C) By declaring the proof invalid.
D) By using induction.

Key Takeaway
The appropriate dialectical response to a refutation depends on the proof type: for certain proofs and legal analogies, the majority answer by preventing the minor; for rational indicators, they prevent the major.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In a jadal session, the respondent says: 'This drink is forbidden because it is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' The objector says: 'But nabidh (date drink) is an intoxicant yet some scholars permit it.' Identify the proof type and state how the majority would answer. (Hint: Is nabidh truly an intoxicant in the relevant sense? Does it intoxicate in large quantities only?)
?
Task 2: Given the rational indicator: 'Zaid's horse is at the bathhouse door, so Zaid is in the bathhouse.' The objector says: 'But 'Amr's horse was at the door and he was not inside.' Should the respondent prevent the minor or the major? Explain why, referencing the passage.

The Metaphorical Objection (al-Naqd al-Majazi) in Jadal
Author classifies metaphorical naqd as an indirect attack on the major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a metaphorical objection (naqd majazi) and distinguish it from a direct naqd in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
A metaphorical objection attacks the major premise indirectly by applying the cause to an absurd case.

In jadal, not all objections are straightforward. The metaphorical naqd targets the claimant's major premise by showing that the common cause, if applied consistently, leads to an absurd conclusion. This lesson unpacks the author's explanation using the example of common sense and the pen, and shows how this objection actually challenges the major premise indirectly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's original argument
Find the claimant's evidence: its minor premise, major premise, and the common cause. In the example, the claimant argues that common sense is that by which perception occurs, and everything by which perception occurs is perceiving, so common sense is perceiving. The common cause is 'that by which the action occurs'.
2
Find the objector's counterexample
The objector applies the common cause to a different case: the pen is that by which writing occurs, and everything by which writing occurs is a writer, so the pen is a writer. This is absurd because a pen is not a writer.
This counterexample uses the same structure but with a different subject.
3
Recognize that the objection targets the major premise indirectly
The absurd consequence shows that the major premise of the claimant (or the common cause) cannot be universal. The objector attaches a correlation to show that the claimant's major premise leads to absurdity. This is called 'metaphorical' because it does not directly deny the major premise but does so via an analogy.
The author says this type is 'metaphorical' because it returns to the evidence of the major premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع مقدمة من دليل الجريان في علة والنقض في هذه الصورة نقض لتلك العلة في الحقيقة كما إذا استدل بأن الحس المشترك ما به الادراك وكل ما به الادراك فهو مدرك فيجرى بخلاصته في أن القلم كاتب بأنه ما به الكتابة وكل ما به الكتابة فهو كاتب فالعلة المشتركة كل ما به الفعل فهو فاعل ، وهو بضم ملازمة إليه يقوم على كبرى دليل المدعى ويضم ملازمة أخرى يجرى في كبرى دليل الجريان فالنقض هنا راجع في الحقيقة إلى دليل الكبرى فيليق أن يسمى هذا النوع من النقض نقضا مجازيا انتهى . وأما النوع الثالث فسيجيء فى الكتاب وهو النقض المكسور ولا يجاب عن هذا النقض عند الجمهور إذا كان دليل المعلل يقينيا أو قياسا شرعيا بمنع الكبرى لأن الجمهور يجعلون الشروط وارتفاع الموانع من متهمات العلة بل يجاب عندهم بمنع الصغرى فقط وأما عند من لم يجعلهما من متمماتها فيجاب عنه بمنع الكبرى كقولنا هذا محرق لأنه حطب ملق في النار وكل حطب ملقى فى النار محرق وقولنا خروج البول ناقض الوضوء لأنه خروج النجاسة كخروج المذى وخروجه ناقض فان الأول دليل يقينى جار عنده فى الحطب الملطخ بالطلق ، وهو دواء ممنع الاحراق والثاني قياس شرعى جار عنده في خروج دم الاستحاضة لكنهما ليسا بباطلين لكون التخلف عنهما لمانع وهو الطلق والاستمرار بحيث يعد عذرا ، وأما عند الجمهور فليسا يجار بين فيهما لتكون قيد بلا مانع ملحوظا في أوساطهما وأما إذا كان الدليل أمارة عقلية كقولنا زيد فرسه فى باب الحمام ومن كان فرسه في باب الحمام فهو في الحمام فيجاب بمنع الكبرى أيضا بالاتفاق فانها لوجرت فى عمرو فعلمنا أنه ليس في الحمام بوجه آخر لا يقدح إفادته ظنا في حق زيد ، فلا يبطله تخلف الحكم عنه ، وأما بطلانها عند المعارضة فلحصول الاعتقاد بعدم كونه في الحمام لا للتخلف فتبصر وكذا الاستقراء كذا قبل.
With the introduction from the evidence of applicability (jarayan) in the cause, and the objection (naqd) in this form is an objection to that cause in reality, like when he argues that the common sense is that by which perception occurs, and everything by which perception occurs is perceiving, so it applies in its summary to the pen being a writer, that it is that by which writing occurs, and everything by which writing occurs is a writer. So the common cause is everything by which the action occurs is an agent. And by attaching a correlation (mulazama) to it, it stands on the major premise of the claimant's evidence, and attaches another correlation that applies in the major premise of the evidence of applicability. So the objection here in reality refers to the evidence of the major premise, so it is appropriate to call this type of objection a metaphorical objection (naqd majazi). End. As for the third type, it will come later in the book, and it is the broken objection (al-naqd al-maksur). And this objection is not answered according to the majority (jumhur), if the claimant's evidence is certain (yaqini) or a legal analogy (qiyas shar'i), by denying the major premise (man' al-kubra), because the majority consider the conditions and the removal of preventives as part of the perfection of the cause. Rather, it is answered according to them by denying the minor premise (man' al-sughra) only. But according to those who do not consider them as part of its perfection, it is answered by denying the major premise, like our statement: 'This is burning because it is wood thrown in fire, and every wood thrown in fire is burning' and our statement: 'The exit of urine invalidates wudu because it is the exit of impurity like the exit of madhi, and its exit is invalidating.' The first is a certain evidence that applies according to him to wood smeared with talq, which is a medicine preventing burning, and the second is a legal analogy that applies according to him to the exit of istihada blood, but they are not invalid because the non-occurrence (takhalluf) for them is due to a preventer, which is the talq and continuity (istimrar) in a way that is considered an excuse. But according to the majority, they do not apply in them because the condition of 'without a preventer' is observed in their middle terms. And if the evidence is a rational indication (amara 'aqliyya), like our statement: 'Zayd's horse is at the door of the bathhouse, and whoever's horse is at the door of the bathhouse is in the bathhouse,' it is answered by denying the major premise also by agreement, because if it applied to 'Amr and we knew that he is not in the bathhouse in another way, its imparting of probability in the case of Zayd is not harmed, so the non-occurrence of the ruling for him does not invalidate it. And as for its invalidity in the case of contradiction, it is due to the occurrence of belief that Zayd is not in the bathhouse, not due to non-occurrence. So ponder. And likewise induction (istiqra'), as was said before.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition of the rules for responding to objections (naqd) in jadal. The author begins by describing a type of objection called 'metaphorical objection' (naqd majazi), where the objector attacks the major premise indirectly by showing that the common cause applies in a way that leads to absurdity. The example uses the common sense argument: if the common sense is that by which perception occurs, then it applies to the pen as writer, leading to a universal cause 'everything by which an action occurs is an agent'. The objector then attaches a correlation to show the major premise of the claimant is flawed. This type is called metaphorical because it actually targets the major premise indirectly. The author then notes that the third type (broken objection) will come later. Next, he discusses how to respond to a naqd when the claimant's evidence is certain (yaqini) or a legal analogy (qiyas shar'i). According to the majority (jumhur), since they consider conditions and removal of preventives as part of the perfection of the cause (tamamat al-illa), they do not allow denying the major premise; instead, they only deny the minor premise. For those who do not consider conditions and preventives as part of the perfection, they can deny the major premise. Two examples illustrate: (1) 'This is burning because it is wood thrown in fire' – this certain evidence applies to wood smeared with talq (a fire-preventive), but the non-burning is due to a preventer, so the evidence is not invalidated. (2) 'Urine invalidates wudu because it is impurity like madhi' – this legal analogy applies to istihada blood, but non-invalidation is due to a preventer (continuity). For the majority, these evidences do not apply because the condition 'without a preventer' is built into the middle term. For rational indications (amara 'aqliyya), the responder can always deny the major premise. The example: 'Zayd's horse is at the bathhouse door, so Zayd is inside' – even if it fails for 'Amr, it still gives probability for Zayd. Invalidity only occurs if a contradictory belief is established, not just due to non-occurrence. The same applies to induction. The passage teaches the nuanced interplay between evidence type and school of thought in jadal.

Key Terms
النقض
النقض المجازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the metaphorical naqd example, what does the objector show by applying the common cause to the pen?
A) The pen is not a writer, so the minor premise is false.
B) The claimant's major premise leads to an absurd conclusion, so it is not universally true.
C) The objector denies the minor premise by showing the pen does not write.
D) The common cause is invalid because pens are not agents.

Key Takeaway
A metaphorical naqd indirectly attacks the major premise by applying the common cause to a counterexample that yields an absurd conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a jadal session, the claimant argues: 'Every act that causes harm is forbidden. Smoking causes harm, so smoking is forbidden.' The objector says: 'Then eating too much honey would be forbidden because it can cause harm in excess.' Identify whether this is a direct or metaphorical naqd and explain how it targets the major premise.

Responding to Naqd: Denying Major or Minor Premise
Author explains rules for responding to naqd with man' al-kubra and man' al-sughra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether to respond to a naqd by denying the major premise (man' al-kubra) or the minor premise (man' al-sughra) based on the type of evidence and the school's stance on conditions and preventives.

Core Idea
For certain or analogical evidence, the majority only allow denying the minor premise; for rational indications, denying the major premise is always allowed.

After understanding types of naqd, the passage explains the appropriate responses. The key factor is the type of evidence (certain/analogical vs. rational indication) and whether conditions and preventives are considered part of the cause's perfection. The majority (jumhur) restrict denying the major premise for certain and analogical evidence, while others allow it. This lesson breaks down the rules and examples.

Guided Path
1
Classify the type of evidence in the claimant's argument
Determine if the evidence is certain (yaqini), a legal analogy (qiyas shar'i), or a rational indication (amara 'aqliyya). In the passage, the examples include: 'This wood is burning because it is in fire' (certain) and 'Urine invalidates wudu because it is impurity like madhi' (analogical).
Certain evidence is based on sense perception or necessary knowledge; analogical evidence is based on a shared 'illa.
2
Check the school's position on conditions and preventives
The majority (jumhur) consider conditions and removal of preventives as part of the perfection of the cause (tamamat al-illa). Therefore, if a non-occurrence is due to a preventer, the evidence does not apply because the condition 'without a preventer' is built into the middle term. Those who do not consider them as part of the perfection allow denying the major premise.
Example: wood smeared with talq does not burn because talq is a preventer; for the majority, the evidence 'wood in fire burns' does not apply because the condition 'without preventer' is not fulfilled.
3
Apply the rule for response
For certain and analogical evidence, the majority only allow man' al-sughra (denying the minor premise). For rational indications, man' al-kubra (denying the major premise) is allowed by agreement. Also, for those who do not consider conditions as part of the cause, man' al-kubra is allowed even for certain/analogical evidence.
Example of rational indication: 'Zayd's horse at bathhouse door implies Zayd is inside' – even if it fails for 'Amr, the probability for Zayd remains; so man' al-kubra is appropriate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع مقدمة من دليل الجريان في علة والنقض في هذه الصورة نقض لتلك العلة في الحقيقة كما إذا استدل بأن الحس المشترك ما به الادراك وكل ما به الادراك فهو مدرك فيجرى بخلاصته في أن القلم كاتب بأنه ما به الكتابة وكل ما به الكتابة فهو كاتب فالعلة المشتركة كل ما به الفعل فهو فاعل ، وهو بضم ملازمة إليه يقوم على كبرى دليل المدعى ويضم ملازمة أخرى يجرى في كبرى دليل الجريان فالنقض هنا راجع في الحقيقة إلى دليل الكبرى فيليق أن يسمى هذا النوع من النقض نقضا مجازيا انتهى . وأما النوع الثالث فسيجيء فى الكتاب وهو النقض المكسور ولا يجاب عن هذا النقض عند الجمهور إذا كان دليل المعلل يقينيا أو قياسا شرعيا بمنع الكبرى لأن الجمهور يجعلون الشروط وارتفاع الموانع من متهمات العلة بل يجاب عندهم بمنع الصغرى فقط وأما عند من لم يجعلهما من متمماتها فيجاب عنه بمنع الكبرى كقولنا هذا محرق لأنه حطب ملق في النار وكل حطب ملقى فى النار محرق وقولنا خروج البول ناقض الوضوء لأنه خروج النجاسة كخروج المذى وخروجه ناقض فان الأول دليل يقينى جار عنده فى الحطب الملطخ بالطلق ، وهو دواء ممنع الاحراق والثاني قياس شرعى جار عنده في خروج دم الاستحاضة لكنهما ليسا بباطلين لكون التخلف عنهما لمانع وهو الطلق والاستمرار بحيث يعد عذرا ، وأما عند الجمهور فليسا يجار بين فيهما لتكون قيد بلا مانع ملحوظا في أوساطهما وأما إذا كان الدليل أمارة عقلية كقولنا زيد فرسه فى باب الحمام ومن كان فرسه في باب الحمام فهو في الحمام فيجاب بمنع الكبرى أيضا بالاتفاق فانها لوجرت فى عمرو فعلمنا أنه ليس في الحمام بوجه آخر لا يقدح إفادته ظنا في حق زيد ، فلا يبطله تخلف الحكم عنه ، وأما بطلانها عند المعارضة فلحصول الاعتقاد بعدم كونه في الحمام لا للتخلف فتبصر وكذا الاستقراء كذا قبل.
With the introduction from the evidence of applicability (jarayan) in the cause, and the objection (naqd) in this form is an objection to that cause in reality, like when he argues that the common sense is that by which perception occurs, and everything by which perception occurs is perceiving, so it applies in its summary to the pen being a writer, that it is that by which writing occurs, and everything by which writing occurs is a writer. So the common cause is everything by which the action occurs is an agent. And by attaching a correlation (mulazama) to it, it stands on the major premise of the claimant's evidence, and attaches another correlation that applies in the major premise of the evidence of applicability. So the objection here in reality refers to the evidence of the major premise, so it is appropriate to call this type of objection a metaphorical objection (naqd majazi). End. As for the third type, it will come later in the book, and it is the broken objection (al-naqd al-maksur). And this objection is not answered according to the majority (jumhur), if the claimant's evidence is certain (yaqini) or a legal analogy (qiyas shar'i), by denying the major premise (man' al-kubra), because the majority consider the conditions and the removal of preventives as part of the perfection of the cause. Rather, it is answered according to them by denying the minor premise (man' al-sughra) only. But according to those who do not consider them as part of its perfection, it is answered by denying the major premise, like our statement: 'This is burning because it is wood thrown in fire, and every wood thrown in fire is burning' and our statement: 'The exit of urine invalidates wudu because it is the exit of impurity like the exit of madhi, and its exit is invalidating.' The first is a certain evidence that applies according to him to wood smeared with talq, which is a medicine preventing burning, and the second is a legal analogy that applies according to him to the exit of istihada blood, but they are not invalid because the non-occurrence (takhalluf) for them is due to a preventer, which is the talq and continuity (istimrar) in a way that is considered an excuse. But according to the majority, they do not apply in them because the condition of 'without a preventer' is observed in their middle terms. And if the evidence is a rational indication (amara 'aqliyya), like our statement: 'Zayd's horse is at the door of the bathhouse, and whoever's horse is at the door of the bathhouse is in the bathhouse,' it is answered by denying the major premise also by agreement, because if it applied to 'Amr and we knew that he is not in the bathhouse in another way, its imparting of probability in the case of Zayd is not harmed, so the non-occurrence of the ruling for him does not invalidate it. And as for its invalidity in the case of contradiction, it is due to the occurrence of belief that Zayd is not in the bathhouse, not due to non-occurrence. So ponder. And likewise induction (istiqra'), as was said before.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition of the rules for responding to objections (naqd) in jadal. The author begins by describing a type of objection called 'metaphorical objection' (naqd majazi), where the objector attacks the major premise indirectly by showing that the common cause applies in a way that leads to absurdity. The example uses the common sense argument: if the common sense is that by which perception occurs, then it applies to the pen as writer, leading to a universal cause 'everything by which an action occurs is an agent'. The objector then attaches a correlation to show the major premise of the claimant is flawed. This type is called metaphorical because it actually targets the major premise indirectly. The author then notes that the third type (broken objection) will come later. Next, he discusses how to respond to a naqd when the claimant's evidence is certain (yaqini) or a legal analogy (qiyas shar'i). According to the majority (jumhur), since they consider conditions and removal of preventives as part of the perfection of the cause (tamamat al-illa), they do not allow denying the major premise; instead, they only deny the minor premise. For those who do not consider conditions and preventives as part of the perfection, they can deny the major premise. Two examples illustrate: (1) 'This is burning because it is wood thrown in fire' – this certain evidence applies to wood smeared with talq (a fire-preventive), but the non-burning is due to a preventer, so the evidence is not invalidated. (2) 'Urine invalidates wudu because it is impurity like madhi' – this legal analogy applies to istihada blood, but non-invalidation is due to a preventer (continuity). For the majority, these evidences do not apply because the condition 'without a preventer' is built into the middle term. For rational indications (amara 'aqliyya), the responder can always deny the major premise. The example: 'Zayd's horse is at the bathhouse door, so Zayd is inside' – even if it fails for 'Amr, it still gives probability for Zayd. Invalidity only occurs if a contradictory belief is established, not just due to non-occurrence. The same applies to induction. The passage teaches the nuanced interplay between evidence type and school of thought in jadal.

Key Terms
منع الكبرى
منع الصغرى
الشروط وارتفاع الموانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Man' al-kubra only
B) Man' al-sughra only
C) Either man' al-kubra or man' al-sughra
D) Neither; the evidence must be discarded

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of denying the major premise in jadal depends on the evidence type and whether conditions/preventives are considered part of the cause.

According to the majority, should the response be man' al-kubra or man' al-sughra? Explain why, assuming this is a legal analogy.

The Structure of the Minor Premise in Jadal Refutation
Author refines the structure of the minor premise in jadal refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the composition of the minor premise in a jadal refutation and defend it against the paradox of self-containment.

Core Idea
The minor premise in jadal is a composite, not a self-referential paradox.

In the art of dialectic (jadal), refuting an opponent's evidence often involves showing that the evidence applies to cases where the ruling is absent (takhalluf). The standard method uses a minor premise that is actually composed of two logical steps. The author explains this as a simplification (musamaha) to avoid a deeper logical complexity, and then addresses a subtle objection about self-containment. Understanding this move sharpens your ability to follow and construct dialectical refutations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the minor premise
The minor premise in the refutation is said to include two premises: jarayan (applicability of the evidence) and the premise leading to takhalluf (non-occurrence). Notice that the author says this is a musamaha (simplification).
2
Unpack the simplification
The second premise is actually a major premise that, together with the first, yields an intermediate conclusion: 'This evidence is applicable to such-and-such material, and everything applicable to such-and-such material is applicable in the case of non-occurrence, so this evidence is applicable in the case of non-occurrence.' So calling it part of the minor is a simplification for dialectical convenience.
This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure.
3
Analyze the objection of self-containment
An objector argues: if the minor premise contains two premises, then the minor premise contains itself (since one of those premises is the minor itself?), leading to absurdity. This seems like a paradox of self-reference.
The objection is based on the idea that the container and the contained would be identical.
4
Examine the author's response
The author denies the entailment: the container is the composite whole (the minor premise as a unit), while the contained are the individual premises (its parts). Thus, no self-containment occurs. This distinction resolves the paradox.
This shows the importance of distinguishing between a composite and its components in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كانت الصغرى من الدليل المذكور مشتملة على مقدمتين وهما الجريان والتخلف بمنع الجريان تارة وسند تحرير الدليل كلا أو بعضا أو تحرير المدعى والتخلف تارة أخرى. ولا يخفى أن هذا مسامحة منه لأن المقدمة الثانية كبرى تنتج مع المقدمة الأولى أن دليل المعلل جار فى المتخلف بأن يقال إن هذا الدليل جار في المادة الفلانية وكل جار فى المادة الفلانية جار فى المتخلف فهذا الدليل جار فى المتخلف فتضم إليها الكبرى القائلة بأن كل دليل جار فى المتخلف فهو باطل فلما حذف الصغرى وأقيم دليلها مقامها سومح، وقيل إن الصغرى مشتملة على مقدمتين. فان قلت إذا كانت الصغرى مشتملة على مقدمتين لزم اشتمال الشيء على نفسه وهو باطل. قلت لا نسلم لزوم اشتمال الشيء على نفسه لم لا يجوز أن يكون المشتمل المجموع من حيث هو هو والمشتمل عليه كل واحد منهما فلا محذور.
And since the minor premise of the mentioned evidence includes two premises – namely, jarayan (applicability) and takhalluf (non-occurrence) – with the denial of jarayan sometimes, and its basis is the analysis (tahrir) of the evidence as a whole or in part, or the analysis of the claim, and denial of takhalluf at other times. And it is not hidden that this is a simplification (musamaha) from him, because the second premise is a major premise that together with the first premise yields that the claimant's evidence is applicable in the case of non-occurrence, by saying: 'This evidence is applicable to such-and-such material, and everything applicable to such-and-such material is applicable in the case of non-occurrence, so this evidence is applicable in the case of non-occurrence.' Then the major premise saying 'Every evidence applicable in the case of non-occurrence is invalid' is added to it. So when the minor premise was omitted and its evidence was put in its place, it was a simplification, and it was said that the minor premise includes two premises. If you say: 'If the minor premise includes two premises, then it would entail the inclusion of a thing by itself, which is false.' I say: 'We do not accept that it entails the inclusion of a thing by itself. Why not permit that the container is the composite (majmu') as such, and the contained is each one of the parts? So there is no harm.'
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a technical point in jadal methodology. The author explains that in the standard method of refuting evidence by showing it applies in cases of non-occurrence (takhalluf), the minor premise is actually composed of two premises: one about applicability (jarayan) and one leading to the conclusion that the evidence is applicable in the non-occurring case. This composition is a simplification (musamaha) because the second premise is really a major premise that, with the first, yields an intermediate conclusion. The author then anticipates an objection: if the minor premise contains two premises, that would mean the minor premise contains itself (ishtimal al-shay' 'ala nafsihi), which is absurd. The author responds by denying the entailment: the container (the minor premise) is the composite whole, while the contained are the individual premises – thus no self-containment occurs. This is a delicate logical point about the meta-structure of arguments in jadal, showing how the practitioners of dialectic carefully avoid self-reference paradoxes.

Key Terms
الجريان
التخلف
مسامحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) It leads to an infinite regress
D) It is rejected as incorrect

Key Takeaway
The minor premise in jadal refutation is a composite of two premises, and the apparent self-containment is resolved by distinguishing the composite from its parts.

Refutation by Leading to Absurdity: The Objection
Author describes refuting evidence by showing it implies circularity or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a refutation (naqd) by accusing an opponent's evidence of implying a vicious circle (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) and deduce its absurdity.

Core Idea
If evidence implies an absurdity, the evidence itself is absurd.

In Islamic dialectic, one of the sharpest tools is showing that your opponent's own evidence leads to an impossibility. This lesson explains how the objector (naqid) uses the threat of circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) to dismantle an argument. You will learn the structure of this refutation and why its major premise is considered self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the evidence under attack
Locate the claimant's (mu'allil) evidence (dalil) that the objector is targeting. In the example, the evidence is 'Man is a human because he is a human' – a clear case of circular definition.
2
Show the implication of circle or regress
Argue that the evidence unconditionally implies a circle (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The key phrase is 'mustalzim lil-dawr la bi-shart shay'' – i.e., the implication is unconditional.
3
Apply the major premise
State the major premise: 'Everything that implies an absurdity is itself absurd' (kullu ma yastalzim al-muhal fahuwa muhal). The author says this premise is self-evident (badihi) and cannot be denied, whether the evidence is transmitted or rational, certain or probable.
4
Conclude the refutation
The conclusion follows: the evidence is absurd (muhal), and therefore invalid as proof.
The minor premise (evidence implies circle/regress) itself contains two premises, but that is a simplification; in reality it is the evidence for the minor premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل الناقض على بطلان دليل المعلل بأنه مستلزم للدور لا بشرط شيء أو التسلسل لا بشرط شيء، وقيل أي مثلا وهو الظاهر وكذا سائر المحالات كاجتماع النقيضين وارتفاعهما. وهو أي الدور أو التسلسل والواو إما حالية أو عاطفة ولا يخفى وجهه محال. والصغرى مشتملة على مقدمتين أيضا وفيه مسامحة أيضا لأنها في الحقيقة دليل الصغرى. وتقريره: دليلك مستلزم للمحال لأنه مستلزم للدور أو التسلسل مثلا وكل واحد منهما محال، ينتج من غير المتعارف هذا الدليل مستلزم للمحال وكل ما يستلزم المحال فهو محال. كقولنا الإنسان بشر لأنه بشر وكل بشر بشر. ولا مجال لمنع الكبرى هنا سواء كان دليلك نقليا أو عقليا وسواء كان يقينيا أو ظنيا لأنها بديهية فيكون منعها مكابرة. وأيضا كما لا مجال لمنع الكبرى في الشاهد الأول. بل قد يمنع الاستلزام الذي هو صغرى دليل الصغرى المطوية وسنده تحرير أجزاء الدليل. وقد يمنع الاستحالة وهي كبرى وسنده تحرير الدور أو التسلسل لأن بعض الدور وهو الدور المعي وبعض التسلسل كالتسلسل في الأمور المعدة والاعتبارية ليس محالا.
And the objector may argue for the invalidity of the claimant's evidence by saying that it implies a vicious circle (dawr) unconditionally or an infinite regress (tasalsul) unconditionally. And it is said: that is, for example, and it is apparent, and likewise other absurdities like the conjunction of two contradictories or their removal. And that (i.e., the circle or regress) is impossible, and the waw is either circumstantial or conjunctive, and its aspect is not hidden. And the minor premise also includes two premises, and there is also a simplification in it because it is in reality the evidence for the minor premise. Its formulation: Your evidence implies an absurdity because it implies a circle or regress, for example, and each of them is impossible, producing in an unconventional way: This evidence implies an absurdity, and everything that implies an absurdity is absurd. Like our statement: 'Man is a human because he is a human, and every human is a human.' And there is no room to deny the major premise here, whether your evidence is transmitted (naqli) or rational ('aqli), and whether it is certain (yaqini) or probable (zanni), because it is self-evident (badihiyya), so denying it would be obstinacy (mukabara). Also, just as there is no room to deny the major premise in the first example (shahid). Rather, the implication (istilzam) – which is the minor premise of the folded minor premise's evidence – may be denied, and its basis is the analysis (tahrir) of the parts of the evidence. And the impossibility (istihala) – which is the major premise – may be denied, and its basis is the analysis of the circle or regress, because some circles, namely the conceptual circle (al-dawr al-ma'ni), and some regresses, like the regress in preparatory and conventional matters, are not impossible.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage expands on a second type of refutation in jadal: showing that the opponent's evidence leads to an absurdity (muhal) such as circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), or other impossibilities like contradictory opposites. The author explains that the structure of this refutation also has a minor premise composed of two premises, but that is a simplification. The example given is a circular definition: 'Man is a human because he is a human, and every human is a human' – this clearly implies a circle. The author states that in such cases, the major premise (that everything implying an absurdity is absurd) cannot be denied because it is self-evident (badihi). However, the defender has two possible responses: (1) deny the implication (istilzam) – i.e., deny that the evidence actually implies a circle or regress – by analyzing the parts of the evidence (tahrir). (2) Deny the impossibility (istihala) of the circle or regress in certain specific cases, such as conceptual circles (al-dawr al-ma'ni) or regresses in preparatory matters (al-umur al-mu'adda) and conventional matters (al-i'tibariyya). These types of circles/regresses are not considered impossible by some scholars. This shows the sophistication of jadal: even when an absurdity is alleged, the defender can counter by either breaking the claim of implication or softening the definition of absurdity in certain contexts.

Key Terms
استلزام المحال
الدور
التسلسل
دليل الصغرى
بديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the major premise 'everything that implies an absurdity is absurd' not be denied?
A) Because it is found in the Quran and Sunnah.
B) Because it is self-evident (badihi), so denying it is obstinacy (mukabara).
C) Because it is a consensus of the scholars.
D) Because it is a logical deduction from the minor premise.

Key Takeaway
The refutation by leading to absurdity is a powerful dialectical tool: if you show that an opponent's evidence implies an impossibility (circle or regress), you have proven the evidence itself absurd, and the major premise of this refutation is self-evident and cannot be denied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a refutation by leading to absurdity against the following argument from a hypothetical debate: 'The Quran is the word of Allah because it is revealed.' Show that this implies a circle (the evidence assumes what it tries to prove).

Defending Against Refutation by Absurdity: Denying Implication or Impossibility
Author describes possible responses to refutation by absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to formulate and evaluate a defense against a refutation by leading to absurdity by either denying the implication (man' al-istilzam) or denying the impossibility (man' al-istihala) in specific contexts.

Core Idea
Not all circles and regresses are impossible; conceptual circles and regresses in conventional matters are exceptions.

When your evidence is accused of implying a circle or infinite regress, all is not lost. This lesson explores two sophisticated defenses from the jadal tradition: you can deny that your evidence actually implies a circle (by careful analysis of its parts), or you can deny that the circle or regress is truly impossible, especially if it is a conceptual circle (dawr ma'ni) or a regress in preparatory matters (umur mu'adda). These nuances show the depth of Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accusation
Determine exactly what circle or regress the objector claims your evidence implies. For example, is it a conceptual circle where terms define each other, or a regress of causes?
2
Option A: Deny the implication (man' al-istilzam)
Analyze the evidence thoroughly (tahrir) to show that it does not actually entail the alleged circle or regress. This may involve clarifying definitions or breaking down the argument into parts.
The passage says the basis for this denial is 'tahrir ajza' al-dalil' (analysis of the parts of the evidence).
3
Option B: Deny the impossibility (man' al-istihala)
Argue that the type of circle or regress is not impossible. For example, conceptual circles (al-dawr al-ma'ni) are permissible because they involve mutual dependence in definition, not causal dependence. Similarly, regresses in preparatory or conventional matters (al-umur al-mu'adda wa al-i'tibariyya) are not impossible because they are not real causal chains.
The passage explicitly states that these types are 'not impossible' (laysa muhalan).
4
Evaluate the defense
Assess whether the defense is sound. Does the analysis genuinely break the implication? Is the exception (conceptual circle or preparatory regress) accepted in the specific discipline? The strength of the defense depends on the context.

Source Text

According to the passage, what is a valid basis for denying the impossibility (istihala) of a circle or regress?
A) Claiming the evidence comes from revelation.
B) Showing the circle is a conceptual circle (dawr ma'ni) or the regress is in preparatory matters.
C) Arguing that the opponent is ignorant of logic.
D) Denying the major premise of the refutation.

Key Takeaway
A defender can rebut an absurdity refutation by either denying that the evidence implies a circle/regress (through careful analysis) or by denying that the alleged circle/regress is truly impossible (citing the permissible types).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Structure of the Witness of Failure (Shahed al-Takhalluf)
Author explains the two premises of the witness of failure and defends its logical structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical structure of the witness of failure (shahed al-takhalluf) and defend its coherence against the self-containment objection.

Core Idea
The witness of failure uses two premises to show a proof contradicts itself.

In dialectical reasoning (jadal), the witness of failure is a powerful tool to refute an opponent's proof. It works by showing that the proof, though claimed to be universal, fails to apply in a specific case where it should apply. The author of our text explains the two premises that make up this witness: jarayan (the proof's applicability) and takhalluf (its failure). He then addresses a subtle objection about the structure of the minor premise. In this lesson, we will dissect this logical construction and see how it withstands criticism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Find the Arabic terms jarayan (جريان) and takhalluf (تخلف) in the passage. Understand that jarayan is the claim that the proof applies to a certain matter, and takhalluf is the claim that the proof fails to apply to a case where it should.
These two premises are the core components of the witness of failure.
2
Understand the logical chain
The author explains that the second premise (takhalluf) is a major premise that, combined with the first, yields an intermediate conclusion: that the proof applies to the case where it fails. Then a third premise (every proof that applies to a failure case is invalid) is combined to reach the final refutation.
This is a compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab).
3
Analyze the self-containment objection
An objector argues: if the minor premise (which itself consists of two premises) contains itself, that would be absurd. Locate this objection in the text and understand its logical force.
The objection targets the structure where a premise appears to be composed of premises that include itself.
4
Evaluate the author's response
The author distinguishes between the composite (مجموع) as the container and each individual premise as the contained. Thus, the minor premise as a whole contains its parts, not itself. This resolves the apparent paradox.
This response preserves the logical consistency of the witness of failure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشتملة على مقدمتين) وهما الجريان والتخلف ) بمنع الجريان تارة ) وسند تحرير الدليل كلا و بعضا أو تحرير المدعى (والتخلف) تارة أخرى ) ولا يخفى أن هذا مسامحة منه لأن المقدمة الثانية كبرى تنتج مع المقدمة الأولى أن دليل المعلل جار فى المتخلف بأن يقال إن هذا الدليل جار في المادة الفلانية وكل جار فى المادة الفلانية جار فى المتخلف فهذا الدليل جار فى المتخلف فتضم إليها الكبرى القائلة بأن كل دليل جار فى المتخلف فهو باطل فلما حذف الصغرى وأقيم دليلها مقامها سومح، وقيل إن الصغرى مشتملة على مقدمتين . فان قلت إذا كانت الصغرى مشتملة على مقدمتين لزم اشتمال الشيء على نفسه وهو باطل . قلت لا نسلم لزوم اشتمال الشيء على نفسه لم لا يجوز أن يكون المشتمل المجموع من حيث هو هو والمشتمل عليه كل واحد منهما فلا محذور
(It consists of two premises) which are jarayan (running) and takhalluf (failure). (By preventing jarayan sometimes) and the basis for refining the proof, entirely or partially, or refining the claim (and takhalluf) sometimes. And it is not hidden that this is a concession from him, because the second premise is a major premise that together with the first premise yields that the proof of the proponent applies to that which is left behind, by saying: 'This proof applies to such and such matter, and everything that applies to such and such matter applies to that which is left behind, so this proof applies to that which is left behind.' Then it is combined with the major premise stating that every proof that applies to that which is left behind is invalid. So when the minor premise was omitted and its proof was put in its place, it was conceded. And it was said that the minor premise consists of two premises. If you say: 'If the minor premise consists of two premises, then this would entail a thing containing itself, which is absurd.' I say: 'We do not accept the entailment of a thing containing itself. Why is it not permissible that the container is the composite (majmoo') as such, and the contained is each one of them? So there is no problem.'
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical explanation of the structure of the 'witness of failure' (shahed al-takhalluf) used in disputation (jadal). The author describes the two premises: the first is 'jarayan' (the given proof running/applying to a similar case) and the second is 'takhalluf' (the proof failing to apply in a case where it should). The critic can object by 'preventing jarayan' or 'preventing takhalluf'. The author then notes a subtle concession: the second premise is actually a major premise that produces a conclusion together with the first premise. The original structure would have a minor premise (the proof applies to the specific case) and a major premise (what applies to that case applies to the left-behind case). But the author says that the minor premise is omitted and its proof is placed in its stead, leading to a concessional form. An objection is raised: if the minor premise itself consists of two premises, then we would have a thing (the minor premise) containing itself (since it would be made of two premises including itself)? The author answers that this is not necessary: the container (the composite) is different from the contained (each premise individually). The argument is akin to saying that a syllogism as a whole is a composite of premises, and the premises are parts, not a self-containment. This discussion is crucial for understanding the precise structure of a refutation by takhalluf.

A) To show the proof is certain
B) To demonstrate that the proof leads to a contradiction
C) To prove that the proponent's claim is false by showing the proof fails where it should hold
D) To establish a new proof

Key Takeaway
The witness of failure is a compound refutation that leverages the contradiction between a proof's claimed applicability and its actual failure in a specific case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following proof: 'Every sale of gharar (uncertainty) is invalid because it leads to dispute. The sale of a missing item is gharar, so it is invalid.' Construct a witness of failure by finding a case where gharar exists (e.g., buying a house unseen) but the ruling of invalidity does not hold (if the buyer accepts), showing the 'illah fails.
?
In a theological debate, the opponent argues that 'the world is eternal because everything that exists necessarily exists eternally.' Show how a witness of failure works by identifying a case where something exists but is not eternal (e.g., accidents), thereby refuting the proof.

The Second Witness: Entailment of Circularity or Regress
Author explains the second witness where the proof entails absurdity like circle or regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the second witness and evaluate the respondent's possible objections in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Not all circularity or regress is impossible; the key is specifying which type is entailed.

In jadal (dialectical debate), the critic often uses the second witness to show that the proponent's proof leads to an absurdity like circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). This passage explains how the critic structures this argument and how the respondent can defend against it. Understanding this nuanced move allows you to follow and participate in sophisticated theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the critic's claim
The critic asserts that the proponent's proof entails circularity or regress, which are absurd. Notice the phrase 'مستلزم للدور أو التسلسل'.
This is the core of the second witness.
2
Break down the argument structure
The critic's argument has a folded minor premise: the proof entails circularity/regress (a claim that itself has two parts), and a major premise: everything that entails absurdity is absurd. The conclusion is that the proponent's proof is absurd.
The minor premise is 'the proof entails circularity/regress' and the major is 'every such entailment is absurd'.
3
Analyze the respondent's possible denials
The respondent can either deny the entailment (the minor) by refining the proof's parts, or deny the impossibility (the major) by specifying that some circularities or regresses are not impossible. The hashia adds precision: if the critic means the impossible type, deny the minor; if absolute, deny the major.
This shows the importance of distinguishing absolute vs. specific claims in debate.

وإلى الثاني أشار بقوله (وقد يستدل الناقض على بطلان دليل المعلل بأنه أى دليلك (مستلزم للدور) لا بشرط شيء (أو التسلسل لا بشرط شيء ، وقيل أى مثلا وهو الظاهر وكذا سائر المحالات كاجتماع النقيضين وارتفاعهما (وهو) أى الدور أو التسلسل والواو إما حالية أو عاطقة ولا يخفى وجهه (محال) والصغرى مشتملة على مقدمتين أيضا وفيه مسامحة أيضا لأنها في الحقيقة دليل الصغرى وتقريره دليلك مستلزم للمحال لأنه مستلزم المدور أو التسلل مثلا وكل واحد منهما محال ينتج من غير المتعارف هذا الدليل مستلزم للمحال (وكل ما ) أى كل دليل ( يستلزم المحال فهو محال) كقولنا الانسان بشر لأنه بشر وكل بشر بشر ) ولا مجاله لمنع السكيرى هنا سواء كان دليلك نقليا أو عقليا وسواء كان يقينيا أو ظنيا لأنها بديهية فيكون منعها مكابرة (أيضا) أى كما لا مجال لنع الكبرى فى الشاهد الأول ( بل قد يمنع الاستلزام) الذى هو صغرى دليل الصغرى المطوية وسنده تحرير أجزاء الدليل ( وقد يمنع الاستحالة) وهى كبرى و بنده تحرير الدور أو التسلسله لأن بعض الدور) وهو الدور المعى (و) بعض (التسلسل كالتسلسل في الأمور المعدة والاعتبارية ) غير محال كما بين في محله. قال في الحاشية وهنا تقرير آخر ، وهو أن يقال إنه مستلزم للدور أو التسلسل وكل ما يستلزم فهو محال فحينئذ يردد المجيب في الصغرى ويقول : إن أردت أنه مستلزم للدور المحال أو التسلسل المحال فلا نسلم الصغرى ، وإن أردت المطلق فلا نسلم الكبرى
And to the second he pointed with his saying: (And the critic may adduce against the validity of the proponent's proof that it, i.e., your proof, entails circularity (dawr) unconditionally, or infinite regress (tasalsul) unconditionally. And it was said: meaning for example, and that is apparent. And likewise other absurdities like the conjunction of opposites and their removal. And it (i.e., circularity or infinite regress) is absurd. And the minor premise consists of two premises as well, and there is concession in it because it is in reality the proof of the minor premise. And its explanation: your proof entails absurdity because it entails circularity or regress for example, and each one of them is absurd—so it yields, unconventionally, that this proof entails absurdity. (And everything that entails absurdity is absurd) like our saying: 'The human is flesh because he is flesh, and every flesh is flesh.' And there is no room for its prevention by the drunk here, whether your proof is transmitted or rational, certain or probable, because they are self-evident, so preventing them is obstinacy. (Also) as there is no room for prevention of the major in the first witness. (But the entailment) which is the minor premise of the folded minor premise may be prevented, and its basis is refining the parts of the proof. (And the impossibility may be prevented), which is the major, and its basis is refining the circularity or regress, because some circularity—the specific circularity—and some regress—like regress in preparatory and considered matters—is not impossible, as explained in its place. In the hashia it says: And here is another formulation, which is that it is said: 'It entails circularity or regress, and everything that entails it is impossible.' Then the respondent (mujīb) vacillates in the minor premise and says: 'If you mean that it entails impossible circularity or impossible regress, then we do not accept the minor; if you mean the absolute, then we do not accept the major.'
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition of the second type of witness used in refutation (naqd), namely that the proponent's proof entails an absurdity such as circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The author explains that the critic uses this witness to show that the proof leads to a contradiction or an impossible chain. The minor premise (that the proof entails such-and-such) itself consists of two premises, analogous to the first witness. The author then notes a subtlety: the major premise 'every proof that entails absurdity is absurd' is so self-evident that denying it is mere obstinacy (mukābara). The respondent can answer in two ways: either by denying the entailment (the minor premise) or by denying the impossibility (the major premise) by specifying that not every circularity or regress is impossible. For example, circularity in definitions that is not vicious, or regress in matters of preparation (mu'addāt) or relative (i'tibāriyya) affairs, are considered not impossible. The hashia adds another formulation: the respondent can argue that if the critic means a specific impossible type, then the minor is not proven; if he means absolute, then the major is not accepted because some types are not impossible. This nuanced approach shows the sophistication of jadal in differentiating between absolute claims and restricted ones.

The second witness in jadal exposes proof that entails absurdity, but the respondent can defeat it by distinguishing between types of circularity or regress.

Answering Refutation and Distinguishing Critic from Objector
Describes answering naqd and distinguishes mu'āriḍ from nāqiḍ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the roles of mu'āriḍ and nāqiḍ in a jadal exchange and determine which response strategy (new proof, mu'āraḍa, taḥrīr) is appropriate for a given refutation.

Core Idea
In jadal, the nāqiḍ attacks the proof; the mu'āriḍ attacks the claim—both must bring proof to be heard.

This passage from the Waldiyya delves into the art of responding to a refutation in formal debate. You will learn the difference between one who challenges the proof (nāqiḍ) and one who challenges the claim itself (mu'āriḍ), and the strategic options available to a proponent when his proof is refuted. These tools are not merely theoretical; they govern real debates in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of attackers
Find where the passage defines mu'āriḍ (one who claims the invalidity of the proven claim) and nāqiḍ (one who claims the invalidity of the proof). Note the crucial difference in their targets.
2
List the response strategies
The passage mentions several ways to answer a refutation: providing a new proof, countering with another witness (mu'āraḍa), or refining (taḥrīr) the proof, claim, or material. Categorise them.
3
Understand the burden of proof
The text states that both mu'āriḍ and nāqiḍ must provide proof for their claims of invalidity, unless the invalidity is self-evident. Reflect on why this rule prevents frivolous objections.
4
Apply to a real scenario
Imagine a debate where someone says: 'Your proof that the world is created is invalid because it assumes temporal origination.’ Is this a naqid or mu'arid? What response strategy could the proponent use?
Use the example from where_this_tool_lives.

وقد يجاب عن النقض مطلقا ( سواء كان بالتخلف أو بخصوص الفساد (بائيات المدعى المنقوض دلیله بدلیل آخر أى بدليل يغاير الدليل الأول ، سواء كان مغايرا له بالكلية أو في الجملة فيشمل الانتقال إلى دليل آخر ) وهذا الاثبات مطلقا أو هذا الجواب إفحام من وجه لعدم تصحيحه الدليل المنقوض وإظهار صواب من وجه آخر لافادته ماهو المقصود . اعلم أنه قد يجاب عن شاهديم النقض بالتخلف بالنقض بشاهد التخلف وبشاهد الاستلزام وعن شاهد النقض بخصوص الفساد بالنقض بشاهد التخلف وبشاهد الاستلزام ، وقد يجاب بالمعارضة عن النقض بالشاهدين، ومعنى المعارضة على النقض أن السائل بطل الدليل بالشاهدين والمعلل أثبته . وقد يجاب عنه بتحرير الدليل وبتحرير المدعى وبتحرير المادة ، لكن فى مقابلة النقض بالنقض كلام كما في مقابلة المعارضة بالمعارضة (واعلم أن المعارض) أى من ادعى بطلان المدعى المدلل ( والناقض) أى من ادعى بطلان الدليل ففيهما تجريد إذا لم يذكرا دليلا فلا يسمع دعواهما (البطلان إذا لم يكن دعوى البطلان من أجلى البديهيات فهو في حكم الاستثناء ، وقيل الدليل أعم من الحقيق والحكمي ، فبداهة العقل داخلة في الدليل ، وإنما لا يكون مسموعا لأنه مكابرة غير مسموعة فلا بد فيهما من الدليل ) ويسمى دليل النقض الاجمالى سواء كان دليل التخلف أو دليل خصوص الفساد ( شاهدا ) لشهادته على بطلان الدليل دون دليل المعارضة ولا مشاحة في الاصطلاح ، وقد يطلق الشاهد على سند المنع لقوته ( إن قلت أليس ) يصح ) للسائل منع مجموع الدليل بمعنى طلب الدليل عليه ) غير محال كما بين في محله
And it is possible to answer the refutation absolutely (whether it is by failure or specifically by corruption) by proving the claimed matter whose proof was refuted, with another proof—i.e., a proof different from the first, whether totally different or partially, so it includes moving to another proof. This absolute proof or this answer is a silencing (ifḥām) from one aspect because it does not correct the refuted proof, and a display of correctness from another aspect because it achieves what is intended. Know that it is possible to answer the two witnesses of refutation by failure with a refutation by the witness of failure and by the witness of entailment, and the witness of refutation specifically by corruption with a refutation by the witness of failure and by the witness of entailment. And it is possible to answer by counter-objection (mu'ārada) to the refutation with the two witnesses. The meaning of counter-objection to the refutation is that the asker invalidated the proof by the two witnesses, and the proponent established it. And it is possible to answer it by refining the proof, by refining the claim, and by refining the material. But in countering refutation with refutation there is discussion, as in countering counter-objection with counter-objection. (Know that the objector (mu'āriḍ))—i.e., the one who claims the invalidity of the proven claim—and the critic (nāqiḍ)—i.e., the one who claims the invalidity of the proof—both, if they do not mention a proof, then their claim of invalidity is not heard, unless the claim of invalidity is among the clearest self-evident truths, in which case it is an exception. It is said that the proof is more general than real and notional (ḥukmī); thus axiomatic rationality is included in proof. It is only not heard because it is obstinacy (mukābara) not to be heard. Therefore both must have proof. And the proof of the abstract refutation (al-naqd al-ijmālī)—whether it is the proof of failure or the proof specifically of corruption—is called a 'witness' (shāhid) because it testifies to the invalidation of the proof, unlike the proof of counter-objection. And there is no quibbling in terminology. And the witness may be applied to the basis (sanad) of prevention because of its strength. (If you say: 'Is it not') valid for the asker to prevent the whole proof, meaning to request proof for it? (It is not impossible) as explained in its place.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion of the general strategies for answering a refutation (naqd) in disputation. The author explains that the proponent whose proof has been refuted can respond by presenting a new proof that is free from the flaw identified by the critic. This new proof may be entirely different or partially different, effectively a transition to another argument. This approach, called 'ithbāt al-mudda'a bi-dalīl ākhar', is described as an 'ifḥām' (silencing) from one angle because it does not repair the original proof, but it achieves the intended purpose from another angle—namely, establishing the truth of the claim. The author then notes that there are multiple ways to answer each type of witness: the witness of failure can be countered by another witness of failure or by the witness of entailment; similarly, the witness of specific corruption can be countered by either. Alternatively, the proponent can use counter-objection (mu'āraḍa) to oppose the critic's witnesses. The author also mentions refining (taḥrīr) the proof, the claim, or the material as possible responses. The passage then clarifies the distinction between a mu'āriḍ (one who claims the invalidity of the proven claim itself) and a nāqiḍ (one who claims the invalidity of the proof). Both must provide evidence for their claim; otherwise, their assertion is not admitted (unless the claim is self-evident). The term 'shāhid' is specifically used for the proof in an abstract refutation (naqd ijmālī) because it serves as a witness to the proof's invalidity, in contrast to the proof in mu'āraḍa. The passage ends with a note that preventing the whole proof (requesting proof for it) is not impossible. This lesson encapsulates higher-level strategies and terminological clarity in jadal.

Key Terms
معارض
ناقض
شاهد
معارضة

Key Takeaway
In jadal, both the critic of the proof (nāqiḍ) and the objector to the claim (mu'āriḍ) must present a proof; otherwise their objection is dismissed, preserving the integrity of the debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Understanding al-Mu'arid and al-Naqid in Dialectical Debate
Author defines terms and establishes proof requirement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-mu'arid and al-naqid and explain why proof is required for objections in jadal.

Core Idea
In jadal, an objection without proof is mere stubbornness.

In dialectical debate, not every objection is automatically valid. The author of this text distinguishes two types of objectors: al-mu'arid, who attacks the proven claim itself, and al-naqid, who attacks the proof. Both must bring evidence for their claim of invalidity; otherwise, their objection is dismissed as stubbornness (mukabara). This lesson will equip you to identify these two roles and understand the crucial requirement of proof in argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two roles
Read the first part of the passage to identify the definitions of al-mu'arid and al-naqid. Al-mu'arid claims the proven claim is false; al-naqid claims the proof is invalid.
These are technical terms in jadal (dialectical debate).
2
Understand the requirement of proof
Notice that both are 'stripped' (tajrid) if they do not bring a proof. The author says their claim of invalidity is not heard unless it is self-evident or supported by evidence.
The text says 'فلا يسمع دعواهما' (their claim is not heard).
3
Recognize the exception for self-evident truths
If the claim of invalidity is among the clearest self-evident truths (ajla al-badihiyyat), it is accepted without proof. Otherwise, proof is required.
This is similar to how certain premises in logic are admitted without demonstration.
4
Distinguish the two types of evidence for the naqid
The evidence brought by the naqid is called a 'witness' (shahid). It can be evidence of non-necessity (takhalluf) or evidence of corruption (fasad). Understand that these target the proof's validity.
Non-necessity shows the proof does not necessitate the conclusion; corruption shows a flaw.

واعلم أن المعارض) أى من ادعى بطلان المدعى المدلل ( والناقض) أى من ادعى بطلان الدليل ففيهما تجريد إذا لم يذكرا دليلا فلا يسمع دعواهما (البطلان إذا لم يكن دعوى البطلان من أجلى البديهيات فهو في حكم الاستثناء ، وقيل الدليل أعم من الحقيق والحكمي ، فبداهة العقل داخلة في الدليل ، وإنما لا يكون مسموعا لأنه مكابرة غير مسموعة فلا بد فيهما من الدليل ) ويسمى دليل النقض الاجمالى سواء كان دليل التخلف أو دليل خصوص الفساد ( شاهدا ) لشهادته على بطلان الدليل دون دليل المعارضة ولا مشاحة في الاصطلاح ، وقد يطلق الشاهد على سند المنع لقوته
And know that the objector (al-mu'arid) who claims invalidity of the proven claim, and the critic (al-naqid) who claims invalidity of the proof, both are stripped (of authority) if they do not mention a proof for their claim of invalidity. The claim of invalidity, if it is not among the clearest self-evident truths, is in the ruling of an exception. It is said that the proof is more general than the real and the legal, so the self-evident of the mind is included in the proof. It is not heard because it is stubbornness that is not heard, so they must have proof. The evidence of the comprehensive objection (al-naqd al-ijmali) – whether it is the evidence of non-necessity (al-takhalluf) or the evidence of particular corruption (al-fasad) – is called a 'witness' (shahid) because it testifies to the invalidity of the proof, unlike the evidence of confrontation (al-mu'arada), and there is no quibbling over terminology. Sometimes the witness is used for the source of denial (al-man') due to its strength.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and procedural discussion in the dialectical context. It defines two key figures in argumentation: المَعَارِض (al-mu'arid), who attacks the proven claim itself, and النَّاقِض (al-naqid), who attacks the proof of the claim. The text establishes that these two are stripped of their stance (تجريد) if they do not bring evidence for their claim of invalidity. This is because a claim of invalidity is not automatically accepted; if it is not self-evidently false, it requires proof. The author notes that the term 'proof' here is broad, including both real (حقيق) and legal (حكمي) proof, and even the self-evident of the mind falls under it. Without proof, the claim is dismissed as mere stubbornness (مكابرة). The evidence brought by the naqid is called a 'witness' (شاهد) because it testifies against the proof. This witness can be either evidence of non-necessity (تخلف) – showing that the proof does not necessitate the conclusion – or evidence of particular corruption (فساد) – showing a flaw in the proof. The term 'witness' is also applied to the source of denial (المنع) when it is strong. The passage ensures that objections are not accepted without supporting evidence, maintaining the rigor of dialectical debate.

الناقض
شاهد
مكابرة

In jadal, any objection (mu'arid or naqid) without evidence is rejected as stubbornness, preserving the discipline's evidentiary rigor.

Why Demanding Proof for the Whole Argument is Impossible
Author argues that demanding proof for the entire argument at once is an impossible burden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why demanding proof for an entire composite argument is impossible in Islamic dialectical debate and distinguish the three possible intentions of the opponent.

Core Idea
Demanding proof for the whole argument at once is an unbearable burden because each proof yields only a single premise.

In Islamic dialectical logic (adab al-bahth), there are strict rules for proper objection. One key rule is that the opponent cannot simply say "prove your whole argument" — that would be an impossible demand, known as taklīf mā lā yutāq. This passage explains why and shows how the proponent should respond by clarifying the opponent's intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's move
The opponent (al-sā'il) may try to deny the entire composite argument (man' majmū' al-dalīl) by demanding proof for the whole proof at once. This is the maneuver under discussion.
The term 'majmū' al-dalīl' means the entire argument considered as a single composite unit.
2
Understand the proponent's response
The proponent (al-mujīb) responds that this is not permissible because it is taklīf mā lā yutāq — imposing an unbearable burden. The reason is that the proof only yields one premise at a time.
This principle is rooted in usul al-fiqh and applied to dialectical procedure.
3
Analyze the conditional reasoning
The passage presents a conditional argument: 'If the proof yields only one premise, then demanding proof for the whole composite argument is an unbearable burden. But the proof yields only one premise. Therefore, the demand is impermissible.' Identify the antecedent and consequent.
This is a modus ponens form: if P then Q; P; therefore Q.
4
Distinguish the three possible intentions of the opponent
The proponent must ask: does the opponent mean to deny one premise, all premises individually, or the whole as a conceptual unit? Each case requires a different response: in the first two, the proponent proves each premise; in the third, he must prove each premise and then prove that the whole is established by the sum.
This clarification prevents confusion and ensures a fair debate.
5
Apply the rule to a sample dialectical exchange
Imagine a debate in usul al-fiqh: the proponent argues that qiyas is a valid source of law. The opponent cannot say 'prove that qiyas is valid' without specifying which premise is contested. Instead, he must object to a specific premise, e.g., that the 'illah is effective.
The rule keeps debate focused and productive.
