If you say: 'Is it not permissible for the opponent (al-sā'il) to deny the entire composite argument (man' majmū' al-dalīl) in the sense of demanding proof for it, which is not impossible as established in its place?' He said in the gloss: 'And here is another exposition, which is to say that it leads to circularity or infinite regress, and everything that leads to them is impossible. Then the respondent (al-mujīb) makes a disjunction in the minor premise and says: if you mean that it leads to impossible circularity or impossible infinite regress, then we do not accept the minor; if you mean absolute, then we do not accept the major.' And it is not issued from a rational person to intend the impossible. And it may be answered to the objection absolutely – whether by non-necessity or by implication of corruption – by establishing the refuted claim with another proof free from the corruption that the objector showed. This establishment, whether it is changing the proof or transitioning to another proof, is a silencing from one aspect for not correcting the rejected proof, and a showing of correctness from another aspect for achieving what is intended. But you know that transitioning to another proof is a form of termination of research unless the first proof is flawed by an intermediary or the other proof is constitutive of the source of denial or the transition is not intended as a proof that he does not establish upon the listeners, and only the last is conceivable here. And know that the objector (al-mu'arid) means the one who claims invalidity of the proven claim, and the critic (al-nāqid) means the one who claims invalidity of the proof – in both there is stripping if they do not mention a proof, real or legal, for what they claim of invalidity of the claim and the proof. Their claim of invalidity is not heard because it is stubbornness, so proof is necessary. And the evidence of the objection is called a witness for its testifying to the invalidity of the proof, unlike the confrontation, and there is no quibbling over terminology. If you say: 'Is it not permissible for the opponent to deny the entire composite argument, i.e., to request proof for it, whether one or generic, without any interpretation at all, so that the claim of invalidity of the proof applies to the entire composite argument?' I say: 'It is not permissible to deny it, because denying the entire composite argument is imposing an unbearable burden (taklīf mā lā yutāq), i.e., imposing on the opponent something that the proponent cannot bear, and every unbearable imposition is impermissible. Because the proof – that is, the proof of the minor premise – yields only one premise. This can be formulated as a direct conditional: if the required proof yields only one premise, then demanding it for the entire composite argument is an unbearable burden, but the antecedent is true. And as an indirect conditional: denying the entire composite argument is an unbearable burden; otherwise the proof would not yield one premise, but the proof does not yield more than one premise.' And here there is a discussion: which is to ask the opponent at that point: do you mean denial of one of its premises, or denial of each one of them, or denial of the entire composite argument as a whole? In the first case, the proponent provides proof for one of its premises; if the opponent is silent, then that is fine. If he says 'I mean the other premise', he also provides proof for it. In the second case, he provides proof for each one of them. In the third case, he provides proof for each one of them and then proves by the establishment of each one the establishment of the whole. This is the summary of what Abu al-Fatḥ said. The exposition of the third: this proof has established its premises, and every proof of this nature is established. The meaning of our saying 'as a whole' is considering the proof as a single thing by a conceptual unity. And know that the comprehensive objection (al-naqd al-ijmālī) divides into two types: one is the famous objection (al-naqd al-mashhūr), and the second is the broken objection (al-naqd al-maksūr). For the objection by the witness of non-necessity is not free from leaving some attributes of the proof after changing it inevitably in the first case, and the second is the famous objection. The first is not free from being without proof for the denial, as in the unproven claim, so it is heard. I say: 'It is not permissible because that denial is an unbearable burden because the proof yields only one premise, and what yields only one, demanding it for the whole composite argument is an unbearable burden.' And here there is a discussion: he said in the gloss: which is to ask the opponent at that point: do you mean denial of one of its premises, or denial of each one, or denial of the whole as a whole? In the first case, the proponent provides proof for one of its premises; if the opponent is silent, then that; if he says 'I mean the other premise', he also provides proof for it. In the second case, he provides proof for each one. In the third case, he provides proof for each one and then proves by the establishment of each one the establishment of the whole. This is the summary of what Abu al-Fatḥ said. The exposition of the third: this proof has established its premises, and every proof of this nature is established. The meaning of our saying 'as a whole' is considering the proof as a single thing by a conceptual unity. So based on the first – i.e., based on the interpretation that the opponent's intention is denial of one of its premises – if he specifies it in the statement when asked, then he provides proof for it; otherwise he provides proof for one of them. If he is silent, then that is the denied one. If he says 'I mean the other premise', if he specifies it, he provides proof for it; otherwise, it follows the mentioned analogy. Indeed, denial of an unspecified premise is not heard, and their custom does not run to denying the proof, but it is not an unbearable burden. This discussion is denial of the minor's second premise on one interpretation, and denial of the major on another, and denial of the approximation on yet another. You saw its chains, so remember, but the inquiry is not necessary; for the proponent can provide evidence for a premise without inquiry. If he is silent, then that; otherwise for another, and so on he provides evidence for all premises. If he is silent, then that; and if he says 'I mean denial of the whole as a whole', he provides evidence for it with the mentioned proof.

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a dialectical discussion about the permissibility of an opponent demanding proof for the entire argument (مجموع الدليل) rather than for a single premise. The text opens with a hypothetical objection: 'Is it not permissible for the opponent to deny the entire composite argument?' The author then cites a gloss that presents a new exposition: the argument leads to circularity or infinite regress, both of which are impossible. The respondent can then ask whether the objector means impossible circularity or absolute, and adjust his response accordingly. The passage then discusses responding to objections generally by providing another proof free from the corruption pointed out. The author notes that transitioning to another proof is a form of termination of research unless certain conditions are met. The definitions of al-mu'arid and al-naqid are repeated, emphasizing the need for proof. The central issue is whether the opponent can deny the entire composite argument at once. The answer is 'no' because that would be imposing an unbearable burden (تكليف بما لا يطاق). The reasoning is that the proof (dalīl) yields only one premise at a time; requiring proof for the whole collection is impossible. The passage then presents a discussion (بحث) where the proponent must clarify the opponent's intention: Is he denying one premise, all premises, or the aggregate as a conceptual unit? For each case, the proponent responds appropriately. The discussion cites Abu al-Fatḥ's summary. Additionally, the text classifies the comprehensive objection (النقض الإجمالي) into two types: the famous objection (النقض المشهور) and the broken objection (النقض المكسور). The first leaves out some attributes of the proof after necessary modification, while the second does not. The passage concludes by noting that inquiry into the opponent's intention is not necessary; the proponent can simply provide evidence for premises sequentially. All examples are drawn from the dialectical logic tradition, using technical terms like صغرى (minor premise) and كبرى (major premise).

Handling Objections to the Whole Argument in Jadal
Author explains why objecting to the whole proof is impossible and clarifies three possible interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of objection to an entire proof and determine the appropriate response for each.

Core Idea
Objecting to the whole proof is impossible unless the objector specifies which premise is targeted.

In formal Islamic debate (jadal), the opponent may try to reject your entire argument at once. This seems like a powerful move, but the classical authorities argue it is actually an impossible burden. In this lesson, we will explore why that is and learn how to ask the objector to clarify their intent. By understanding the three possible meanings of objecting to the whole, you will be equipped to respond correctly and advance the debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule being discussed
The passage begins by stating that objecting to the sum of the proof (man' majmu' al-dalil) is not permissible because it is an impossible burden. Locate this claim in the Arabic.
The phrase 'تكليف بما لا يطاق' is key.
2
Understand the justification using conditional syllogisms
The author uses two forms of conditional syllogism (istithna'i) to justify: the proof produces only one premise, so demanding the whole proof is an impossible burden. Recognize that this is a logical defense of the rule.
The regular conditional (mustaqim) and irregular (ghayr mustaqim) are mentioned.
3
Extract the three possible interpretations
The opponent can mean: (1) objecting to a single premise, (2) objecting to each premise individually, or (3) objecting to the whole as a unity. Write them down with their Arabic labels if available.
The passage lists them as 'منع مقدمة عن مقدماته', 'منع كل واحد منها', and 'منع مجموع الدليل من حيث المجموع'.
4
Determine the prover's response for each case
For (1), prove that premise; for (2), prove each premise; for (3), prove each premise then argue that the whole follows from the parts. This shows the prover always has a way forward.
The third case involves proving the whole by establishing its parts.
5
Practice identifying the type from a hypothetical objection
Given a statement like 'Your entire proof is invalid!', decide which category it falls into (likely the third) and what the response should be.
In real debates, the objector usually means one of these three.

أى على مجموع الدليل قلت (لا يجوز منعه (لأنه) أى منع مجموع الدليل ( تكليف بما لا يطاق ) أي تكليف السائل المعلل بشيء لا يتحمل المعلل له ، وكل تكليف بما لا يطاق غير جائز (لأن الدليل ) وهو دليل الصغرى ( لا ينتج إلا مقدمة واحدة ) يمكن تصويره من الاستثنائى المستقيم بأن يقال إذا كان الدليل المطلوب لا ينتج إلا مقدمة واحدة كان طلبه على مجموع الدليل تكليفا بما لا يطاق لكن المقدم حق : ومن الاستثنائى الغير للمستقيم بأن يقال منع مجموع الدليل تكليف بما لا يطاق، وإلا لما كان الدليل منتجا مقدمة واحدة ، لكن الدليل لا ينتج إلا مقدمة واحدة ( وهنا بحث ) وهو أن يستفسر عن السائل حينئذ أن مرادك هل هو منع مقدمة عن مقدماته أو منع كل واحد منها ، أو منع مجموع الدليل من حيث المجموع ، فعلى الأول يستدل المعلل على واحد من مقدماته : فان سكت السائل فذلك ، وإن قال مرادى المقدمة الأخرى يستدل علها أيضا ، وعلى الثانى يستدل على كل واحد منها ، وعلى الثالث يستدل على كل واحد منها تم يستدل بثبوت كل واحد على ثبوت المجموع وهذا خلاصة ما قاله أبو الفتح . وتقرير الثالث أن هذا الدليل ثبت مقدماته ، وكل دليل شأنه هذا فثابت ، ومعنى قولنا من حيث المجموع اعتبار الدليلية شيئا واحدا وحدة اعتبارية .
That is, regarding the sum of the proof, he said: 'It is not permissible to object to it, because objecting to the sum of the proof is an impossible burden,' i.e., imposing on the questioner (the objector) something the objector cannot bear. And every impossible burden is not permissible, because the proof—that is, the proof of the minor premise—produces only one premise. This can be illustrated by the regular conditional syllogism: if the required proof produces only one premise, then demanding the sum of the proof is an impossible burden, but the antecedent is true. And by the irregular conditional syllogism: objecting to the sum of the proof is an impossible burden, otherwise the proof would not produce a single premise, but the proof produces only one premise. (Here there is a discussion): that the objector should then be asked: do you mean objecting to one of its premises, or objecting to each one of them, or objecting to the sum of the proof as a whole? On the first, the objectee (the one presenting the proof) proves one of his premises: if the objector remains silent, that is it; if he says 'I mean the other premise', he proves that too. On the second, he proves each one of them. On the third, he proves each one of them, then proves the validity of the sum by the validity of each. This is the summary of what Abu al-Fath said. The exposition of the third: this proof has its premises established, and every proof of this nature is established. The meaning of our statement 'as a whole' is considering the proof as one thing with a notional unity.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical explanation of a rule in jadal (debate). It addresses the objection (man') to an entire argument (majmu' al-dalil). The commentator begins by reiterating that objecting to the sum of the proof is an impossible burden (takleef ma la yutaq) because the proof (specifically the proof of the minor premise) produces only one premise. He illustrates this using conditional syllogisms: if a proof produces only one premise, demanding the whole proof is impossible. Then he introduces a discussion (bahth) to clarify the objector's intent. He presents three possibilities: 1) objecting to a single premise, 2) objecting to each premise individually, 3) objecting to the sum of the proof as a whole (min hayth al-majmu'). For each case, the response of the prover (mu'allil) is explained: in the first, he proves that one premise; in the second, he proves each premise; in the third, he proves each premise and then uses them to establish the whole. The commentator notes that this is based on Abu al-Fath's work. The phrase 'as a whole' means treating the proof as one notional entity. This passage teaches the student how to handle objections that target the entire argument, a common dialectical move.

منع مجموع الدليل من حيث المجموع

B) Ask the opponent to clarify whether he means a single premise, each premise, or the whole as a sum.

Key Takeaway

Summarized Objection: Famous vs. Broken
Author classifies summarized objection into two types: famous and broken.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the famous (mashhur) and broken (maksur) summarized objections based on whether evidence is provided and whether attributes are left out necessarily.

Core Idea
The broken objection necessarily omits attributes; the famous objection lacks evidence.

In dialectical debate (jadal), not all objections are alike. When an objector targets the entire proof in a summarized fashion, we must check if the objection is accompanied by evidence or if it simply asserts a flaw. This lesson unpacks the two categories of summarized objection—famous and broken—and clarifies their logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
The author states that the summarized objection divides into two: famous (mashhur) and broken (maksur). Locate these terms in the Arabic text.
They are introduced right after the division statement.
2
Understand the criterion of necessity
The classification depends on whether, after modifying the proof, some attributes are left out necessarily or not. The broken objection leaves out attributes necessarily; the famous objection may leave out attributes unnecessarily.
'Necessarily' means the modification forces omission.
3
Check for evidence
The famous objection is described as being without evidence for the objection itself (like an undemonstrated claim). The broken objection may have evidence. Confirm this by reading: 'the former [broken] is not free from being without evidence' and 'without evidence ... the latter [famous]'.
The text uses 'شاهد التخلف' (counter-evidence of failure) as the context.
4
Apply to a sample objection
Imagine an objector says: 'Your proof for the prohibition of riba is invalid because it fails to include the condition of excess.' If he gives no reason for why it fails, that is a famous objection. If he explains why the condition is missing necessarily, it is broken.
This uses a real fiqh example.

واعلم أن النقض الإجمالي ينقسم إلى قسمين: أحدهما النقض المشهور. والثاني النقض المكسور ، لأن النقض بشاهد التخلف لا تخلو من أن يترك فيه بعض أوصاف دليل بعد تغييره بما لا بد منه أولا : والثانى هو النقض المشهور . والأول لا يخلو من أن يكون بلا دليل على المنع كما في المدعى الغير المدلل فتسمع ) قلت لا يجوز لأنه ( أى ذلك المنع ( تكليف بما لا يطاق لأن الدليل لا ينتج إلا مقدمة واحدة ) وما لا ينتج إلا إياها فطلبه على مجموع الدليل تكليف بما لا يطاق (وههنا بحث) قال في الحاشية وهو أن يستفسر عن السائل حينئذ أن مرادك هل هو منع مقدمة من مقدماته أو منع كل منها أو منع مجموع الدليل من حيث المجموع ، فعلى الأول يستدل المعلل على واحد من مقدماته ، فان سكت السائل فذلك ، وإن قال مرادى المقدة الأخرى يستدل علها أيضا ، وعلى الثانى يستدل على كل واحد منها ، وعلى الثالث يستدل على ، كل واحد منها ثم يستدل بثبوت كل واحد منها على ثبوت المجموع من حيثم المجموع ، وهذا خلاصة ما قاله أبو الفتح ، وتقرير الثالث أن هذا دليل ثبت مقدماته ، وكل دليل هذا شأنه فثابت ومعنى قولنا من حيث المجموع اعتبار الدليل شيئا واحدا وحدة اعتبارية انتهى. فعلى الأول : أى على تقرير كون مراد السائل منع مقدمة من مقدماته ، فان عينها في الاخبار عند الاستفسار فيستدل عليها وإلا فيستدل على واحدة منها ، فان سكت فذلك الممنوع ، وإن قال مرادى المقدمة الأخرى فان عينها فيستدل عليها وإلا فعلى القياس المذكور ، نعم منع المقدمة الغير المعينة ليس مسموع ولم تجرعادتهم ممنع الدليل الكنه ليس تكليف مالا يطاق، وهذا البحث منع الصغرى الثانية على تقدير ، ومنع الكبراها على تقدير ، ومنع للتقريب على تقدير آخر ، ورأيت أسانيدها فتذكر لكن الاستفسار ليس بلازم ؛ إذ يمكن للمعلل أن يستدل على مقدمة بلا استفسار ، فان سكت فذلك وإلا فعلى أخرى وهكذا يستدل على جميع المقدمات ، فان سكت فذلك وإن قال مرادى منع المجموع من حيث المجموع يستدل عليه بالدليل المذكور
And know that the summarized objection (al-naqd al-ijmali) is divided into two parts: one is the famous objection (al-naqd al-mashhur), and the second is the broken objection (al-naqd al-maksur). This is because the objection by counter-evidence of failure (shahid al-takhaluf) is not free from leaving out some attributes of the proof after modifying it necessarily or not: the latter is the famous objection. The former is not free from being without evidence for the objection, as in the case of an undemonstrated claim, so it is heard. I say: it is not permissible because that objection is an impossible burden, because the proof produces only one premise, and what produces only one, demanding the sum of the proof is an impossible burden. (Here there is a discussion) mentioned in the gloss: that the objector should then be asked: do you mean objecting to a premise from its premises, or objecting to each of them, or objecting to the sum of the proof as a whole? ... On the first interpretation, meaning that the objector intends to object to a premise from its premises: if he specifies it when asked, then he proves it; otherwise, he proves one of them. If he remains silent, that is the objected premise. If he says 'I mean the other premise', then if he specifies it, he proves it; otherwise, it follows the mentioned analogy. Indeed, objecting to an unspecified premise is not heard, and it is not their habit to object to the proof, but it is not an impossible burden. This discussion is an objection to the second minor premise in one interpretation, and to the major premise in another, and to the approximation in yet another. I have seen its chains, so recall, but the inquiry is not necessary; because the prover can prove a premise without inquiry. If he remains silent, that is it; otherwise, he proves another; and so on until he proves all premises. If he remains silent, that is it; if he says 'I mean to object to the sum as a whole', he proves it by the mentioned proof.

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the summarized objection (naqd ijmail) into two types: famous (mashhur) and broken (maksur). The author explains that the objection by counter-evidence of failure necessarily involves leaving out some attributes of the original proof after modifying it. The second type (where attributes are left out without necessity) is the famous objection. The first type (where attributes are left out with necessity) can be without evidence and is thus heard. The discussion then revisits the three types of objection from the previous lesson, adding details from a gloss. The author notes that objecting to an unspecified premise is not accepted, and clarifies that the inquiry (istifsar) is not obligatory because the prover can proceed directly. This passage reinforces the categories of objection and their practical application in debate.

النقض المشهور
النقض المكسور

The Broken Objection (Naqd Maksur) in Dialectic
Author defines the broken objection and illustrates it with a Shafi'i legal example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a broken objection in a legal argument and explain the dialectical moves of preventing application and refuting it.

Core Idea
A broken objection omits a key attribute of the proof, but the prover can block it if that attribute is essential.

In dialectic, not all objections are created equal. Some objections, called 'broken' (maksur), modify the prover's proof by leaving out certain attributes, potentially creating an invalid counterexample. Understanding how to handle such objections is crucial in legal debates, as illustrated by Imam al-Shafi'i's ruling on selling an absent item.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of a broken objection
Read the definition: it is an objection that omits some attributes of the prover's proof after necessary modification. The omitted attributes are particularities of the middle term (in categorical syllogisms) or of the predicate of the repeated part (in conditional syllogisms).
2
Identify the key attributes omitted
Notice that the omitted attribute may or may not be causally relevant. The author says the valid objection is included here, but it can be restricted to invalid ones. This means a broken objection can be valid if the omitted attribute is not essential.
3
Analyze the dialectical response: man' al-jaryan
When a broken objection is raised, the prover can prevent its application (man' al-jaryan) by arguing that the omitted attribute has a causal role. The objector can then refute this by proving the attribute has no causal effect.
4
Work through the Shafi'i example
Imam al-Shafi'i argues: selling an absent item is invalid because it is an item of unknown attribute (majhul al-sifah). A broken objection might apply this to a case where the attribute is known, omitting the unknownness. The prover would then prevent application by stating that unknownness is essential to the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فى بيان النقض المكسور ( اعلم أن الناقض بالتخلف ، قيل ولا يبعد أن يكون أعم منه ولا يخفى ما فيه (قد يترك) بعد تغييره دليل المعلل بمالابد منه وإلا لكان جميع النفوض مكسوراً، إذ التغيير فى الجملة حاصل فى كل نقض (بعض أوصاف دليل المعلل أى بعض خصوصيات الحد الأوسط فى الاقترانى وبعض خصوصيات محمول الجزء المتكرر في الاستثنائي إذا كان المقدم والتالى مشتركين في الموضوع . وأما الحد الأكبر فى الاقترانى ومحمول الجزء الغير المتكرر في صورة الاشتراك فلا بد أن يتفاوتا فى المدعى ومادة التخلف لا فى ذاتهما ولا فى صفتهما فتأمل سواء كان لذلك البعض المتروك مدخل فى العلية أولا وسواء كان تركه على زعم أنه ليس بمدار للاستدلال أولا فيدخل فيه النقض الصحيح ، ويمكن أن يخصص بالنقض الفاسد ) عند إجرائه ) أي عند إجراء الناقض دليل المعلل فيسمى ذلك النقض ( نقض مكسورا) لكون الكسر فيه زائد على ما يقتضيه آخرية المدعى وقيل لوقوع الكسر فيه بالنسبة إلى النقض المشهور ( فللملل حينئذ منع الجريان) أى منع جريان الدليل في آخر منعا مستندا بأن للوصف المتروك مدخلا في العلية والاستلزام ، وهذا الندمساو المنع فيفيد إبطاله ( وقد يبطل السائل) ذات هذا السند باثبات أن لا مدخل لذلك الوصف في العلية) بأن يقال الوصف ليس له تأثير فى العلية وكل شيء شأنه هذا ليس له مدخل في العملية ثم يثبت صغرى هذا الدليل (مثاله) أى مثال النقض المكسور مع مورده كأن (قال) الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله لا يصح بيع الغائب لأنه أى الغائب (مبيع مجهول السفة) عند العاقدين أو أحدهما ، وكل مبيع شأنه هذا لا يصح يعه
Chapter on explaining the broken objection. Know that the objector by counter-evidence of failure — it is said that it is not unlikely to be more general than that, and what is in it is not hidden — may leave out, after modifying the prover's proof necessarily (otherwise all objections would be broken, because modification occurs generally in every objection), some attributes of the prover's proof, i.e., some particularities of the middle term in the categorical syllogism and some particularities of the predicate of the repeated part in the conditional syllogism when the antecedent and consequent share the subject. As for the major term in the categorical and the predicate of the non-repeated part in the case of sharing, they must differ in the claim and the matter of failure, not in themselves or their attributes, so reflect — whether that left-aside attribute has a role in causality or not, and whether its omission is based on the claim that it is not a basis for inference or not. Thus the valid objection is included, and it can be restricted to the invalid objection. When it is applied, i.e., when the objector applies the prover's proof, that objection is called a 'broken objection', because the break is additional to what is required by the finality of the claim, or because the break occurs relative to the famous objection. Then the prover may prevent the application, i.e., prevent the application of the proof to another case, based on the claim that the omitted attribute has a role in causality and implication. And this prevention is equal to the objection, so it results in its nullification. And the objector may invalidate this basis by proving that the attribute has no role in causality, by saying: the attribute has no effect on causality, and every attribute of this sort has no role in the operation; then he establishes the minor premise of this proof. Its example: the example of the broken objection with its response: as if Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'The sale of an absent item is invalid because it is an item whose attribute is unknown to the contracting parties or one of them, and every item of this description is invalid to sell.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section on the broken objection (naqd maksur). The author explains that the objector may leave out some attributes of the prover's evidence after necessary modification. He specifies which attributes: in categorical syllogisms, these are particularities of the middle term; in conditional syllogisms, particularities of the predicate of the repeated part when antecedent and consequent share the subject. The major term and non-repeated predicate must differ in the claim and the material of failure. The omitted attribute may or may not have a role in causality; the valid objection is included but can be restricted to the invalid. When the objector applies the prover's proof, it is called broken because of an extra 'break' beyond what the finality of the claim requires. The prover can counter by preventing the application, arguing that the omitted attribute is essential to causality. The objector can then refute this by proving the attribute has no causal role. The example from Shafi'i illustrates: the sale of an absent item (gha'ib) is invalid because its attribute is unknown; this is the prover's proof. An objector might use a broken objection by applying the same reasoning to a case where the attribute is known, leaving out the condition of unknownness, but then the prover can argue that unknownness is essential. This passage teaches the dialectical tactics of handling broken objections.

Key Terms
النقض المكسور
منع الجريان
الحد الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The attribute being unknown (majhul al-sifah).
D) The contracting parties being one.

Key Takeaway
The broken objection is a dialectical move where the objector omits a potentially essential attribute of the prover's proof, and the prover's counter-move is to prevent application by arguing the omitted attribute is causally relevant.

Definition and Structure of the Broken Refutation
Definition of broken refutation and response options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key features of a broken refutation and distinguish it from other refutation types in jadal.

Core Idea
A broken refutation omits a critical attribute, allowing the proponent to defend by asserting its causal relevance.

In dialectical debate (jadal), a refutation by non-application (naqd bi al-takhalluf) can be flawed if the objector drops some attributes of the proof's middle term. This is called 'broken refutation' (al-naqd al-maksur) because the break goes beyond the mere difference in claims. The passage defines it and outlines the dynamics: the proponent can block application (man' al-jaryan) by citing the omitted attribute's causal role, while the objector may counter by proving its irrelevance.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
Read the first part of the passage: 'اعلم أن الناقض بالتخلف قد يترك بعض أوصاف دليل المعلل...'. Note that the objector intentionally omits some attributes of the proof when applying it to a counterexample.
Omission is the key; the refutation is called 'broken' due to this extra defect.
2
Understand the name 'broken'
The text says 'لكون الكسر فيه زائداً على ما يقتضيه آخرية المدعى'. The break (kusr) is additional beyond the fact that the proof is applied to a different claim.
3
Identify the proponent's defense
The proponent (al-mu'allil) can say 'man' al-jaryan' (prevent application), arguing that the omitted attribute has an influence (madkhal) in the effective cause ('illa) and implication (istilzam).
This defense is a standard move in jadal.
4
Recognize the objector's counter
The objector (al-sa'il) can nullify this defense by proving that the omitted attribute has no causal role.
The exchange continues until one side's proof is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فى بيان النقض المكسور
اعلم أن الناقض بالتخلف قد يترك بعض أوصاف دليل المعلل عند إجرائه في مدعى آخر فيسمى ذلك النقض نقضاً مكسوراً لكون الكسر فيه زائداً على ما يقتضيه آخرية المدعى. فللمعلل حينئذ منع الجريان مستنداً بأن للوصف المتروك مدخلاً في العلية والاستلزام. وقد يبطل السائل هذا السند بإثبات أن لا مدخل لذلك الوصف في العلية.
مثاله: كأن قال الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله: لا يصح بيع الغائب لأنه مبيع مجهول الصفة عند العاقدين أو أحدهما، وكل مبيع شأنه هذا لا يصح بيعه. فنقضناه بأنه جار في تزوج امرأة غائبة لأنها مجهولة الصفة عند العاقدين أو أحدهما مع أنه صحيح عندك. فقد حذفنا من الأوسط قيد المبيعة. فللشافعي منع الجريان مستنداً بأن قيد المبيعة له مدخل في العلية. ولنا إبطال سنده.
Section on explaining the broken refutation: Know that the refuter by way of [claiming] non-application may omit some attributes of the proof of the party giving the proof when applying it to another claim, and that refutation is called 'broken refutation' because the breaking in it is additional to what is required by the otherness of the claim. The one giving the proof then has the [option] of preventing the application, based on the omitted attribute having an influence in the effective cause and implication. And the questioner may nullify this basis by proving that there is no influence for that attribute in the effective cause. Its example: As if Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'The sale of an absent thing is not valid because it is a sold item whose description is unknown to the two contracting parties or one of them, and every sold item of this nature is not valid to be sold.' We refuted it by saying that it applies to the marriage of an absent woman, because she is of unknown description to the two contracting parties or one of them, while it is valid according to you. So we omitted from the middle term the qualification of being sold. Al-Shafi'i then may prevent the application, based on the qualification of being sold having an influence in the effective cause. And we [may] nullify his basis.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of 'broken refutation' (al-naqd al-maksur) in dialectical debate. It begins by defining it: when a refuter, while applying the proof to a counterexample, omits some attributes of the proof's middle term, the refutation is called 'broken'. The term 'broken' indicates an extra defect beyond the mere fact that the proof is applied to a different claim. The author then describes the correct response: the original arguer (al-mu'allil) can block the application by arguing that the omitted attribute is essential to the effective cause ('illa). Alternatively, the questioner can undermine this defense by proving the omitted attribute has no causal effect. To illustrate, the author uses a classic usul al-fiqh example from Imam al-Shafi'i concerning the sale of an absent item (bay' al-gha'ib). Al-Shafi'i argues that such a sale is invalid because the item's description is unknown. The refuter objects that the same reasoning would apply to marriage of an absent woman (which is valid), but in doing so they drop the qualification 'sold item' from the middle term. This omission makes the refutation 'broken'. Al-Shafi'i can then defend by asserting the relevance of that qualification, or the questioner can try to prove its irrelevance. The passage thus teaches both the nature of this refutation and the dynamics of defending a proof in jadal.

Key Terms
النقض المكسور
العِلّة
الحد الأوسط
منع الجريان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What makes a refutation 'broken' (al-naqd al-maksur) according to the passage?
A) The objector applies the proof to a completely unrelated claim.
B) The objector omits some attributes of the proof's middle term when applying it to a counterexample.
C) The proponent fails to respond to the refutation.
D) The objector introduces a new attribute not present in the original proof.

Key Takeaway
The broken refutation is defined by the omission of a critical attribute, which empowers the proponent to block the application by asserting the causal necessity of that attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write your own example of a broken refutation in a hypothetical fiqh debate: start with a legal proof, then construct an objector's refutation that omits a key condition from the middle term. Describe how the proponent could respond.
?
In the passage, the objector can nullify the proponent's basis by proving the omitted attribute has no causal role. Explain what kind of evidence would be required to prove that an attribute is irrelevant to the effective cause ('illa).

The Shafi'i Example: Applying the Broken Refutation
Concrete example of broken refutation with al-Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the broken refutation in al-Shafi'i's example and analyze the responses of the proponent and objector.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i's proof on selling absent goods is broken when applied to marriage by omitting the condition 'sold item', triggering a dialectical exchange.

Using the definition from the previous lesson, the passage now illustrates the broken refutation with a classic example from Imam al-Shafi'i. He argues that selling an absent item is invalid because its description is unknown. The objector counters that the same reasoning would make marriage of an absent woman invalid—yet marriage is valid. But the objector dropped the qualification 'sold item' from the middle term. This allows al-Shafi'i to defend by asserting that condition's causal relevance. The lesson walks through this exchange, showing how the broken refutation works in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Shafi'i's original argument
Read the example: 'لا يصح بيع الغائب لأنه مبيع مجهول الصفة عند العاقدين أو أحدهما، وكل مبيع شأنه هذا لا يصح بيعه.' This is a complete proof: the sale is invalid because the item is unknown in description.
The middle term is 'mabi'a majhul al-sifa' (sold item of unknown description).
2
Recognize the objector's counterexample
The objector says: 'فنقضناه بأنه جار في تزوج امرأة غائبة'. The same reasoning would apply to marriage of an absent woman, which is valid, so the proof fails.
The objector is applying the proof to marriage, not sale.
3
Detect the omission
The objector omitted the qualification 'المبيعة' (being a sold item) from the middle term, keeping only 'مجهول الصفة' (unknown description). The text says: 'فقد حذفنا من الأوسط قيد المبيعة'.
This is the 'broken' part: the middle term is not identical to the original.
4
Understand al-Shafi'i's defense
Al-Shafi'i can respond with 'منع الجريان' (preventing application) by arguing that the omitted 'قيد المبيعة' (condition of being sold) has an influence in the effective cause (علة) of invalidity.
The proponent asserts that the condition is essential.
5
Consider the objector's rebuttal
The objector can try to nullify this defense by proving that the omitted condition does not affect the cause. The passage says: 'ولنا إبطال سنده.'
This is the next step in the dialectical process.

Source Text

We refuted it by saying that it applies to the marriage of an absent woman, because she is of unknown description to the two contracting parties or one of them, while it is valid according to you. And we [may] nullify his basis.

Invalid Refutations Due to Verbosity, Correction, and Obscurity
Author lists three grounds on which a refutation is not accepted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an objection based on verbosity, correction, or obscurity is a valid refutation in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
A flawed expression does not make an argument invalid; only defects in correctness matter.

In dialectical debate (munazara), not every objection to an opponent's argument is accepted. This passage from Al-Waladiyya explains three common but invalid grounds for refuting an argument: verbosity (tatwil), correction (istidrak), and obscurity (khafa'). Understanding these helps you focus on substantive logical critique rather than stylistic complaints.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish content from expression
The passage distinguishes between the essence of the proof (dhat al-dalil) and its wording. Only defects affecting correctness (sihha) are valid refutations, not those affecting beauty (husn).
This is the key principle: the existence of a better expression does not make a correct argument invalid.
2
Identify verbosity (tatwil)
Verbosity is excessive wording where some parts are unnecessary and do not add meaning. The argument is still correct, just wordy. Objecting to verbosity is not a valid refutation.
Example: 'The world is created because it is contingent, and everything contingent has a cause.' Adding extra phrases like 'as everyone knows' is verbose but not a flaw in reasoning.
3
Recognize correction (istidrak)
Correction occurs when one part of the wording makes another part redundant, but the argument remains sound. Objecting to redundancy is not a refutation.
Example: 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates, and intoxicants are forbidden.' Saying both 'intoxicates' and 'intoxicants' is a correction, but the argument still holds.
4
Detect obscurity (khafa')
Obscurity can be in the intended meaning (e.g., ambiguous terms) or in the indication (e.g., rare words, figurative language without context). However, if the intended sense can be clarified, obscurity is not a ground for rejecting the argument.
Example: Using a rare Arabic word like 'khamr' for wine might be obscure to some, but the argument's validity remains if the meaning is clarified.
5
Apply the principle of the superior path
The passage states: 'the existence of a superior path does not necessitate the invalidity of the inferior path' (al-tariq al-rajih la yujibu butlan al-tariq al-marjuh). So even if a better expression exists, the original argument is not refuted.
This principle safeguards debates from descending into style wars and keeps focus on logical substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فى بيان النقض الغير المسموع
لا ينقض ذات الدليل وغيره من المركبات التامة والناقصة بالاشتمال على التطويل أو الاستدراك أو الخفاء إلى غير ذلك مما يزيل حسنه ولا يزيل صحته. والتطويل هو إكثار اللفظ بحيث لا يعنى بعضه عن بعض. والاستدراك هو إغناء بعض اللفظ عن بعض. والخفاء في المدلول أو الدلالة. فلا يصح لأحد المناظرين أن يقول للآخر إن ما ذكرته باطل لأن المعنى الذي أديته بما ذكرته من العبارة يصح أداؤه بأحسن منها. وإنما لا يصح ذلك النقض لأن وجود الطريق الراجح لا يوجب بطلان الطريق المرجوح. وإنما يصح الاعتراض به.
Section on explaining the refutation that is not heard. The essence of the proof and other perfect and incomplete composites are not refuted by the inclusion of verbosity, correction, or obscurity, up to other things that remove its beauty but do not remove its correctness. Verbosity is excessive wording such that some part does not suffice for another. Correction is that some wording makes other wording unnecessary. Obscurity is in the meaning or the indication. So it is not valid for either of the two disputants to say to the other that what you mentioned is false because the meaning you conveyed with the expression you mentioned can be conveyed with a better one. Indeed, that refutation is not valid because the existence of a superior path does not necessitate the invalidity of the inferior path. And objection is only valid [when based on that].
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of 'invalid refutation' (al-naqd ghayr al-masmu'), i.e., a refutation that is not accepted by the scholars of debate. It explains that one cannot reject an argument merely based on three features: verbosity (tatwil), where some words are not needed but the meaning is still correct; correction (istidrak), where one word makes another redundant; or obscurity (khafa'), whether in the intended meaning (like the terms 'aql and ruh) or in the indication (e.g., rare words, ambiguous words without context, etc.). The underlying principle is that the existence of a superior way of expressing the argument does not nullify the validity of the argument itself. As long as the core content remains sound, the form may be imperfect but still acceptable. The author emphasizes that such objections are not valid as refutations of the argument itself; they are merely about the wording, and the correct response is not to reject the argument but to acknowledge that there are better formulations. The passage teaches the discipline of focusing on substantive logical critique rather than stylistic complaints.

Key Terms
التطويل
الاستدراك
الخفاء
الطريق الراجح
الطريق المرجوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid ground for rejecting an argument according to the passage on invalid refutation?
A) The argument includes unnecessary wording (tatwil)
B) The argument is obscure in its intended meaning (khafa')
C) The argument contains a contradictory premise
D) The argument has a redundant clause (istidrak)

Key Takeaway
Objections to an argument based on verbosity, redundancy, or obscurity are not accepted as refutations—only defects that undermine correctness matter.

Three Invalid Objections: Taṭwīl, Istidrāk, Khafā'
Lists three unacceptable objections from dialectical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of invalid objections (taṭwīl, istidrāk, khafā') and explain why they do not invalidate the argument itself.

Core Idea
Stylistic flaws do not invalidate logical arguments.

In dialectical debates, not every objection carries weight. The author of this text identifies three specific types of objections that are commonly raised but are invalid because they attack the expression rather than the logical substance. In this lesson, you will learn to distinguish these objection types and understand why they are dismissed in proper disputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objection types
From the passage, extract the three Arabic terms: taṭwīl, istidrāk, khafā'. Read their definitions: taṭwīl is excessive wording with redundancy; istidrāk is unnecessary insertion; khafā' is obscurity in meaning or indication.
Focus on the definitions in the parentheses.
2
Understand the principle of invalidity
The author states that these objections do not invalidate the argument because the meaning can be conveyed with a better expression. The key principle: existence of a preferable path (ṭarīq rājiḥ) does not negate the inferior path (ṭarīq marjūḥ).
3
Analyze examples of khafā'
The text gives examples: obscurity in meaning (like 'aql and rūḥ) or in indication due to strange words, homonyms without context, metaphors without indicators. Recognize these as types of khafā'.
This helps distinguish when obscurity is harmless versus when it might be a problem.
4
Apply the rule
Given an objection, determine if it belongs to one of these three categories. If so, and it only targets expression, deem it invalid according to the text.
Remember: the objection must be about the substance, not the style.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في بيان النقض الغير المسموع (لا ينقض ذات الدليل وغيره من المركبات التامة والناقصة بالاشتمال على التطويل) وهو إكثار اللفظ بحيث لا يعنى بعضه عن بعض (أو الاستدراك) وهو إغناء بعض اللفظ عن بعض (أو الخفاء) في المدلول كما في العقل والروح، أو في الدلالة إما لعدم اعتبار استعمال الدال في ذلك المدلول، كما في الألفاظ الغريبة أو لتعدد مدلوله كما في اللفظ المشترك بدون قرينة معينة أو لعدم تعينه كما في اللفظ المجاز بدونها (إلى غير ذلك مما يزيل حسنه) فلا يصح لأحد المناظرين أن يقول للآخر إن ما ذكرته باطل، لأن المعنى الذي أديته بما ذكرته من العبارة يصح أداؤه بأحسن منها (وإنما لا يصح ذلك النقض لأن وجود الطريق الراجح لا يوجب بطلان الطريق المرجوح وإنما يصح الاعتراض به أى بذلك الاشتمال على حسن العبارة ويسمى هذا الاعتراض تعيين الطريق وهو ليس من دأب المناظرين الناظرين لإظهار الصواب. قال بعض الأفاضل ورد عليه أن الاعتراض بالاستدراك كثير في كلام الفحول. قال عصام الدين: إذا اعترض السائل أن هناك طريقا راجحا لسهولته وقلة مؤنته، فلابد في العدول عنه إلى هذا الطريق من نكتة فلا يندفع بأن يقال إنه تعيين الطريق، بل يجب بيان نكتة هذا. وهنا استثناء وهو أن كون التعريف أخفى من المعرف دلالة عند كونه تعريفا لفظيا ومدلولا عند كونه تعريفا حقيقيا، فيشمل الكلام كلا التعريفين ومن قصره على التعريف الحقيقي فقد قصر في البيان (يبطله كما عرفت).
Chapter: On the explanation of the unaccepted objection. It does not invalidate the essence of the proof and other [things] from complete and incomplete compounds by containing prolixity, which is abundance of wording such that some does not substitute for others; or insertion, which is enrichment of some wording from others; or obscurity in the meaning, as in intellect and soul, or in indication either due to lack of consideration of the use of the signifier in that signified, as in strange words, or due to multiplicity of its signified as in the homonym without a specifying clue, or due to lack of determination as in the metaphorical word without such [clue]; up to other things that remove its goodness. So it is not valid for either of the two disputants to say to the other: 'What you mentioned is invalid,' because the meaning that you conveyed by the expression you mentioned is validly conveyable by a better one. And that objection is only invalid because the existence of a preferable path does not necessitate the invalidity of the inferior path. Rather, objection by that containing is only valid regarding the goodness of the expression, and this objection is called 'specifying the path' (ta'yīn al-ṭarīq), and it is not from the practice of the disputants who look to manifest correctness. Some virtuous scholars said, and it was objected against him, that objection by insertion is frequent in the speech of the masters. 'Iṣām al-Dīn said: If the questioner objects that there is a preferable path due to its ease and less effort, then it is necessary for deviating from it to this path to have a subtle point, so it is not refuted by saying it is specifying the path; rather, that subtle point must be explained. And here there is an exception: that the definition is more obscure than the definiendum in indication when it is a verbal definition, and in meaning when it is a real definition, so it encompasses both definitions, and whoever restricts it to real definition has been deficient in explanation. It invalidates it as you have learned.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage classifies types of objections that are not accepted in dialectical debate (jadal). The author identifies three specific forms of unacceptable objections: (1) taṭwīl (prolixity) — where the expression is overly wordy such that parts are redundant; (2) istidrāk (insertion) — where unnecessary wording is added; and (3) khafā' (obscurity) — which can be in the meaning itself (like obscure concepts such as 'aql and rūḥ) or in the indication (dalāla) due to the signifier being strange, ambiguous (as in homonyms without context), or metaphorical without indicators. These three, along with similar defects that merely diminish the stylistic quality of the argument, do not invalidate the argument itself. The reason is that having a better, more elegant expression does not make the current one false. The author explains that such an objection is called 'ta'yīn al-ṭarīq' (specifying a better path), which is not the practice of true disputants who seek truth, because it aims at stylistic superiority rather than logical error. A rejoinder by some scholars notes that objection by istidrāk is common among masters, and 'Iṣām al-Dīn refutes that by arguing that if a questioner points out a better path, there must be a reason for deviating from it; simply calling it 'ta'yīn al-ṭarīq' does not suffice. The passage concludes with an exception: if the definition itself is more obscure than the definiendum, whether in indication (for verbal definitions) or in meaning (for real definitions), then such obscurity does invalidate the definition. This exception shows that when the defining expression fails to clarify, it is a genuine defect.

Key Terms
تطويل
استدراك
خفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, an objection based on taṭwīl (prolixity) is considered:
A) Valid and must be addressed
B) Invalid because it only targets the style, not the substance
C) Valid only if the meaning is obscured
D) A type of objection that the author recommends

Key Takeaway
Taṭwīl, istidrāk, and khafā' are invalid objections in jadal because they attack the expression, not the logical validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When Obscurity Matters: The Exception and Ta'yīn al-Ṭarīq
Details the exception for obscurity and the controversy over ta'yīn al-ṭarīq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the exception when khafā' invalidates a definition, and evaluate the dispute about ta'yīn al-ṭarīq as an objection.

Core Idea
Obscurity invalidates a definition only if the definition is more obscure than the definiendum.

While most objections based on obscurity are invalid, there is an important exception: if the definition itself is more obscure than the term being defined, it is a genuine defect. Furthermore, the passage discusses the objection called 'ta'yīn al-ṭarīq' (specifying a better path) and whether it is acceptable. Let's explore these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception
Find the sentence starting with 'وهنا استثناء'. It states that if the definition is more obscure than the definiendum, it invalidates the definition.
This is the only case where khafā' is a valid objection.
2
Distinguish verbal vs. real definitions
The text specifies: for verbal definitions (ta'rif lafẓī), obscurity is in indication (dalāla); for real definitions (ta'rif ḥaqīqī), obscurity is in meaning (madlūl). Recognize this distinction.
This nuance is important for accurate application.
3
Understand ta'yīn al-ṭarīq
Read the part about 'تعيين الطريق'. The author initially says it is not from the practice of disputants. Then 'Iṣām al-Dīn argues that if a better path is identified, deviation requires justification.
This shows a disagreement in the tradition.
4
Evaluate the debate
Consider both sides: the objection of ta'yīn al-ṭarīq is generally dismissed, but 'Iṣām al-Dīn's response suggests it can have merit if the questioner points out a genuinely superior path.
This reflects the dynamic nature of jadal rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في بيان النقض الغير المسموع (لا ينقض ذات الدليل وغيره من المركبات التامة والناقصة بالاشتمال على التطويل) وهو إكثار اللفظ بحيث لا يعنى بعضه عن بعض (أو الاستدراك) وهو إغناء بعض اللفظ عن بعض (أو الخفاء) في المدلول كما في العقل والروح، أو في الدلالة إما لعدم اعتبار استعمال الدال في ذلك المدلول، كما في الألفاظ الغريبة أو لتعدد مدلوله كما في اللفظ المشترك بدون قرينة معينة أو لعدم تعينه كما في اللفظ المجاز بدونها (إلى غير ذلك مما يزيل حسنه) فلا يصح لأحد المناظرين أن يقول للآخر إن ما ذكرته باطل، لأن المعنى الذي أديته بما ذكرته من العبارة يصح أداؤه بأحسن منها (وإنما لا يصح ذلك النقض لأن وجود الطريق الراجح لا يوجب بطلان الطريق المرجوح وإنما يصح الاعتراض به أى بذلك الاشتمال على حسن العبارة ويسمى هذا الاعتراض تعيين الطريق وهو ليس من دأب المناظرين الناظرين لإظهار الصواب. قال بعض الأفاضل ورد عليه أن الاعتراض بالاستدراك كثير في كلام الفحول. قال عصام الدين: إذا اعترض السائل أن هناك طريقا راجحا لسهولته وقلة مؤنته، فلابد في العدول عنه إلى هذا الطريق من نكتة فلا يندفع بأن يقال إنه تعيين الطريق، بل يجب بيان نكتة هذا. وهنا استثناء وهو أن كون التعريف أخفى من المعرف دلالة عند كونه تعريفا لفظيا ومدلولا عند كونه تعريفا حقيقيا، فيشمل الكلام كلا التعريفين ومن قصره على التعريف الحقيقي فقد قصر في البيان (يبطله كما عرفت).
Chapter: On the explanation of the unaccepted objection. It does not invalidate the essence of the proof and other [things] from complete and incomplete compounds by containing prolixity, which is abundance of wording such that some does not substitute for others; or insertion, which is enrichment of some wording from others; or obscurity in the meaning, as in intellect and soul, or in indication either due to lack of consideration of the use of the signifier in that signified, as in strange words, or due to multiplicity of its signified as in the homonym without a specifying clue, or due to lack of determination as in the metaphorical word without such [clue]; up to other things that remove its goodness. So it is not valid for either of the two disputants to say to the other: 'What you mentioned is invalid,' because the meaning that you conveyed by the expression you mentioned is validly conveyable by a better one. And that objection is only invalid because the existence of a preferable path does not necessitate the invalidity of the inferior path. Rather, objection by that containing is only valid regarding the goodness of the expression, and this objection is called 'specifying the path' (ta'yīn al-ṭarīq), and it is not from the practice of the disputants who look to manifest correctness. Some virtuous scholars said, and it was objected against him, that objection by insertion is frequent in the speech of the masters. 'Iṣām al-Dīn said: If the questioner objects that there is a preferable path due to its ease and less effort, then it is necessary for deviating from it to this path to have a subtle point, so it is not refuted by saying it is specifying the path; rather, that subtle point must be explained. And here there is an exception: that the definition is more obscure than the definiendum in indication when it is a verbal definition, and in meaning when it is a real definition, so it encompasses both definitions, and whoever restricts it to real definition has been deficient in explanation. It invalidates it as you have learned.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage classifies types of objections that are not accepted in dialectical debate (jadal). The author identifies three specific forms of unacceptable objections: (1) taṭwīl (prolixity) — where the expression is overly wordy such that parts are redundant; (2) istidrāk (insertion) — where unnecessary wording is added; and (3) khafā' (obscurity) — which can be in the meaning itself (like obscure concepts such as 'aql and rūḥ) or in the indication (dalāla) due to the signifier being strange, ambiguous (as in homonyms without context), or metaphorical without indicators. These three, along with similar defects that merely diminish the stylistic quality of the argument, do not invalidate the argument itself. The reason is that having a better, more elegant expression does not make the current one false. The author explains that such an objection is called 'ta'yīn al-ṭarīq' (specifying a better path), which is not the practice of true disputants who seek truth, because it aims at stylistic superiority rather than logical error. A rejoinder by some scholars notes that objection by istidrāk is common among masters, and 'Iṣām al-Dīn refutes that by arguing that if a questioner points out a better path, there must be a reason for deviating from it; simply calling it 'ta'yīn al-ṭarīq' does not suffice. The passage concludes with an exception: if the definition itself is more obscure than the definiendum, whether in indication (for verbal definitions) or in meaning (for real definitions), then such obscurity does invalidate the definition. This exception shows that when the defining expression fails to clarify, it is a genuine defect.

Key Terms
تعيين الطريق
خفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the definition is in a foreign language
B) When the definition is more obscure than the definiendum in indication or meaning
C) When the definition is too short
D) When the definition is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
Obscurity invalidates a definition only when it undermines clarity, and the debate over ta'yīn al-ṭarīq shows that identifying a better path can demand justification.

?
In a jadal session, a questioner objects that the respondent's proof for the existence of the soul uses the term 'rūḥ' which is obscure. Is this a valid objection? Use the text's exception to determine.

Explain why 'Iṣām al-Dīn's critique of dismissing ta'yīn al-ṭarīq is important for the fairness of debate.

Objections Against Expression in Islamic Dialectic
Author defines objections against expression based on linguistic violations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objection against expression (naqḍ al-ʿibāra) is valid in Islamic dialectic based on its violation of Arabic linguistic rules.

Core Idea
Linguistic errors in expression can invalidate an argument in Islamic debate.

In the previous section, we learned that stylistic flaws like prolixity do not necessarily invalidate an argument. Now we turn to a more serious type of objection: when the very wording of a statement violates the rules of Arabic. This passage opens a new chapter on objections directed at the expression (ʿibāra) itself. Such objections are grounded in the linguistic sciences—lexicology, morphology, grammar, and orthography. Understanding this type of objection is crucial for engaging in rigorous dialectical debates (munāẓara) where precision of language is paramount.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression under objection
Locate the exact wording (ʿibāra) that the objector is challenging. The expression could be from a definition, a division, a proof, or any other part of the argument.
2
Determine the linguistic rule allegedly violated
Check which of the Arabic sciences the objector claims is violated: lexicology (lugha), morphology (ṣarf), grammar (naḥw), or orthography (khaṭṭ). The passage lists these as valid grounds for objection.
Other Arabic sciences may also be relevant.
3
Verify the violation is genuine
Assess whether the expression truly violates the identified rule. The objection is only valid if the violation is real, not merely a perceived stylistic preference.
Refer to authoritative sources of the language (e.g., dictionaries, grammar books).
4
Evaluate the impact on the argument
Even if a violation exists, consider whether it affects the clarity or correctness of the argument. The passage implies that any such violation is sufficient to object, but in practice, some errors may be minor.
The author states that the objection is based on 'violation in reality or in judgment.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في بيان المناظرة الجارية في العبارة ( وقد ينقض العبارة ) سواء كانت عبارة التعريف أو التقسم أو الدليل أو غير ذلك وهى اللفظ سمى بها لعبور المخاطب منه إلى المعنى والمتكلم من المعنى إليه ولا يبعد تعميمها للخط ليعود الناظر منه إلى اللفظ أو الكاتب من اللفظ إليه. ومعناه دعوى السائل بطلانها مستدلا حقيقة أو حكما بمخالفتها قانون اللغة أو الصرف أو النحو أو الخط إلى غير ذلك من العلوم العربية.
Chapter: On the explanation of the disputation that occurs regarding the expression. And the expression may be objected to, whether it is the expression of a definition, division, proof, or other. It is the wording that is named by it [i.e., 'ibāra] for the crossing of the addressee from it to the meaning, and the speaker from the meaning to it. And it is not far to generalize it to writing, for the crossing of the viewer from it to the wording or the writer from the wording to it. Its meaning is the claim of the questioner of its invalidity, arguing, in reality or in judgment, by its violation of the law of language, morphology, grammar, orthography, or other Arabic sciences.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage opens a new topic on objections directed at the expression (ʿibāra) itself in dialectical debate. The author defines 'expression' as the wording that allows the addressee to cross from the words to the meaning, and the speaker from the meaning to the words. This can be extended to writing as well, where the reader goes from writing to wording and the writer from wording to writing. The objection here is the claim of the questioner that the expression is invalid, based on its violation of the rules of Arabic language: language (lugha), morphology (ṣarf), grammar (naḥw), orthography (khaṭṭ), or other Arabic sciences. Unlike the previous section where stylistic flaws like prolixity did not invalidate the argument, here linguistic incorrectness can be a valid ground for objecting to the expression itself. This is important because in Islamic dialectic, the language of argumentation must conform to the rules of Arabic to ensure clarity and correctness. The commentary notes that this kind of objection is separate from objections to the logical structure of the argument. For example, if a debater says 'Zayd qāma' with incorrect iʿrāb, the opponent may object on grammatical grounds, and this objection is valid.

Key Terms
عبارة
نقض العبارة
قانون اللغة

Key Takeaway
In Islamic dialectic, an expression can be objected to if it violates any rule of the Arabic sciences (lexicology, morphology, grammar, orthography), and such objections are valid and must be addressed.

The Objection of Determining the Path: When Is It Valid?
Analysis of the ta'yin al-tariq objection and its nuanced validity in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection of ta'yin al-tariq in a dialectical exchange and evaluate whether the respondent must provide a justification (nukta).

Core Idea
Not all path-preference objections are invalid; the respondent must have a good reason to avoid an easier path.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), the questioner often objects that the respondent should have taken a different, easier line of reasoning. This is called 'ta'yin al-tariq'. The author initially rejects it, but then presents a nuanced exception. In this lesson, we will unpack the objection and when it requires a response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Look for the questioner saying there is a more preferable path due to ease or less effort. The text defines ta'yin al-tariq as 'preferring one path over another path that was taken'.
The objection is about the method, not the content.
2
Recognize the default rule
The author states that such objections are not part of the dialecticians' practice for revealing truth. Initially, ta'yin al-tariq is dismissed as irrelevant.
This is the starting position.
3
Note the exception
If the questioner points out a clearly better path (due to ease or less burden), the respondent must give a subtle point (nukta) for avoiding it. Simply dismissing the objection as 'ta'yin al-tariq' is insufficient.
The exception is introduced via 'Isam al-Din.
4
Distinguish definition types
The exception also covers obscurity in definitions: verbal definitions allow obscurity in signification, real definitions allow obscurity in meaning. The text warns against restricting this to real definitions only.
This is a refinement of the rule about obscurity.
5
Evaluate the response
Determine if the respondent's nukta is sufficient or if the objection is merely a distraction. The author's final note (nazar) clarifies that obscurity in meaning does not ruin a good expression or definition unless it becomes more obscure than the common understanding.
This adds depth to the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على حسن العبارة ويسمى هذا الاعتراض تعيين الطريق وهو ترجيح طريق على طريق سلك إليه. وهو ليس من دأب المناظرين لإظهار الصواب، ولا مدخل لهذا الاعتراض في إظهار الصواب. قال بعض الأفاضل: ورد عليه أن الاعتراض بالاستدراك كثير في كلام الفحول. قال عصام الدين: إذا اعترض السائل أن هناك طريقا راجحا لسهولته وقلة مؤنته، فلابد في العدول عنه إلى هذا الطريق من نكتة فلا يندفع بأن يقال إنه تعيين الطريق، بل يجب بيان نكتة هذا. وهنا استثناء: وهو أن كون التعريف أخفى من المعرف دلالة عند كونه تعريفا لفظيا ومدلولا عند كونه تعريفا حقيقيا، فيشمل الكلام كلا التعريفين. ومن قصره على التعريف الحقيقي فقد قصر في البيان. يبطله كما عرفت. وفي هذا نظر إذ يفهم منه أن الخفاء في المدلول يزيل حسن العبارة التي ليست بتعريف وأنه يزيل حسن التعريف الذي وجد فيه ولم يكن فهم مدلولها أخفى من فهم مدلول العرف وليس كذلك. لا يقال المراد من الخفاء: الخفاء في الدلالة فقط. لأنا نقول التعريف الحقيقي لا ينقض ولو كان أخفى دلالة من المعرف فلا يصح الاستنتاج فتأمل.
On the beauty of expression, and this objection is called 'determining the path'—it is preferring one path over another path that was taken. And it is not the practice of the dialecticians for revealing correctness, and this objection has no entry in revealing correctness. Some distinguished scholars said: It is countered that the objection by seeking precedence is frequent in the speech of the masters. 'Isam al-Din said: If the questioner objects that there is a more preferable path due to its ease and less burden, then there must be in turning away from it to this path some point, so it is not repelled by saying it is 'determining the path'; rather, it is necessary to state the point of this. And here there is an exception: that the definition is more obscure than the defined in signification when it is a verbal definition, and in meaning when it is a real definition; so the statement includes both definitions. And whoever restricts it to the real definition has fallen short in explanation. He refutes it as you have known. And in this there is consideration, for it is understood from it that obscurity in the meaning removes the beauty of the expression which is not a definition, and that it removes the beauty of the definition in which it is found, and the understanding of its meaning is not more obscure than the understanding of the meaning of the definition—it is not so. It cannot be said that what is meant by obscurity is only obscurity in signification, for we say the real definition is not refuted even if it is more obscure in signification than the defined; so the conclusion is not valid—ponder.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the dialectical objection known as 'determining the path' (ta'yin al-tariq). The author begins by stating that this objection—where the questioner criticizes the respondent for not choosing an easier or more obvious line of reasoning—is not part of the proper practice of dialecticians whose goal is to reveal truth. The objection has no role in uncovering truth. Then some scholars are cited as saying that such an objection (by seeking a more preferable path) is actually common among the greatest scholars. 'Isam al-Din responds that if the questioner points out a more preferable path due to its ease, the respondent must have a good reason to avoid it; simply dismissing the objection as 'determining the path' is insufficient. Next, an exception is introduced: the definition being more obscure than the defined is legitimate in verbal definitions (where obscurity is in the word's signification) and in real definitions (where obscurity is in the concept). A critic who restricts the exception to real definitions only is mistaken. Finally, the author adds a critical note (nazar) that the previous explanation might be misunderstood: obscurity in meaning does not actually invalidate a good expression or a definition unless it becomes more obscure than what is commonly understood, which is not the case here. He warns against narrowing 'obscurity' to signification only, because a real definition is not refuted even if its signification is more obscure than the defined.

Key Terms
تعيين الطريق
استثناء
نكتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is always a valid objection in dialectic.
B) It should be rejected outright because it does not help reveal truth.
C) It can be valid, but the respondent must provide a point (nukta) justifying the choice.
D) It only applies to verbal definitions.

Key Takeaway
The objection of ta'yin al-tariq is not a blanket fallacy; the respondent must provide a point (nukta) if the questioner points out a clearly superior path, but frivolous objections are rejected.

But you could have used the simpler definition of refraining from food, drink, and sexual relations from dawn to sunset.' Does this require a nukta? Why or why not?
