Dialectical Defense: Roles of Objector and Preventer in Jadal
Author illustrates a dialectical exchange defending an expression against grammatical objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the roles of the objector (nāqiḍ) and preventer (māni') in a dialectical exchange and apply the correct method of objection and response according to the rules of jadal.

Core Idea
In jadal, an objection is an argument that must be refuted by denying a premise, not by mere rejection.

In classical Islamic dialectic, objecting to an opponent's statement is not a simple denial—it is an argument that must be met with a reasoned response. This lesson unpacks a passage from the Waliyya that explains how to properly object and defend using the roles of nāqiḍ (objector) and māni' (preventer). You will learn to analyze the logical structure of objections and construct valid defenses based on denying premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles
The objector (nāqiḍ) raises an objection and is considered an arguer (mustadill) who provides evidence. The defender (māni') responds by preventing the objection, but only by denying a premise of the objector's argument.
This is a key principle in jadal: objections are not mere claims but structured arguments.
2
Analyze the objection's structure
The objector presents a categorical syllogism: a minor premise (the expression contains implicit reference before mention) and a major premise (every such expression violates grammar), leading to the conclusion that the expression is invalid.
Identifying the premises is crucial for the defender to know which to deny.
3
Examine the defender's options
The defender can deny the minor premise by rephrasing the expression to show no implicit reference exists, or deny the major premise by citing a grammatical school that permits such constructions.
The defender must provide evidence or authority for the denial, not just assert it.
4
Apply the principle of argumentative refutation
The defender must actively engage with the objector's premises. Simply rejecting the objection without addressing the premises does not count as a valid defense.
This ensures dialectical exchanges remain rigorous and evidence-based.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزى ربه على عدي بن حاتم جزاء الكلاب العاويات وقد فعل. يعترض السائل بأن هذه العبارة مخالفة لقانون النحو وكل عبارة شأنها هذا فهي فاسدة، ويثبت الصغرى بأنها مشتملة على الإضمار قبل الذكر لفظا ورتبة فهي مخالفة لقانون النحو. وربما يجاب عن هذا النقض بمنع الاشتمال بتحرير تلك العبارة بحيث يظهر به عدم الاشتمال إن أمكن. وقد يجاب عنه بمنع مخالفتها لقانون العربية مستندا بمذهب من مذاهب أهل العربية يصح عليه تلك العبارة، بأن يقال في العبارة السابقة لا نسلم أنها مشتملة على الإضمار قبل الذكر لفظا ورتبة؛ لم لا يجوز أن يكون الضمير في جزى ربه راجعا إلى الجزاء المفهوم من قوله جزى: أي جزى رب الجزاء. وإن سلم أنها مشتملة على الإضمار قبل الذكر فلا نسلم أن كل عبارة مشتملة كذلك مخالفة لقانون النحو؛ لم لا يجوز أن يكون الكلام مبنيا على مذهب الأخفش وابن جني وهو جائز عندهما. وقد اشتهر بين المحصلين أن ناقض العبارة مستدل وموجهها مانع. ومعناه أن الاعتراض على العبارة سواء كان على ذاتها أو على حسنها بسبب مخالفتها القانون العربي لا يصح على طريق المنع بل على طريق الإبطال والاستدلال، وأن توجيهها لا يكون إلا بمنع مقدمة من مقدمات ذلك الاستدلال. لكن هذا النقض لا ينفع المعلل لعدم إتيانه بما يجب عليه عند منع المانع أي عند دفع السائل مدعاه مدللا كان أولا أو مقدمة دليله معينة أولا. وربما يجاب عن هذا النقض بمنع الاشتمال مستندا بتحرير تلك العبارة بحيث يظهر به عدم الاشتمال. وقد يجاب عنه بمنع مخالفتها لقانون العربية مستندا بمذهب من مذاهب أهل العربية تصح عليه تلك العبارة. كما إذا قيل: قوله مستدلا باطل لأنه مشتمل على الإضمار قبل الذكر وهو مخالف لقانون النحو وكل قول شأنه كذا فباطل؛ فإنه يجاب عنه بمنع الاشتمال مستدلا برجوع ذلك الضمير المدعى إلى المفهوم من قوله دعوى بطلانها وهو مذكور حكما. ولو قيل لأنه مشتمل على ضمير لم يذكر مرجعه صريحا ليجاب بمنع المخالفة معتدا بأن الذكر الحكمي يكفي في إرجاع الضمير. ولو ذكر مرجع الضمير صريحا بعد قوله مستدلا لإمكان الاستناد عند المنع بأن المرجع مذكور بعده وذلك جائز على رأي. وقد اشتهر بين المحصلين أن ناقض العبارة مستدل وموجهها مانع.
May God reward 'Adi ibn Hatim with the reward of howling dogs, and he did. The objector objects that this expression violates the law of grammar, and every expression of this sort is invalid. He establishes the minor premise by saying it contains an implicit reference before mention in word and order, thus violating grammar. Perhaps this objection is answered by denying the inclusion by editing that expression so that non-inclusion appears if possible. And it may be answered by denying its violation of the law of Arabic, relying on a school of Arabic scholars on which that expression is valid, by saying in the previous expression: we do not accept that it contains an implicit reference before mention in word and order; why may the pronoun in 'jazā rabbahu' not refer to the reward understood from his saying 'jazā': that is, 'the Lord of reward rewarded'? And if it is admitted that it contains an implicit reference before mention, we do not accept that every such expression violates the law of grammar; why may the speech not be built on the school of al-Akhfash and Ibn Jinnī, which is permissible in their view? And it is well-known among the learned that the objector to the expression is an arguer and its defender is a preventer. Its meaning is that objection to the expression, whether concerning its essence or its goodness, due to its violation of the Arabic law, is not valid by way of prevention but by way of invalidation and argumentation, and its defense can only be by denying a premise of that argumentation. But this objection does not benefit the proponent because he did not do what was required of him when the preventer prevents, i.e., when the objector repels his claim, whether it was first argued or a specific premise of his evidence. And perhaps this objection is answered by denying the inclusion, relying on editing that expression so that non-inclusion appears. And perhaps it is answered by denying its violation of the law of Arabic, relying on a school of Arabic scholars on which that expression is valid. As when it is said: his statement 'arguing' is invalid because it contains an implicit reference before mention, and that violates grammar, and every such statement is invalid; then it is answered by denying the inclusion, arguing that that claimed pronoun refers to the understood meaning from his saying 'claim of its invalidity', and it is mentioned implicitly. And if it is said because it contains a pronoun whose referent is not explicitly mentioned, then it is answered by denying the violation, considering that implicit mention suffices for the pronoun's reference. And if the pronoun's referent is explicitly mentioned after his saying 'arguing', due to the possibility of reliance when denying that the referent is mentioned afterwards, and that is permissible in one opinion. And it is well-known among the learned that the objector to the expression is an arguer and its defender is a preventer.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion (jadal) showing how an objection (naqḍ) to a statement's form is handled. The author begins with an example: the statement 'جزى ربه على عدي بن حاتم جزاء الكلاب العاويات وقد فعل' (May God reward 'Adi ibn Hatim with the reward of howling dogs, and he did). The opponent objects that this expression violates grammar because it includes an implicit reference (iḍmār) before the explicit mention of the referent, both in word and order. This, the opponent argues, makes the expression invalid. The author explains how the proponent can respond in two ways. First, by denying that the statement contains such an implicit reference altogether, rephrasing it to show the pronoun's referent is already understood. Second, even if it does contain such an implicit reference, the proponent can deny that this violates grammar, citing the school of al-Akhfash and Ibn Jinnī which permits such constructions. The author then lays down a key principle: the objector to a statement (nāqiḍ al-'ibāra) is considered an arguer (mustadill), while the defender (muwajjih) is a preventer (māni'). This means that objecting to a statement's form or validity is not a mere claim (man') but an argument that must be rebutted by invalidating its evidence. The defense, therefore, must proceed by denying one of the premises of the objector's argument. The author notes that if the proponent simply denies without providing a reasoned basis (i.e., without establishing what he denied), the objection does not benefit him; he must actually refute the objector's evidence. The passage ends with a detailed example of how to answer different formulations of an objection, emphasizing that implicit mention (dhikr ḥukmī) can suffice for pronoun reference, and that explicit mention later can also be acceptable. Throughout, the author stresses that the correct method in dialectic is to engage arguments, not merely to reject them.

Key Terms
النقض
ناقض العبارة
مانع
الذكر الحكمي

A) Simply deny the objection without evidence.
B) Accept the objection and modify the statement.
C) Deny one of the premises of the objector's argument with evidence.
D) Withdraw the statement entirely.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, an objection is an argument (istidlāl) that must be countered by denying a premise, not by mere rejection.

?

The Four Types of Objection in Dialectic
Author enumerates and distinguishes four types of naqd: definition, division, proof, and expression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four recognized types of objection (naqd) in formal jadal, and explain why objections to undemonstrated claims or premises are classified differently.

Core Idea
Only four types of objection are admissible in formal dialectic: definition, division, proof, and expression.

In this lesson, we examine the classification of objections (naqd) in Islamic dialectical theory. The author presents four absolute types and clarifies why certain challenges are not counted as legitimate objections. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for anyone who wants to engage in formal debate (munazara) according to the rules of adab al-bahth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of naqd
Locate the four types listed: objection to definition (naqḍ al-ta'rīf), division (naqḍ al-taqsīm), proof (naqḍ al-dalīl), and expression (naqḍ al-'ibāra). Note the word 'مطلقا' (absolutely), meaning these are unqualified forms.
2
Analyze the imagined objection to expansion
The author anticipates an argument: why not six types, including objection to an undemonstrated claim and premise? Understand that the opponent is trying to broaden the classification.
3
Examine the author's response
The author rejects the addition by noting that technical objection (al-naqḍ al-iṣṭilāḥī) does not include these; they are not 'admissible' (masmū'). Instead, they are termed 'detailed objection' (naqḍ tafsīlī).
4
Distinguish absolute vs detailed objection
See that absolute objections directly attack a definition, division, proof, or expression, while detailed objection merely demands evidence for a claim or premise. This distinction is crucial for proper jadal procedure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة أن النقض أربعة: الأول نقض التعريف مطلقا، والثاني نقض التقسيم مطلقا، والثالث نقض الدليل مطلقا، والرابع نقض العبارة مطلقا. فإن قلت: بل هو ستة لأن معنى النقض الهدم والإبطال فيدخل فيه إبطال المدعى الغير المدلل وإبطال المقدمة الغير المدللة. قلت: الكلام في النقض الاصطلاحي وهما ليسا من النقض المسموع بالاتفاق. وأما طلب الدليل على المدعى مدللا كان أو لا أو المقدمة المعينة الغير مدللة فلا يسمى نقضا مطلقا بل نقضا تفصيليا كما مر.
In summary, objection is four: first, objection to the definition absolutely; second, objection to the division absolutely; third, objection to the proof absolutely; fourth, objection to the expression absolutely. For if you say: rather it is six, because the meaning of objection is demolition and invalidation, so it includes invalidation of the undemonstrated claim and invalidation of the undemonstrated premise. I say: the discussion concerns technical objection, and these two are not among the admissible objections by consensus. As for demanding evidence for the claim, whether it was demonstrated or not, or for a specific undemonstrated premise, it is not called objection absolutely but rather detailed objection, as has passed.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a classification (taqsīm) of the types of objection (naqḍ) in dialectical theory. The author states that objection is of four absolute kinds: (1) objection to a definition (naqḍ al-ta'rīf) – showing that the definition does not apply correctly; (2) objection to a division (naqḍ al-taqsīm) – showing that a classification is incomplete or flawed; (3) objection to a proof (naqḍ al-dalīl) – refuting the evidence given for a claim; and (4) objection to an expression (naqḍ al-'ibāra) – criticizing the wording or grammar. The author then anticipates a question: why not six, including objection to an undemonstrated claim (naqḍ al-mudda'ā ghayr al-mudallal) and objection to an undemonstrated premise (naqḍ al-muqaddima ghayr al-mudallala)? The answer is that these are not considered legitimate objections ('adam al-samā') in technical terminology; they are not admissible in formal debate. Instead, demanding evidence for a claim or premise (whether already argued or not) is called a detailed objection (naqḍ tafsīlī), not an absolute objection. This distinction is important for understanding the rules of legitimate challenge in jadal.

Key Terms
نقض التعريف
نقض التقسيم
نقض الدليل
نقض العبارة
النقض التفصيلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Objection to the definition
B) Objection to the expression
C) Objection to the undemonstrated claim
D) Objection to the proof

Key Takeaway
In formal dialectic, only objections to definition, division, proof, and expression are recognized as absolute objections; challenges to unsubstantiated claims are classified as detailed objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Four Types of Naqd in Munazara
Classification of naqd into four types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four accepted types of naqd and explain why invalidation of an unproven claim is excluded from them.

Core Idea
Naqd is a specific technical move that invalidates an unspecified premise, not any objection.

In formal dialectical disputation (munazara), not every challenge counts as a refutation (naqd). The author of our passage defines naqd as the invalidation of an unspecified premise – a move that shifts the discussion to a line unhelpful to the defender. He then lists exactly four recognized types. Let's walk through them and understand why other potential moves are excluded.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of naqd
Read the opening sentence: naqd is the invalidation of an unspecified premise, and it forces the disputants to digress into an inquiry that does not help the original arguer. This is a technical definition, not a general one.
The phrase 'غير معينة' (unspecified) is key: naqd attacks a premise that has not been explicitly stated or fully defended.
2
Identify the four types listed
The author explicitly says there are four types: naqd of the definition, naqd of the division, naqd of the evidence, and naqd of the wording. Each is described as 'مطلقا' (absolutely), meaning it applies in all cases without exception.
These correspond to the four main components of a disputation: how terms are defined, how topics are divided, how proofs are presented, and how expressions are used.
3
Recognize the objection and the author's response
An imaginary objector suggests that the general meaning of naqd (demolition) would include invalidating an unproven claim or premise, making six types. The author replies that in technical terminology, those two are not accepted; they are either considered 'usurped' terms or not heard among the verifiers.
This shows how technical disciplines restrict everyday words to precise meanings – a common move in Islamic logic.
4
Distinguish naqd from requesting evidence
Finally, the author clarifies that simply asking for evidence on a claim or premise does not count as naqd, unless it is specified as 'detailed naqd' (naqd tafsili). This draws a clear boundary: naqd must invalidate a premise, not just demand support.
This preserves the specialized role of naqd in munazara; otherwise, every request for proof would derail the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعارضة والنقض إذ النقض إبطال مقدمة غير معينة في الحقيقة بل هو انتقال منه إلى بحث آخر لا ينفعه في إثبات المدفوع. فتفطن. وبالجملة أن النقض أربعة: الأول نقض التعريف مطلقا، والثاني نقض التقسيم مطلقا، والثالث نقض الدليل مطلقا، والرابع نقض العبارة مطلقا. فإن قلت: بل هو سنة لأن معنى النقض الهدم والابطال فيدخل فيه إبطال المدعى الغير المدلل وإبطال المقدمة الغير المدللة. قلت: الكلام في النقض للمصطاح وهما يمنعان غصبا في اصطلاح المناظرين أو يقال في النقض المسموع بالاتفاق وهما غير مسموعين عند المحققين. وأما طلب الدليل على المدعى مدللا أولا أو المقدمة مطلقا فلا يسمى نقضا أو بدون قيد التفصيلي بل نقضا تفصيليا كما مر.
And al-mu'arada (counter-argument) and al-naqd (refutation): indeed al-naqd is the invalidation of an unspecified premise in reality, rather it is a transition from him (i.e., the one giving the reason) to another investigation that does not benefit him in proving what he is defending. So be attentive. In summary, al-naqd is of four types: first, naqd of the definition absolutely; second, naqd of the division absolutely; third, naqd of the evidence absolutely; fourth, naqd of the wording absolutely. For if you said: rather it is six, because the meaning of naqd is demolition and invalidation, so it includes invalidating the unproven claim and invalidating the unproven premise. I said: the discussion is about naqd according to the terminology, and those two are prevented as usurpation in the terminology of the disputants, or it is said about heard naqd by agreement, and those two are not heard according to the verifiers. And as for requesting evidence for the claim, whether it is supported by evidence or not, or the premise absolutely, it is not called naqd, or without the detailed qualification, rather a detailed naqd as has passed.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical classification of the concept of al-naqd (refutation) as used in munazara (dialectical disputation). The author first defines naqd as the invalidation of an unspecified premise, emphasizing that it is a move that shifts the discussion to a different line of investigation that does not help the original defender. Then he enumerates four accepted types: naqd of the definition, naqd of the division, naqd of the evidence, and naqd of the wording. A potential objection is raised: some say there are six types because invalidation of an unproven claim and invalidation of an unproven premise also fall under the general meaning of naqd. The author responds that in technical terminology, those two are not counted; they are either considered usurped terms or not accepted among the verifiers. Finally, he clarifies that simply requesting evidence for a claim or premise is not naqd unless it is specified as 'detailed naqd' (naqd tafsili). This draws a precise boundary around the technical term, ensuring that only certain objection moves qualify as naqd in formal munazara.

Key Terms
النقض (al-naqd): The invalidation of an unspecified premise in a disputation, moving the discussion to a different line that does not help the original arguer's defense. [Note: In earlier logic, naqd could refer to counterexample; here it is specifically a technical move in munazara.]
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): Counter-argument or objection that does not invalidate a premise but presents a parallel argument. [Note: In falsafa, mu'arada can mean counter-syllogism; here it is paired with naqd as a dialectical tool.]
التفصيلي (al-tafsili): Detailed refutation that specifies the flawed premise or claim being attacked. [Note: This term is specific to the Waldiyya commentary; not widely used in other logic works.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is NOT a recognized type of naqd?
A) Naqd of the definition
B) Naqd of the unproven claim
C) Naqd of the evidence
D) Naqd of the wording

Key Takeaway
Naqd in munazara is a technical term limited to four precise types: definition, division, evidence, and wording – requests for proof and attacks on unproven claims are not counted.

What Counts as Naqd? The Boundary of Refutation in Munazara
Boundary conditions of naqd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid naqd move and an objection that does not qualify as naqd, such as requesting evidence or invalidating an unproven claim.

Core Idea
Not every objection is naqd – only those that invalidate an unspecified premise and shift the discussion away from the defender's proof.

In the previous lesson we learned the four accepted types of naqd. Now we will focus on what is excluded: simple requests for evidence, attacks on unproven claims, and unproven premises. The author carefully restricts naqd to moves that actually invalidate a premise in the opponent's argument, not just challenge its support.

Guided Path
1
Contrast naqd with mu'arada
The passage begins by pairing naqd with mu'arada (counter-argument). While both are objections, mu'arada does not invalidate a premise but offers a parallel argument. Naqd specifically invalidates an unspecified premise.
Mu'arada is a different tool; it provides an alternative proof, not a refutation.
2
Examine the excluded types
The objector suggests including 'invalidating the unproven claim' and 'invalidating the unproven premise' as additional types. The author rejects them because in technical munazara, these do not meet the definition: they attack something not yet established, which is not a premise in play.
The technical restriction ensures naqd targets premises that have been presented, not those that are missing.
3
Understand the role of 'detailed naqd'
The author says that requesting evidence for a claim or premise is not called naqd unless it is specified as 'detailed naqd' (naqd tafsili). This suggests that a mere request is insufficient; one must point out a specific flaw or gap in the proof.
This nuance prevents debates from devolving into endless demands for justification.
4
Apply the boundary to a real debate
Imagine a munazara on the validity of khabar al-wahid. If one opponent says 'Your premise that the narrators are trustworthy is unproven,' that is not a naqd because it does not invalidate the premise; it only says it hasn't been proven. However, saying 'Your premise is false because two narrators are known liars' would be a naqd of evidence.
The key is whether the objection actually demolishes the premise or merely asks for more support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A potential objection is raised: some say there are six types because invalidation of an unproven claim and invalidation of an unproven premise also fall under the general meaning of naqd. The author responds that in technical terminology, those two are not counted; they are either considered usurped terms or not accepted among the verifiers. Finally, he clarifies that simply requesting evidence for a claim or premise is not naqd unless it is specified as 'detailed naqd' (naqd tafsili). This draws a precise boundary around the technical term, ensuring that only certain objection moves qualify as naqd in formal munazara.

Key Terms
النقض (al-naqd): The invalidation of an unspecified premise in a disputation, moving the discussion to a different line that does not help the original arguer's defense. [Note: In earlier logic, naqd could refer to counterexample; here it is specifically a technical move in munazara.]
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): Counter-argument or objection that does not invalidate a premise but presents a parallel argument. [Note: In falsafa, mu'arada can mean counter-syllogism; here it is paired with naqd as a dialectical tool.]
التفصيلي (al-tafsili): Detailed refutation that specifies the flawed premise or claim being attacked. [Note: This term is specific to the Waldiyya commentary; not widely used in other logic works.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered a valid naqd?
A) Requesting evidence for the claim
B) Invalidating an unproven premise
C) Naqd of the wording
D) Invalidating the unproven claim

Key Takeaway
Naqd has a strict technical boundary: it must invalidate an unspecified premise that has been presented, and does not include mere requests for evidence or attacks on unproven elements.

Incomplete Compounds as Qayd: Rules of Objection in Dialectic
Author explains when an incomplete compound is a qayd and thus subject to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when an incomplete compound in a dialectical discourse can be objected to by man' (prevention) and identify the correct dialectical response based on whether it is a qayd of the proposition.

Core Idea
Incomplete compounds are only open to man' when they function as a qayd — they become conceptual truths.

In dialectical debates, not every expression can be challenged by simple denial. The author of the Waldiyya refines the rules: an incomplete compound (like 'Roman man') is treated as a conceptual truth only when it restricts the proposition. This lesson unpacks when you may object and when you may not, and what to do after a proof is given.

Guided Path
1
Identify the incomplete compound
Find the phrase that cannot stand alone as a complete sentence within a larger proposition. For example, 'Roman' in 'this is a Roman man' is an incomplete compound.
It may be attached to subject, predicate, or relation.
2
Determine if it is a qayd of the proposition
Ask whether deleting the compound would change the logical content. If yes, it is a qayd and a conceptual truth (tasdiq ma'na). For example, 'Roman' restricts the predicate 'man'.
Possessive constructions like 'one of Zayd's slaves' are not qayd.
3
If it is a qayd, the questioner may use man' to deny the added attribute specifically
The objector can deny the Roman-ness without denying the core humanity. The author says man' is valid absolutely unless the compound is an evident truth.
The denial is targeted only at the added attribute, not the whole proposition.
4
If the defender provides a proof, the questioner has three options
The questioner may: (1) prevent a premise of that proof, (2) present a counter-argument (mu'arada), or (3) refute the proof (naqd). If the compound is not a qayd, only naqd for grammatical violation is allowed.
This structured response ensures the debate remains focused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المناظرة الجارية في المركبات الناقصة. اعلم أن المركب الناقص وهو الذي لا يصح السكوت عليه إذا كان قيدا للقضية سواء كانت حملية أو شرعية موجبة أو سالبة بأن كان قيدا للمحكوم به أو للمحكوم عليه أو قيدا للنسبة فذا تصديق معنى يعني أن قولك هذا إنسان رومي بمنزلة قولك هذا إنسان ورومي. وكذا سائر المركبات الناقصة، والمراد بالمركب هنا المعني الثاني. وإذا كان كذلك فيرد عليه المنع مطلقا سواء كان مجردا أو مع السند مطلقا إذا لم يكن بديهيا جليا حقيقيا أو حكميا. مثال للمركب الناقص كأن تقول هذا العالم إنسان رومي دائما فان الرومي وقيد دائما مركب ناقص المحكوم به وهو بمنزلة قولنا هذا رومي. قيل فان مجموع الرومي والإنسان والنسبة التقييدية بينهما مركب ناقص وقيد للقضية لعدم احتياجها إلى ذلك المجموع وهو بمنزلة قولك هذا رومي وقس عليه قيد المحكوم عليه والنسبة. ولا يخفى ما فيه لأن القيد لابد أن يكون خارجا عن القضية فتأمل. فللسائل أن يمنع روميته فقط أى بدون أن يمنع إنسانيته وأن يمنع عالميته فقط وأن يمنع دوام النسبة. فإن أثبت المعلل روميته أو عالميته أو دوام النسبة بدليل أو تنبيه فللسائل حينئذ ثلاث وظائف إما أن يمنع مقدمة من مقدمات ذلك الدليل أو يعارضه أو ينقضه. والمتفطن لا يخفى عليه ذلك. وأما إذا لم يكن المركب الناقص قيدا للقضية بأن لا يكون قيدا سواء كان ذلك المركب مركبا إضافيا كأن قال أحد غلام زيد يسكون الغلام وجر زيد أو مركبا تعداديا كأن قال خمسة عشر أو غير ذلك كرجل عالم أو راقود خلا أو يكون قيد المركب ناقص كأن قال غلام رجل عالم أو يكون قيدا للانشاء كأن قال لا تقرا القرآن محدثا فلا يعترض عليه بشيء من المنع لأنه ليس بتصديق معنى فلا يجرى فيه المناظرة كالمفرد والإنشاء إلا بالنقض مخالفة ذلك اللفظ القانون العربي إذا خالفه.
Chapter on the dialectical discourse concerning incomplete compounds. Know that the incomplete compound, which is that upon which it is not proper to be silent, when it is a restriction of the proposition, whether it is categorical or conditional, affirmative or negative, whether it is a restriction of the thing judged or the subject or the relation, then that is a conceptual truth, meaning that your saying 'this is a Roman man' is equivalent to your saying 'this is a man and a Roman'. And similarly for other incomplete compounds. And what is meant by 'compound' here is the second meaning. And if it is so, then objection by prevention (man') is valid absolutely, whether it is bare or with a support absolutely, if it is not an evident truth in reality or by ruling. An example of an incomplete compound is when you say 'this world is a Roman man always', for 'Roman' and the restriction 'always' are an incomplete compound of the thing judged, and it is equivalent to our saying 'this is a Roman'. It was said that the sum of 'Roman' and 'man' and the restrictive relation between them is an incomplete compound and a restriction of the proposition because the proposition does not need that sum, and it is equivalent to your saying 'this is a Roman'. And analogize to this the restriction of the subject and the relation. And it is not hidden what is in it, because the restriction must be outside the proposition, so reflect. So it is for the questioner to prevent his Roman-ness only, i.e., without preventing his humanity, and to prevent his world-ness only, and to prevent the perpetuity of the relation. Then if the one giving the reason proves his Roman-ness or his world-ness or the perpetuity of the relation by evidence or indication, then for the questioner at that time there are three functions: either to prevent a premise from the premises of that evidence, or to counter it, or to refute it. And the attentive one does not find that hidden. And as for when the incomplete compound is not a restriction of the proposition, such that it is not a restriction, whether that compound is an additive compound like when one says 'one of Zayd's slaves' with sukun on 'al-ghulam' and jarr on 'Zayd', or a numeral compound like 'fifteen', or other than that like 'a learned man' or 'a jar of vinegar', or it is a restriction of an incomplete compound like 'the slave of a learned man', or it is a restriction of a performative utterance like 'do not recite the Quran while in ritual impurity', then it is not objected to by anything from prevention, because it is not a conceptual truth, so dialectical discourse does not apply to it like the single word and performative utterance, except by refutation for violation of the Arabic grammatical law if it violates it.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical classification of how incomplete compounds (al-murakkab al-naqis) are treated in munazara. The author first defines an incomplete compound as a phrase that cannot stand alone as a complete sentence. Such a compound, when it functions as a qayd (restriction) on a proposition—whether it restricts the subject, predicate, or the relation—is considered a 'conceptual truth' (tasdiq ma'na). For example, 'Roman man' is equivalent to saying 'man and Roman' separately. In such cases, the questioner may object by simply denying (man') the additional attribute (e.g., refusing to accept the 'Roman-ness') without denying the core. If the defender proves that attribute, the questioner then has three options: deny a premise of that proof, present a counter-argument (mu'arada), or refute the proof (naqd). On the other hand, if the incomplete compound is not a qayd of the proposition—for example, possessive constructions like 'one of Zayd's slaves', numeral compounds like 'fifteen', or compound descriptions like 'a learned man'—then it is not treated as a conceptual truth and no objection by man' is allowed; the only permissible move is a naqd if the expression violates Arabic grammatical norms. The distinction hinges on whether the compound directly modifies the logical content of the proposition or is merely a linguistic complex.

Key Terms
المركب الناقص
القيد
تصديق معنى
المنع
المعارضة
النقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT subject to objection by man' according to the passage?
A) 'Roman man' as a qayd
B) 'Always' as a qayd of perpetuity
C) 'One of Zayd's slaves' as an additive compound
D) 'Do not recite Quran while in ritual impurity' as a performative restriction

Key Takeaway
In dialectical debates, only incomplete compounds functioning as logical restrictions (qayd) of a proposition are treated as conceptual truths and subject to direct denial (man'); otherwise, only grammatical refutation (naqd) is allowed.

Defining the Incomplete Compound and the Rule of Denial
Defines the incomplete compound and states the condition for applying denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the incomplete compound in the dialectical sense and state when denial (man') is applicable to it.

Core Idea
An incomplete compound is not a full proposition but a restriction—denial applies unless it is self-evident.

In dialectical debates, not every expression can be denied. The incomplete compound occupies a middle ground: it is more than a single word but less than a complete proposition. This lesson establishes what it is and when a questioner may object to it by denial.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the meaning of 'compound' in this context
The text specifies that 'compound' here refers to the second meaning, i.e., any composite expression that is not a complete proposition (not truth-bearing). Think of it as a phrase that functions as a restriction or qualification.
A complete proposition (qadiyya) is what can be true or false; an incomplete compound cannot.
2
Identify the condition for applying denial (man')
Denial is allowed on any incomplete compound that is a genuine restriction (qayd) of the subject or predicate, provided the compound is not self-evident (badihi), clear (jali), real (haqiqi), or judgmental (hukmi). Self-evident restrictions are not subject to denial because they are obviously true.
The exception ensures that trivial or obvious qualifiers do not become points of dispute.
3
Distinguish bare compounds from those with a support (sanad)
The rule applies equally whether the incomplete compound stands alone (mujarrad) or is attached to a supporting proof (sanad). The key factor is whether it functions as a restriction of the proposition.
A support is an additional premise that backs the compound, but it does not change the nature of the compound itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا سائر المركبات الناقصة، والمراد بالمركب هنا المعنى الثاني، وإذا كان كذلك فيرد عليه المنع مطلقا سواء كان مجردا أو مع السند مطلقا إذا لم يكن بديهيا جليا حقيقيا أو حكميا. مثال للمركب الناقص كأن تقول هذا العالم إنسان رومي دائما، فإن الرومي وقيد دائما مركب ناقص المحكوم به وهو بمنزلة قولنا هذا رومي. قيل فإن مجموع الرومي والإنسان والنسبة التقييدية بينهما مركب ناقص وقيد للقضية لعدم احتياجها إلى ذلك المجموع وهو بمنزلة قولك هذا رومي، وقس عليه قيد المحكوم عليه والنسبة. ولا يخفى ما فيه لأن القيد لابد أن يكون خارجا عن القضية فتأمل. فللسائل أن يمنع روميته فقط، أي رومية المشار إليه مطلقا فقط، يعني دون أن يمنع إنسانيته وأن يمنع عالميته فقط وأن يمنع دوام النسبة. قيل والنقض الشبيهي أو المعارضة التقديرية فكلامه مختل. أقول هذا مني عن الذهول عما سبق من المصنف من تعريف الغصب، وهما عنده عصيان فلا اختلال. فإن أثبت المعلل روميته أو عالميته أو دوام النسبة بدليل أو تنبيه، فللسائل حينئذ ثلاث وظائف: إما أن يمنع مقدمة من مقدمات ذلك الدليل أو يعارضه أو ينقضه. والمتفطن لا يخفى عليه ذلك. وأما إذا لم يكن المركب الناقص قيدا للقضية، بأن لا يكون قيدا، سواء كان ذلك المركب مركبا إضافيا كأن قال أحد غلام زيد، أو مركبا تعداديا كأن قال خمسة عشر، أو غير ذلك كرجل عالم وراقود خلا، أو يكون قيد المركب ناقص كأن قال غلام رجل عالم، أو يكون قيدا للإنشاء كأن قال لا تقرأ القرآن محدثا، فلا يعترض عليه بشيء من المنع لأنه ليس بتصديق معنى، فلا يجرى فيه المناظرة كالمفرد والإنشاء، إلا بالنقض مخالفة ذلك اللفظ القانون العربي إذا خالفه. والاعتراض باعتبار الدعوى الضمنية بل للمعارضة التقديرية والنقض الاجمالي الشبيهي مجوز بهما. قيل وقد عرفت أن المركب الذي كان قيدا للانشاء يقبل المنع كما إذا نهاك أحد حين تلاوتك بشيء بالنهي المذكور فلك أن تقول لا نسلم كون المقروء قرآنا أو كونى محدثا أو عدم جواز قراءة المحدث قرآنا، ولا يخفى أن كل واحد من هذه المنوع باعتبار الدعوى الضمنية ولا ينكره أحد.
And likewise all incomplete compounds. What is meant by compound here is the second meaning. If that is the case, then denial (man') is applicable to it absolutely, whether it is bare or with a support (sanad), provided it is not self-evident, clear, real, or judgmental. An example of an incomplete compound is when you say 'This world is always a Roman man,' for 'Roman' and the restriction 'always' are an incomplete compound of the predicate, and it is like saying 'This is Roman.' It was said that the totality of 'Roman' and 'man' and the restrictive relation between them is an incomplete compound and a restriction of the proposition because the proposition does not need that totality, and it is like your saying 'This is Roman.' Compare to the restriction of the subject and the relation. What is in it is not hidden because the restriction must be outside the proposition, so reflect. So the questioner may deny only his being Roman, i.e., only the Roman-ness of the referent absolutely, meaning without denying his humanity, and may deny only his being knowledgeable, and may deny only the perpetuity of the relation. It was said: the similar counterargument (naqd shabihi) or hypothetical counter-argument (mu'arada taqdiriya) — his speech is confused. I say this is from me due to heedlessness of what preceded from the author regarding the definition of usurpation (ghasb), and both are disobedience in his view, so there is no confusion. If the reason-giver proves his being Roman, or his being knowledgeable, or the perpetuity of the relation by a proof (dalil) or a hint (tanbih), then the questioner has three functions: either to deny a premise of that proof, or to counter it, or to refute it. The intelligent student is not unaware of that. And if the incomplete compound is not a restriction of the proposition, i.e., it is not a restriction, whether that compound is an additive compound like 'one slave of Zaid' (with sukun on 'ghulam' and jarr on 'Zaid'), or a numerical compound like 'fifteen', or other than that like 'a scholar man' and 'a jug of vinegar', or it is a restriction of an incomplete compound like 'slave of a scholar man', or it is a restriction for a performative (insha') like 'Do not recite the Quran while in a state of ritual impurity', then it is not objected to by any denial because it is not a propositional affirmation, so debate does not proceed in it like the single word and performative, except by refutation for violating the Arabic grammatical rule if it violates it. Objection on the basis of implicit claim, rather hypothetical counter-argument and general similar refutation are permissible. It was said: You have known that the compound that is a restriction for a performative admits denial, as when someone forbids you at the time of your recitation with the mentioned prohibition, so you may say 'We do not accept that what is recited is Quran, or that I am in a state of impurity, or the impermissibility of an impure person reciting the Quran,' and it is not hidden that each of these denials is on the basis of an implicit claim, and no one denies it.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about the incomplete compound (al-murakkab al-naqis) and how it is treated in munazara. The text begins by stating that the incomplete compound, in the sense that is the second meaning (not a complete proposition), is subject to denial (man') absolutely, unless it is self-evident or clear. An example is given: 'This world is always a Roman man.' The predicate 'Roman' together with the restriction 'always' forms an incomplete compound. The author then discusses whether the entire combination of 'Roman' and 'man' with their restrictive relation constitutes a restriction on the proposition. The conclusion is that the restriction must be external to the proposition, so the questioner may deny only the Roman-ness of the subject, without denying his humanity, and similarly may deny only his knowledge or the perpetuity of the relation. If the respondent provides a proof, the questioner has three options: deny a premise, counter the proof, or refute it. The text then distinguishes cases where the incomplete compound is not a restriction of the proposition. In such cases, denial is not allowed because the compound is not a truth-bearing statement; debate does not proceed with denial except by refutation (naqd) if the expression violates Arabic grammatical rules. The passage also allows hypothetical counter-argument and general similar refutation. Finally, it discusses the compound that is a restriction for a performative (insha'), showing that denial is possible based on implicit claims, as in the example of forbidding Quran recitation while in a state of impurity. This section sharpens the student's understanding of when objections can be raised in dialectical argument.

The Example: What Can Be Denied in 'This world is always a Roman man'
Presents an example of an incomplete compound and analyses what can be denied.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of denial to a concrete dialectical example and determine which parts of an incomplete compound can be denied.

Core Idea
You can deny the restriction but not the core subject or predicate.

A concrete example brings the rule to life. The sentence 'This world is always a Roman man' contains several restrictions. This lesson walks through what the questioner may deny and why, following the author's analysis.

Guided Path
1
Break down the example into its components
The statement is: 'This world is always a Roman man.' Identify the predicate: 'always a Roman man'. The incomplete compound here is the combination of 'Roman' and 'always' as a restriction of the predicate. Also, 'Roman' qualifies 'man'.
The base proposition is 'This world is a man' (subject + predicate). The additional words are restrictions.
2
Distinguish what is core and what is a restriction
The humanity (insaniyya) of the subject is part of the core predicate, not a restriction. The restrictions are: being Roman (rumiyya), being knowledgeable (alimiyya) – implied if the argument expands – and the perpetuity (dawam) of the relation. Only these restrictions can be denied.
The text says the questioner may deny only the Roman-ness, knowledge, or perpetuity, not the humanity.
3
Understand why the restriction must be external to the proposition
The author notes that the restriction must be outside the proposition (qayd karij 'an al-qadiyya). This means the incomplete compound is not part of the core truth-bearer; it is an additional qualifier. Thus, denying the restriction does not touch the core claim.
This is crucial: if the restriction were internal, denying it would change the proposition itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا سائر المركبات الناقصة، والمراد بالمركب هنا المعنى الثاني، وإذا كان كذلك فيرد عليه المنع مطلقا سواء كان مجردا أو مع السند مطلقا إذا لم يكن بديهيا جليا حقيقيا أو حكميا. مثال للمركب الناقص كأن تقول هذا العالم إنسان رومي دائما، فإن الرومي وقيد دائما مركب ناقص المحكوم به وهو بمنزلة قولنا هذا رومي. قيل فإن مجموع الرومي والإنسان والنسبة التقييدية بينهما مركب ناقص وقيد للقضية لعدم احتياجها إلى ذلك المجموع وهو بمنزلة قولك هذا رومي، وقس عليه قيد المحكوم عليه والنسبة. ولا يخفى ما فيه لأن القيد لابد أن يكون خارجا عن القضية فتأمل. فللسائل أن يمنع روميته فقط، أي رومية المشار إليه مطلقا فقط، يعني دون أن يمنع إنسانيته وأن يمنع عالميته فقط وأن يمنع دوام النسبة. قيل والنقض الشبيهي أو المعارضة التقديرية فكلامه مختل. أقول هذا مني عن الذهول عما سبق من المصنف من تعريف الغصب، وهما عنده عصيان فلا اختلال. فإن أثبت المعلل روميته أو عالميته أو دوام النسبة بدليل أو تنبيه، فللسائل حينئذ ثلاث وظائف: إما أن يمنع مقدمة من مقدمات ذلك الدليل أو يعارضه أو ينقضه. والمتفطن لا يخفى عليه ذلك. وأما إذا لم يكن المركب الناقص قيدا للقضية، بأن لا يكون قيدا، سواء كان ذلك المركب مركبا إضافيا كأن قال أحد غلام زيد، أو مركبا تعداديا كأن قال خمسة عشر، أو غير ذلك كرجل عالم وراقود خلا، أو يكون قيد المركب ناقص كأن قال غلام رجل عالم، أو يكون قيدا للإنشاء كأن قال لا تقرأ القرآن محدثا، فلا يعترض عليه بشيء من المنع لأنه ليس بتصديق معنى، فلا يجرى فيه المناظرة كالمفرد والإنشاء، إلا بالنقض مخالفة ذلك اللفظ القانون العربي إذا خالفه. والاعتراض باعتبار الدعوى الضمنية بل للمعارضة التقديرية والنقض الاجمالي الشبيهي مجوز بهما. قيل وقد عرفت أن المركب الذي كان قيدا للانشاء يقبل المنع كما إذا نهاك أحد حين تلاوتك بشيء بالنهي المذكور فلك أن تقول لا نسلم كون المقروء قرآنا أو كونى محدثا أو عدم جواز قراءة المحدث قرآنا، ولا يخفى أن كل واحد من هذه المنوع باعتبار الدعوى الضمنية ولا ينكره أحد.
And likewise all incomplete compounds. What is meant by compound here is the second meaning. If that is the case, then denial (man') is applicable to it absolutely, whether it is bare or with a support (sanad), provided it is not self-evident, clear, real, or judgmental. An example of an incomplete compound is when you say 'This world is always a Roman man,' for 'Roman' and the restriction 'always' are an incomplete compound of the predicate, and it is like saying 'This is Roman.' It was said that the totality of 'Roman' and 'man' and the restrictive relation between them is an incomplete compound and a restriction of the proposition because the proposition does not need that totality, and it is like your saying 'This is Roman.' Compare to the restriction of the subject and the relation. What is in it is not hidden because the restriction must be outside the proposition, so reflect. So the questioner may deny only his being Roman, i.e., only the Roman-ness of the referent absolutely, meaning without denying his humanity, and may deny only his being knowledgeable, and may deny only the perpetuity of the relation. It was said: the similar counterargument (naqd shabihi) or hypothetical counter-argument (mu'arada taqdiriya) — his speech is confused. I say this is from me due to heedlessness of what preceded from the author regarding the definition of usurpation (ghasb), and both are disobedience in his view, so there is no confusion. If the reason-giver proves his being Roman, or his being knowledgeable, or the perpetuity of the relation by a proof (dalil) or a hint (tanbih), then the questioner has three functions: either to deny a premise of that proof, or to counter it, or to refute it. The intelligent student is not unaware of that. And if the incomplete compound is not a restriction of the proposition, i.e., it is not a restriction, whether that compound is an additive compound like 'one slave of Zaid' (with sukun on 'ghulam' and jarr on 'Zaid'), or a numerical compound like 'fifteen', or other than that like 'a scholar man' and 'a jug of vinegar', or it is a restriction of an incomplete compound like 'slave of a scholar man', or it is a restriction for a performative (insha') like 'Do not recite the Quran while in a state of ritual impurity', then it is not objected to by any denial because it is not a propositional affirmation, so debate does not proceed in it like the single word and performative, except by refutation for violating the Arabic grammatical rule if it violates it. Objection on the basis of implicit claim, rather hypothetical counter-argument and general similar refutation are permissible. It was said: You have known that the compound that is a restriction for a performative admits denial, as when someone forbids you at the time of your recitation with the mentioned prohibition, so you may say 'We do not accept that what is recited is Quran, or that I am in a state of impurity, or the impermissibility of an impure person reciting the Quran,' and it is not hidden that each of these denials is on the basis of an implicit claim, and no one denies it.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about the incomplete compound (al-murakkab al-naqis) and how it is treated in munazara. The text begins by stating that the incomplete compound, in the sense that is the second meaning (not a complete proposition), is subject to denial (man') absolutely, unless it is self-evident or clear. An example is given: 'This world is always a Roman man.' The predicate 'Roman' together with the restriction 'always' forms an incomplete compound. The author then discusses whether the entire combination of 'Roman' and 'man' with their restrictive relation constitutes a restriction on the proposition. The conclusion is that the restriction must be external to the proposition, so the questioner may deny only the Roman-ness of the subject, without denying his humanity, and similarly may deny only his knowledge or the perpetuity of the relation. If the respondent provides a proof, the questioner has three options: deny a premise, counter the proof, or refute it. The text then distinguishes cases where the incomplete compound is not a restriction of the proposition. In such cases, denial is not allowed because the compound is not a truth-bearing statement; debate does not proceed with denial except by refutation (naqd) if the expression violates Arabic grammatical rules. The passage also allows hypothetical counter-argument and general similar refutation. Finally, it discusses the compound that is a restriction for a performative (insha'), showing that denial is possible based on implicit claims, as in the example of forbidding Quran recitation while in a state of impurity. This section sharpens the student's understanding of when objections can be raised in dialectical argument.

Key Terms
رومي
دائما
قيد
إنسانية
رومية
عالمية
دوام النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'This world is always a Roman man,' which of the following can the questioner deny?
A) The humanity (insaniyya) of the subject
B) The world being a man
C) The Roman-ness (rumiyya) of the subject
D) The fact that the statement is a proposition

Key Takeaway
The questioner may deny specific restrictions within an incomplete compound, but not the core subject or predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the statement 'The fasting person who travels is allowed to break the fast.' Identify the incomplete compound and determine which part(s) can be denied according to the rule.

The Three Functions of the Questioner When a Proof Is Provided
Explains the three options available to the questioner when the respondent proves a denied restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and apply the three functions of the questioner (deny a premise, counter-argument, refutation) in response to a proof supporting a denied restriction.

Core Idea
When a restriction is proved, the questioner must move from denying the restriction to attacking the proof itself.

If the respondent responds to a denial by providing a proof (dalil) or hint (tanbih), the questioner cannot simply repeat the denial. Instead, he must choose among three clean dialectical moves. This lesson clarifies those options.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the trigger: the respondent proves the denied restriction
The text assumes the respondent has provided evidence for Roman-ness, knowledge, or perpetuity. At this point, the original denial is no longer effective; the questioner must respond to the evidence.
The proof could be explicit (dalil) or implied (tanbih).
2
Understand the first function: deny a premise of that proof
The questioner can challenge the proof itself by denying one of its premises (man' muqaddama). This attacks the foundation of the respondent's argument.
This is different from the original denial of the restriction; it is a new denial directed at the supporting argument.
3
Understand the second function: counter-argument (mu'arada)
The questioner can produce a counter-argument that directly opposes the respondent's proof. This is a constructive move showing that the evidence is not decisive.
Mu'arada is a standard dialectical tool where the opponent offers an alternative reasoning that blocks the conclusion.
4
Understand the third function: refutation (naqd)
The questioner can refute by showing a flaw in the proof, e.g., a counterexample or logical inconsistency. This is a direct critique of the proof itself.
Naqd here includes similar counterargument (naqd shabihi) and general refutation.

Source Text

When Denial Is Not Allowed: Non-Restrictive Incomplete Compounds and Performatives
Lists cases where the incomplete compound is not a restriction and explains the limits of objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify incomplete compounds that are not restrictions of a proposition and know that denial is not allowed for them, except by refutation (naqd) for linguistic violations or by implicit claim.

Core Idea
If an incomplete compound is not a genuine restriction on a proposition, you cannot deny it—only refute it if the wording breaks Arabic rules.

Not all incomplete compounds are treated equally in debate. This lesson covers the cases where the compound is not a restriction of the proposition: additive, numerical, and other compounds, as well as restrictions on performatives. Understanding these limits prevents the misuse of denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify cases where the compound is not a restriction of the proposition
The author lists: additive compounds like 'one slave of Zaid' (مضاف), numerical compounds like 'fifteen', and others like 'a scholar man' and 'a jug of vinegar'. Also, compounds that are a restriction of another incomplete compound (e.g., 'slave of a scholar man').
These are not directly attached to the subject or predicate as qualifiers of a proposition.
2
Understand why denial is not allowed here
Because these compounds are not truth-bearing (ليس بتصديق معنى), they are like single words or performatives (insha'). Debate does not proceed with denial on such elements.
The core reason: a proposition (qadiyya) is required for truth evaluation; these compounds do not form or restrict a proposition directly.
3
Recognize the exception: refutation (naqd) for linguistic violation
Even though denial is not allowed, refutation (naqd) is permitted if the expression violates the rules of Arabic grammar (القانون العربي). This is a safeguard against ungrammatical formulations.
This exception shows that linguistic correctness is a legitimate object of debate.
4
Understand the role of implicit claim (da'wa dimniyya)
Objection on the basis of an implicit claim is allowed, including hypothetical counter-argument (mu'arada taqdiriya) and general similar refutation (naqd ijmali shabihi). This means that even if the compound is not a direct restriction, one can object by drawing out an implied claim.
The performative example: 'Do not recite the Quran while impure' – the questioner can deny the implicit claims that what is recited is Quran, that he is impure, or that it is impermissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

I say this is from me due to heedlessness of what preceded from the author regarding the definition of usurpation (ghasb), and both are disobedience in his view, so there is no confusion. If the reason-giver proves his being Roman, or his being knowledgeable, or the perpetuity of the relation by a proof (dalil) or a hint (tanbih), then the questioner has three functions: either to deny a premise of that proof, or to counter it, or to refute it. The intelligent student is not unaware of that. And if the incomplete compound is not a restriction of the proposition, i.e., it is not a restriction, whether that compound is an additive compound like 'one slave of Zaid' (with sukun on 'ghulam' and jarr on 'Zaid'), or a numerical compound like 'fifteen', or other than that like 'a scholar man' and 'a jug of vinegar', or it is a restriction of an incomplete compound like 'slave of a scholar man', or it is a restriction for a performative (insha') like 'Do not recite the Quran while in a state of ritual impurity', then it is not objected to by any denial because it is not a propositional affirmation, so debate does not proceed in it like the single word and performative, except by refutation for violating the Arabic grammatical rule if it violates it. Objection on the basis of implicit claim, rather hypothetical counter-argument and general similar refutation are permissible. It was said: You have known that the compound that is a restriction for a performative admits denial, as when someone forbids you at the time of your recitation with the mentioned prohibition, so you may say 'We do not accept that what is recited is Quran, or that I am in a state of impurity, or the impermissibility of an impure person reciting the Quran,' and it is not hidden that each of these denials is on the basis of an implicit claim, and no one denies it.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about the incomplete compound (al-murakkab al-naqis) and how it is treated in munazara. The text begins by stating that the incomplete compound, in the sense that is the second meaning (not a complete proposition), is subject to denial (man') absolutely, unless it is self-evident or clear. An example is given: 'This world is always a Roman man.' The predicate 'Roman' together with the restriction 'always' forms an incomplete compound. The author then discusses whether the entire combination of 'Roman' and 'man' with their restrictive relation constitutes a restriction on the proposition. The conclusion is that the restriction must be external to the proposition, so the questioner may deny only the Roman-ness of the subject, without denying his humanity, and similarly may deny only his knowledge or the perpetuity of the relation. If the respondent provides a proof, the questioner has three options: deny a premise, counter the proof, or refute it. The text then distinguishes cases where the incomplete compound is not a restriction of the proposition. In such cases, denial is not allowed because the compound is not a truth-bearing statement; debate does not proceed with denial except by refutation (naqd) if the expression violates Arabic grammatical rules. The passage also allows hypothetical counter-argument and general similar refutation. Finally, it discusses the compound that is a restriction for a performative (insha'), showing that denial is possible based on implicit claims, as in the example of forbidding Quran recitation while in a state of impurity. This section sharpens the student's understanding of when objections can be raised in dialectical argument.

Key Terms
مركب إضافي
مركب تعدادي
قيد المركب الناقص
الإنشاء
الدعوى الضمنية
المعارضة التقديرية
النقض الاجمالي الشبيهي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is denial (man') not allowed on additive compounds like 'one slave of Zaid'?
A) Because such compounds are always self-evident
B) Because they are not propositional affirmations (ليس بتصديق معنى)
C) Because they are too short to be debated
D) Because they are used only in performative speech

Key Takeaway
If an incomplete compound is not a restriction of a proposition, denial is not allowed; objections are limited to linguistic refutation and implicit claims.

Types of Answers in Dialectic: Tahqiqi and Ilzami
Classifies answers in dialectic into veritative (tahqiqi) and obligatory (ilzami) based on the opponent's acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a tahqiqi answer and an ilzami answer in a dialectical exchange and identify the purpose of each.

Core Idea
In dialectic, truth and compulsion are two different goals: tahqiqi aims at truth, ilzami aims at victory.

In classical Islamic dialectic (jadal), not every answer aims to establish truth. Some answers are strategic: they use the opponent's own premises to compel him, even if the respondent knows those premises are false. This lesson unpacks the two main types of answers—tahqiqi and ilzami—and their conditions of acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for an answer to be heard
The text states that the support (sanad) and premises must apparently be accepted by one of the two opponents; otherwise the answer is not heard. This is the gateway for any answer in jadal.
2
Distinguish tahqiqi from ilzami
A tahqiqi answer is based on premises that the respondent himself accepts, regardless of the questioner's view. An ilzami answer is built on premises that the questioner accepts, even if the respondent believes them false.
The difference lies in whose acceptance grounds the answer.
3
Examine the purpose of ilzami answers
The text explicitly says the purpose of an ilzami answer is not to manifest truth but only to compel the opponent (إلزام الخصم فقط). It is a dialectical move aimed at victory, not truth.
This distinguishes it from tahqiqi, which pursues truth.
4
Apply the classification to a sample exchange
Given a short dialectical exchange, determine whether each answer is tahqiqi or ilzami based on whether the respondent uses his own accepted premises or the questioner's accepted premises.
Practice with examples from kalam or fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان الأجوبة المقبولة وغيرها: اعلم أن السند ومقدمات الأدلة والتنبيهات لابد أن تكون مسلمة في الظاهر عند أحد الخصمين وإلا فلا يكون الجواب مسموعا. فإذا أجاب المعلل أو المعرف أو القاسم بجواب مسلم عنده فذلك الجواب جواب تحقيقي وإن لم يكن صحيحا عند السائل ولم يكن صحيحا في نفس الأمر. وأما إذا أجاب المعلل عن اعتراض السائل بجواب مبني على ما سلمه السائل بأن يثبت المعلل ما منعه السائل من المدعى أو المقدمة بدليل مشتمل على مقدمة مسلمة عند السائل مع علم المعلل واعتقاده بأن الكلام الذي سلمه السائل باطل وإن لم يكن باطلا في نفس الأمر، فذلك الجواب جواب إلزامي جدلي لا جواب تحقيقي. وليس الغرض منه إظهار الحق والصواب بل إلزام الخصم فقط. وكذا يدفع المعلل أو القاسم أو المعرف كلام الناقض أو المعارض مستدلا أو مستندا بمقدمة بمنع ذلك، وكذا يكون جوابا جدليا.
A chapter on explaining acceptable answers and others: Know that the support (sanad) and the premises of proofs and hints must apparently be accepted by one of the two opponents; otherwise the answer is not heard. So if the reason-giver (mu'allil) or the definer (mu'arif) or the divider (qasim) answers with an answer that is accepted by him, that answer is a veritative (tahqiqi) answer, even if it is not correct according to the questioner and not correct in reality. And if the reason-giver answers the questioner's objection with an answer built on what the questioner accepted, by proving what the questioner denied from the claim or the premise with a proof that includes a premise accepted by the questioner, while the reason-giver knows and believes that the statement which the questioner accepted is false, even if it is not false in reality, then that answer is an obligatory dialectical answer (ilzami jadali), not a veritative answer. Its purpose is not to manifest truth and correctness but only to compel the opponent. Similarly, the reason-giver or the divider or the definer refutes the speech of the refuter or the objector by arguing or relying on a premise with a denial of that, and likewise it is a dialectical answer.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a classification of answers in dialectic (jadal) into two types: tahqiqi (veritative) and ilzami (obligatory). The text begins by stating a condition for any answer to be acceptable: the support (sanad) and the premises of proofs must be apparently accepted by one of the two opponents; otherwise the answer is not heard. The first type, tahqiqi, occurs when the respondent (mu'allil, mu'arif, or qasim) gives an answer that is accepted by him (the respondent), regardless of whether it is true for the questioner or in reality. The second type, ilzami, occurs when the respondent builds his answer on premises that the questioner has accepted, even though the respondent himself knows those premises are false. The purpose of an ilzami answer is not to reveal truth but to compel the opponent. The text also notes that similar dialectical answers occur when the respondent refutes the objection using a premise that the opponent accepts. This distinction is crucial in dialectical training: a student must know when an answer is meant to establish truth (tahqiqi) and when it is a strategic move to silence the opponent (ilzami).

Key Terms
جواب تحقيقي
جواب إلزامي
السند

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main purpose of an ilzami (obligatory) answer?
A) To demonstrate absolute truth
B) To compel the opponent based on his own premises
C) To reject all premises
D) To provide a new proof

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, answers are classified by their basis: tahqiqi when the respondent accepts the premise, ilzami when it is based on the opponent's acceptance, aiming to compel rather than reveal truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a dispute: The questioner claims 'Prayer is not obligatory for a traveller.' The respondent says: 'But you accept that the Prophet (peace be upon him) shortened prayer while travelling, and you accept that the Prophet's actions are a source of obligation. Therefore prayer is obligatory for a traveller.' Is this a tahqiqi or ilzami answer? Justify your answer based on the criteria from the passage.
?
Create your own short dialectical exchange on a topic from fiqh (e.g., riba or zakat) where the respondent gives a tahqiqi answer. Then modify the exchange so that the answer becomes ilzami, showing how the basis of acceptance shifts.

Investigative vs. Compelling Answers in Dialectic
Author classifies answers in dialectic into investigative and compelling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an investigative answer (tahqiqi) and a compelling answer (ilzami) based on the answerer's belief and the opponent's admission of premises.

Core Idea
Truth of an answer is measured by the answerer's conviction, not by reality.

In formal debate (jadal), not all answers aim to reveal truth. Some aim only to compel the opponent. This lesson unpacks the two main categories of answers defined by classical dialecticians: investigative (based on what the answerer truly believes) and compelling (based on premises the opponent admits, even if the answerer considers them false). Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating debates in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of answers
Find the terms 'jawāb taḥqīqī' and 'jawāb ilzāmī' in the passage. Note that both are forms of response in dialectic, but they differ fundamentally in basis.
The passage contrasts these terms explicitly.
2
Determine the criterion for each type
For a tahqiqi answer, the answerer believes the premises are true and builds his answer on his own conviction. For an ilzami answer, the answerer uses premises that the opponent admits, even if the answerer himself believes they are false.
The passage says: 'بناها على ما علم واعتقد حقيته' for tahqiqi, and 'مبنى على ما سلمه السائل' for ilzami.
3
Recognize the purpose difference
A tahqiqi answer seeks to manifest truth, while an ilzami answer aims only to silence or compel the opponent, not to reveal truth. The passage explicitly states: 'ليس الغرض منه إظهار الحق والصواب بل إلزام الخصم فقط.'
This purpose distinction is crucial in evaluating the ethical use of arguments.
4
Apply to a sample scenario
Consider a debate about whether the world is created. If the answerer uses the premise 'every change has a cause' because he truly believes it, the answer is tahqiqi. If he uses a premise that the opponent accepts (e.g., 'the Quran says X') even though he doubts it, the answer is ilzami.
This shows how the same conclusion can be reached via different answer types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان الأجوبة المقبولة وغيرها : اعلم أن السند ومقدمات الأدلة والتنبيهات لابد أن تكون مسلمة فى الظاهر عند أحد الخصمين وإلا فلا يكون الجواب مسموعا . وإذا أجاب المعلل أو المعرف أو القاسم بجواب مسلم عنده فذلك الجواب جواب تحقيقي وإن لم يكن صحيحا عند السائل ولم يكن صحيحا في نفس الأمر. وأما إذا أجاب المعلل عن اعتراض السائل بجواب مبنى على ما سلمه السائل بأن يثبت المعلل ما منعه السائل من المدعى أو المقدمة بدليل مشتمل على مقدمة مسلمة عند السائل مع علم المعلل واعتقاده بأن الكلام الذي سلمه السائل باطل وإن لم يكن باطلا في نفس الأمر، فذلك الجواب جواب إلزامي جدلي لا جواب تحقيقي، ليس الغرض منه إظهار الحق والصواب بل إلزام الخصم فقط. والجدل هو المدافعة لإسكات الخصم أو لإظهار الفضل لا لإظهار الحق. فلا ينبغي للمعلل والمعرف والقاسم أن يجيب بمثل ذلك الجواب الجدلي في كل وقت من الأوقات إلا إذا كان الخصم متعنتا أي طالبا لزلة المعلل أو المعرف أو القاسم لا طالبا لإظهار الحق والصواب، فإن الخصم حينئذ متكبر والتكبر على المتكبر صدقة. قال في الخلاصة: التمويه والحيلة في المناظرة أن تكلم متعلما مسترشدا أو تكلم على الإنصاف بلا تعنت يكره، وكذا إذا تكلم غير مسترشد لكن على الإنصاف بلا تعنت، فإن الحيلة مع من يريد التعنت ويريد أن يطرحه لا يكره ويحتال كل حيلة ليدفع عن نفسه لأن الحيلة لدفع التعنت مشروعة. والجواب التحقيقي هو الذي بناه المعلل أو المعرف أو القاسم على ما علم واعتقد حقيته وإن لم يكن حقا. والحاصل أن المجيب إن اعتقد صحة جوابه فجوابه تحقيقي وإن لم يصح في نفس الأمر، وإلا فجدلي وإن صح، ويسمى أيضا إلزاميا إن سلمه السائل.
A chapter on explaining acceptable answers and others: Know that the foundational chain and premises of proofs and reminders must be outwardly admitted by one of the two opponents; otherwise the answer is not heard. If the explainer, definer, or classifier answers with an answer admitted by him, that answer is an investigative answer, even if it is not correct according to the questioner and not correct in reality. But if the explainer answers the questioner's objection with an answer based on what the questioner admitted, by the explainer proving what the questioner denied—whether the claim or the premise—with a proof containing a premise admitted by the questioner, while the explainer knows and believes that the statement which the questioner admitted is false, even if it is not false in reality, then that answer is a compelling dialectical answer, not an investigative answer; its purpose is not to manifest truth and correctness but only to compel the opponent. Dialectic is defense to silence the opponent or to manifest superiority, not to manifest truth. Therefore, it is not proper for the explainer, definer, or classifier to answer with such a dialectical answer at all times, except when the opponent is obstinate—i.e., seeking the explainer’s slip, not seeking the manifestation of truth and correctness. For such an opponent is arrogant, and arrogance toward the arrogant is charity. It is said in al-Khulasa: Deception and trickery in disputation—if you speak as a learner seeking guidance, or speak fairly without obstinacy, it is disliked; likewise if you speak without seeking guidance but fairly without obstinacy. But trickery with one who wants obstinacy and wants to throw you is not disliked; he uses every trick to defend himself, because trickery to repel obstinacy is legitimate. The investigative answer is the one that the explainer, definer, or classifier built on what he knew and believed to be true, even if it is not true. In sum, if the answerer believes his answer is correct, his answer is investigative even if it is not correct in reality; otherwise it is dialectical even if correct, and it is also called compelling if the questioner admits it.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage classifies answers in dialectical debate (jadal) into two main types: investigative (tahqiqi) and compelling (ilzami). It begins by stating a foundational rule: the premises and proofs must appear to be admitted by one of the two opponents; otherwise the answer is not acceptable. If the answerer gives an answer that he himself admits to be true, that is an investigative answer, regardless of its actual truth or the questioner's acceptance. If the answerer builds his answer on premises that the questioner admits, but the answerer himself believes those premises to be false (even if they are not actually false), then it is a compelling dialectical answer. The purpose of such an answer is not to manifest truth but only to compel the opponent. The passage then defines dialectic as defense aimed at silencing the opponent or showing superiority, not at revealing truth. It advises that such dialectical answers should not be used at all times, only when the opponent is obstinate (mu'annit), i.e., seeking the answerer's error rather than truth. In that case, arrogance toward the arrogant is charity. The text cites al-Khulasa to clarify that deception (tamwih) and trickery (hila) are disliked in fair discourse, but are permissible against an obstinate opponent who intends to harm. Finally, it reiterates that the investigative answer is based on what the answerer believes to be true; if he believes it correct, it is investigative even if false; otherwise it is dialectical, and also called compelling if the questioner admits it.

Key Terms
جواب تحقيقي
جواب إلزامي
جدل
متعنت
حيلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a dialectical answer (ilzami) permissible?
A) At all times, because it is effective.
B) Only when the opponent is obstinate (muʿannit).
C) Only if the answerer believes the premises are true.
D) Only if the questioner admits the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The classification of answers hinges on the answerer's belief: investigative answers are built on personal conviction, while compelling answers exploit the opponent's admissions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following answer as investigative or compelling: In a debate on the permissibility of music, the answerer says: 'You admit that the Quran does not explicitly prohibit music; therefore it is permissible.' The answerer does not believe this argument is sound but uses it to silence the opponent.

Permissibility of Trickery Against the Obstinate Opponent
Author cites al-Khulasa on permissibility of trickery against obstinate opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when it is permissible to use trickery (hila) in debate based on the opponent's intention as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Trickery is charity against arrogance, but only against the obstinate.

The passage does not stop at classifying answers; it also provides ethical guidance. Citing al-Khulasa, it addresses the controversial tool of deception (tamwih) and trickery (hila) in disputation. This lesson unpacks the conditions under which such tactics are permissible, focusing on the opponent's character and intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on trickery in general
The passage states that tamwih and hila are disliked (yukrah) when speaking as a learner or when speaking fairly without obstinacy. Note the baseline: deception in debate is generally disapproved.
The source says 'يكره' (it is disliked) for these cases.
2
Identify the exception for obstinate opponents
The exception is when the opponent (mu'annit) is obstinate, seeking the answerer's slip. In that case, trickery is not disliked (la yukrah). The answerer may use every trick to defend himself.
The passage says: 'فإن الحيلة مع من يريد التعنت ويريد أن يطرحه لا يكره'.
3
Understand the rationale
The passage calls the obstinate opponent 'mutakabbir' (arrogant) and says 'al-takabbur 'ala al-mutakabbir sadaqah' (arrogance toward the arrogant is charity). Thus, using trickery against such an opponent is a form of resistance to injustice.
This provides a moral justification for what might otherwise be considered unethical.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Imagine a debate where the opponent clearly does not want to learn the truth but only wants to embarrass the answerer. According to the passage, the answerer may use rhetorical tricks or dialectical answers (ilzami) without guilt.
This does not allow dishonesty in all contexts; only in specific adversarial situations.

A chapter on explaining acceptable answers and others: Know that the foundational chain and premises of proofs and reminders must be outwardly admitted by one of the two opponents; otherwise the answer is not heard. If the explainer, definer, or classifier answers with an answer admitted by him, that answer is an investigative answer, even if it is not correct according to the questioner and not correct in reality. Therefore, it is not proper for the explainer, definer, or classifier to answer with such a dialectical answer at all times, except when the opponent is obstinate—i.e., seeking the explainer’s slip, not seeking the manifestation of truth and correctness. For such an opponent is arrogant, and arrogance toward the arrogant is charity.
