This passage classifies answers in dialectical debate (jadal) into two main types: investigative (tahqiqi) and compelling (ilzami). It begins by stating a foundational rule: the premises and proofs must appear to be admitted by one of the two opponents; otherwise the answer is not acceptable. If the answerer gives an answer that he himself admits to be true, that is an investigative answer, regardless of its actual truth or the questioner's acceptance. If the answerer builds his answer on premises that the questioner admits, but the answerer himself believes those premises to be false (even if they are not actually false), then it is a compelling dialectical answer. The purpose of such an answer is not to manifest truth but only to compel the opponent. The passage then defines dialectic as defense aimed at silencing the opponent or showing superiority, not at revealing truth. It advises that such dialectical answers should not be used at all times, only when the opponent is obstinate (mu'annit), i.e., seeking the answerer's error rather than truth. In that case, arrogance toward the arrogant is charity. The text cites al-Khulasa to clarify that deception (tamwih) and trickery (hila) are disliked in fair discourse, but are permissible against an obstinate opponent who intends to harm. Finally, it reiterates that the investigative answer is based on what the answerer believes to be true; if he believes it correct, it is investigative even if false; otherwise it is dialectical, and also called compelling if the questioner admits it.

Key Terms
تمويه
حيلة
متعنت
جدل
صدقة

A) When speaking as a learner seeking guidance.
B) When speaking fairly without obstinacy.
C) When the opponent is obstinate and wants to harm the speaker.
D) When the speaker is not seeking guidance but is fair.

Key Takeaway
Trickery in debate is only permissible against an opponent who is bent on obstruction, not in good-faith discussions.

The answerer uses this to argue that human free will must exist, even though the answerer believes evil can be attributed to Allah in a certain sense. Is this hila permissible? Justify using the passage.
?
Construct a response to an obstinate opponent who denies the need for revelation, using a compelling (ilzami) argument that the opponent would admit, without compromising your own beliefs.

Distinguishing Tahqiq and Jadal in Questions and Answers
Author distinguishes tahqiq from jadal in question and answer types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a question or answer as tahqiq or jadal based on the respondent's intention and the opponent's attitude.

Core Idea
Tahqiq is sincere belief; jadal is strategic disputation.

In Islamic dialectic, not every question or answer seeks truth. This passage introduces two fundamental categories: verification (tahqiq) and disputation (jadal). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the epistemic status of arguments in a debate and for determining when defensive rhetorical tactics are permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of question and answer
Read the passage and note the four terms: سؤال تحقيق (verification question), سؤال جدلى (dialectical question), جواب تحقيق (verification answer), جواب جدلى (dialectical answer). Write them down.
2
Define jadal by its purpose
The text says jadal is disputation to silence the opponent or show superiority, not to manifest truth. Highlight the phrase 'لإسكات الخصم أو لإظهار الفضل لا لإظهار الحق'.
Jadal is about defeating the opponent, not discovering truth.
3
Define tahqiq by the respondent's belief
Tahqiq is defined as an answer built on what the respondent knows and believes to be true, even if it is not actually true. Find the phrase 'بناه المعلل على ما علم واعتقد حقيقته وإن لم يكن حقا'.
Belief is the criterion, not objective correctness.
4
Apply the distinction to a scenario
Consider a scholar asked about a ruling by an obstinate opponent seeking a slip. The scholar gives an answer he does not personally believe but that silences the opponent. Classify this as jadal or tahqiq. The passage allows jadal against an obstinate opponent.
This illustrates the ethical boundary: jadal is only permissible against the muʿannit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن كلا من السؤال والجواب على قسمين سؤال تحقيق وسؤال جدلى وجواب تحقيق وجواب جدلى، والجدل، وهو المدافعة لإسكات الخصم أو لإظهار الفضل لا لإظهار الحق، ففرض المناظر إظهار الصواب وغرض المجادل حفظ للدم ودفع الخصم وإلزامه، فقواعد الجدل لعلها حيل ومغالطات لا ينبغى أن يقابل بها إلا الخصم للتعنت. فلا ينبغى للمعلل والمعرف والقاسم أن يجيب بمثل ذلك الجواب الجدلى فى كل وقت من الأوقات إلا إذا كان الخصم متعنتا أي طالبا لزلة المعلل أو المعرف أو القاسم لا طالبا لإظهار الحق والصواب فان الخصم حينئذ متكبر والتكبر على المتكبر صدقة. والجواب التحقيق هو الجواب الذي بناه المعلل أو المعرف أو القاسم على ما علم واعتقد حقيته وإن لم يكن حقا. والحاصل أن المجيب إن اعتقد صحة جوابه جوابه تحقيق وإن لم يصح وإلا فجدلى وإن صحح، ويسمى أيضا إلزاميا إن سلمه السائل، وكذا السؤال بلا فرق.
The summary is that both question and answer are of two types: a verification question and a dialectical question, and a verification answer and a dialectical answer. Dialectic is disputation to silence the opponent or to show superiority, not to manifest the truth. The duty of the disputant is to manifest correctness, while the aim of the dialectician is to protect blood, repel the opponent, and bind him. So the rules of dialectic are perhaps tricks and fallacies that should only be faced against an obstinate opponent. So it is not befitting for the reason-giver, the definer, and the divider to answer with such a dialectical answer at all times except when the opponent is obstinate, i.e., seeking a slip of the reason-giver, definer, or divider, not seeking to manifest truth and correctness, for then the opponent is arrogant, and arrogance against the arrogant is charity. The verification answer is the answer that the reason-giver, definer, or divider built upon what he knew and believed to be true, even if it is not true. The summary is that the respondent, if he believes his answer is correct, it is a verification answer even if it is not correct, otherwise it is dialectical even if correct, and it is also called binding if the questioner accepts it, and likewise the question without difference.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage classifies questions and answers in disputation (jadal) into two categories: tahqiq (verification) and jadal (dialectical). The author defines jadal as disputation aimed at silencing the opponent or displaying superiority, not at revealing truth. The role of a munāẓir (disputant seeking truth) is to show correctness, while a mujādil (dialectician) aims to protect his position and compel the opponent. The rules of jadal are described as tricks and fallacies, which should only be used against an obstinate opponent (muʿannit) who seeks to embarrass the reason-giver (muʿallil) rather than seek truth. The author cites the principle 'arrogance against the arrogant is charity' to justify the use of such tactics. A tahqiq answer is defined as one that the respondent sincerely believes to be true, even if it is objectively false. If the respondent believes his answer is correct, it is tahqiq; otherwise it is jadal, even if the answer is actually correct. A jadal answer may become 'binding' (ilzāmī) if the questioner accepts it. This classification applies to both questions and answers without difference. In Islamic debate tradition, this distinction is crucial: tahqiq is the ideal for genuine seekers of truth, while jadal is a legitimate defensive tool against obstinate opponents.

The distinction between tahqiq and jadal hinges on the respondent's belief, not the objective truth of the answer.

Binding and Retraction in Dialectical Exchange
Explains binding by silence and conditions for retracting a concession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a questioner becomes bound by a premise and when retraction of a concession is permitted in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Silence binds the questioner, but retraction is allowed except for self-evident or school-necessary truths.

In dialectical argument (jadal), the binding of a questioner (ilzām) occurs when he fails to object to a premise he previously accepted. However, the rules allow him to retract his concession under certain conditions. This lesson unpacks the author's explanation of these rules and the famous principle that 'the questioner has no school,' granting flexibility in objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two triggers for binding
First, when the questioner remains silent after hearing the premise — his silence indicates confusion and inability to object. Second, when the answer includes a premise that the questioner had accepted at other times (i.e., not just in this session).
Both cases lead to binding; silence is not the only path.
2
Understand when retraction is allowed
The questioner may retract a previous concession only if he is qualified (ahl) and if the conceded point is not self-evident (badīhī jalī) nor among the necessary tenets of his own school (ḍarūriyyāt al-madhhab).
Qualification means he has the knowledge to plausibly claim hesitation.
3
Apply the 'no school' maxim
The author quotes a maxim: 'The questioner has no school.' This means in the context of objection, the questioner can adopt any school's position that helps his case, because he is not defending a fixed position but challenging the reason-giver.
This maxim prevents the reason-giver from requiring the questioner to be consistent with a specific madhhab.
4
Test the limits of retraction
Even with this flexibility, the questioner cannot retract what is self-evident (e.g., 'the whole is greater than a part') or what is integral to his own school (e.g., a Shāfiʿī cannot deny that the basmala is part of the Fātiḥa in prayer).
This preserves the integrity of basic rational and doctrinal commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحصل له (أى للسائل) الإلزام لأن سكوت السائل يدل على أن تلك المقدمة اضطرب السائل قبولها فعجز عن الاعتراض فصل له الإلزام كما حصل الإلزام إذا لم يكن الجواب مشتملا على مقدمة مسلمة في سائر الأوقات. فإن لم يسكت السائل بأن منع السائل ما سلمه من قبل (أى قبل إثبات المعلل) فله ذلك المنع إذا كان أهلا له إذ يجوز له أن يدعى التردد فيما سلمه بعد الجزم به مالم يكن ما سلمه بديهيا جليا أو من ضروريات مذهبه. ولذا قيل إن لمانع لا مذهب له أى لا مذهب له معين فيذهب في مقام المنع أي مذهب يشاء ويختار ما هو أحرى بحاله وأليق بقاله.
Binding occurs for (i.e., for the questioner) because the silence of the questioner indicates that he was confused about accepting that premise and was unable to object, so binding applies to him, just as binding occurs if the answer does not include a premise accepted by the questioner at other times. If the questioner is not silent, i.e., he retracts what he had previously conceded (i.e., before the reason-giver's proof), then he may do so if he is qualified for it, because it is permissible for him to claim hesitation in what he conceded after being certain of it, as long as what he conceded was not self-evident or among the necessary tenets of his school. Hence it is said: 'The questioner has no school,' i.e., no fixed school, so he may adopt in the context of objection whichever school he wishes and choose what is more fitting for his state and more appropriate for his speech.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage explains the mechanism of binding (ilzām) in dialectical exchange. The author states that when a reason-giver (muʿallil) presents a proof that includes a premise the questioner had previously accepted, and the questioner falls silent, that silence indicates his inability to object, and thus he becomes bound (mulzam). The same binding occurs if the answer does not involve a premise accepted by the questioner at the time. However, if the questioner does not remain silent but instead retracts his earlier concession, he is allowed to do so provided he is qualified (ahl). He can claim that he was hesitant (taraddud) in that concession, even if he was previously certain, as long as the conceded point was not self-evident (badīhī jalī) or from the necessary tenets of his own madhhab. The author adds a maxim: 'The questioner has no school,' meaning the questioner can adopt any school's position in his objection, choosing what suits his argument. This illustrates the flexibility allowed to the questioner to avoid being bound, but only within limits: he cannot retract what is self-evident or integral to his own school. In Islamic dialectical practice, this prevents clever opponents from escaping valid arguments through arbitrary retraction.

Key Terms
إلزام
بديهي جلي
ضروريات المذهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A questioner's retraction of a concession is allowed only if the concession is not self-evident and not from his school's necessary tenets; otherwise, silence or acceptance binds him.

Going Along with the Opponent (Mujarat al-Khasm)
Author explains the tactic of accepting an opponent's premise to refute his argument with Quranic example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the dialectical tactic of mujārāt al-khaṣm and apply it to a Quranic example.

Core Idea
Accept the undeniable premise, deny the correlation—silence the opponent without a new proof.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), not every argument requires a fresh proof. Sometimes the most effective response is to concede an undeniable premise but block the conclusion by denying the claimed correlation. This lesson unpacks that tactic, known as mujārāt al-khaṣm, and shows how the prophets used it in the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Find what the opponent asserts as a necessary connection (mulāzama) between a premise (malzūm) and a conclusion (lāzim). In the Quranic example, the disbelievers claim that being human (malzūm) implies not being a prophet (lāzim).
The malzūm must be something the respondent cannot reasonably deny.
2
Determine the undeniable premise
The respondent accepts the malzūm because it is obviously true or cannot be refuted. Here, the messengers accept that they are human.
This acceptance is strategic, not a concession of the whole argument.
3
Formulate the response by denying the correlation
Instead of attacking the premise, the respondent denies that the claimed necessary connection (mulāzama) holds. The messengers affirm their humanity but reject that it precludes prophethood.
The denial of mulāzama is the core of the tactic.
4
Recognize the rhetorical power
The text states that this method is more effective in confounding the opponent than providing an independent proof. It silences the opponent without engaging in a lengthy refutation.
This is a high-level dialectical move, not a sign of weakness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه) قال بعض الأفاضل: مجاراة الخصم عبارة عن المنع مع التسليم المخصوص، ويسمى أيضا إرخاء العنان، وتوضيحه أن السائل يزعم ثبوت ملازمة مع أن الملزوم ما لا مجال للمعلل أن ينكره واللازم يناقض دعوى المعلل فيعارض المعلل بواسطة تلك الملازمة مع ثبوت الملزوم فجواب المعلل عنه بمنع الملازمة مع تسليم ثبوت الملزوم مجاراة الخصم، وهذا أشد تأثيرا في تبكيت الخصم وإسكاته من إثبات مدعاه بدليل آخر وهو ظاهر، ومنه قوله تعالى حكاية عن الرسل (إن نحن إلا بشر مثلكم ولكن الله يمن على من يشاء من عباده) فكأن الكفار توهموا أن البشرية تستلزم عدم الرسالة، بل لا يكون الرسل إلا من الملائكة فعارضوا دعوى الرسالة بقولهم: إن أتم إلا بشر مثلنا، فأجابهم الرسل على سبيل المجاراة.
Notification: Some scholars said: Going along with the opponent (mujārāt al-khaṣm) is the act of prevention with a specific acceptance, and it is also called giving free rein (irkhāʾ al-ʿinān). Its explanation is that the questioner claims the existence of a correlation (mulāzama) while the correlated ground (malzūm) is something the reason-giver cannot deny, and the correlate (lāzim) contradicts the reason-giver's claim; so the reason-giver opposes through that correlation with the existence of the ground, and the reason-giver's response is to deny the correlation while accepting the existence of the ground. This is more effective in confounding and silencing the opponent than proving his own claim with another proof, and that is obvious. An example of this is the saying of Allah Almighty, narrating from the messengers: 'We are but men like you, but Allah bestows His favor upon whom He wills of His servants.' It is as if the disbelievers assumed that humanity necessitates absence of prophethood—indeed, they thought messengers could only be from angels—so they opposed the claim of prophethood by saying: 'You are but men like us.' So the messengers answered them by way of going along.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage explains the dialectical tactic known as mujārāt al-khaṣm (going along with the opponent) or irkhāʾ al-ʿinān (giving free rein). The author describes a scenario where the opponent (sāʾil) claims a correlation (mulāzama) between a premise (malzūm) that the respondent (muʿallil) cannot deny and a conclusion (lāzim) that contradicts the respondent's own claim. The respondent's strategy is to accept the premise but deny the correlation, thereby avoiding the damaging conclusion while not disputing an undeniable fact. This is said to be more effective in silencing the opponent than providing an independent proof. The author illustrates this with the Quranic example of the messengers' response to disbelievers (Quran 14:11). The disbelievers argued: 'You are but men like us,' implying that humans cannot be prophets; the messengers conceded they are human but denied the correlation, noting that Allah chooses whom He wills. This demonstrates the tactic of accepting the opponent's ground but rejecting the inferred implication. In Islamic dialectic, this method is valued for its rhetorical and argumentative power.

Key Terms
مجاراة الخصم
ملازمة
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) It is a sign of weakness

Key Takeaway
Mujārāt al-khaṣm allows a debater to accept an undeniable premise while denying the inference, thereby refuting the opponent without conceding the argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Two Stances of a Transmitter in Debate
Explains the two options for a transmitter and their consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a transmitter who commits to the truth of the transmitted content and one who does not, and identify the only valid objection against the latter.

Core Idea
If you don't commit to its truth, only the transmission itself can be challenged.

In dialectical debate, when citing a source, you must clarify your stance: are you vouching for its truth or just reporting? The Waldiyya commentary explains that your level of commitment determines what objections your opponent may raise. This lesson unpacks that foundational distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transmitter's stance
Determine whether the debater expresses commitment to the correctness (sihha) of the transmitted statement. The key phrase is 'iltizam al-sihha'.
2
If no commitment, only one objection
If the transmitter does not commit to correctness in wording or meaning, the opponent can only demand verification of the transmission (talab tashih al-naql). This is called man‘ al-naql.
The opponent must prove the transmission itself, not the content.
3
If commitment, full range of objections
If the transmitter commits to the truth of the meaning, then the opponent can raise denial (man‘) and discussion (munaqasha) as per the relevant chapter (definition, division, or assent).
The type of objection depends on what was transmitted.
4
Exception for self-evident or obligatory matters
If the transmitted content is divinely revealed, prophetic, consensual, or self-evident, no objection is valid even if commitment is made.
This protects foundational truths from dialectical challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في بيان المناظرة على تقدير النقل سواء كان نقل تعريف أو تقسيم أو تصديق مطلقا أو غيرها. إن كنت ناقلا فإما أن تلتزم صحة المنقول أو لم تلتزم. فان لم تلتزم صحة المنقول لا لفظا ولا معنى سواء كان ذلك المنقول مفردا أو إنشاء أو مركبا ناقصا أو تعريفا أو تقسيما أو تصديقا فلا يرد عليك شيء إلا طلب تصحيح النقل أى بيان صحته إذ لم تكن صحته بديهيا جليا أو معلوما أو مسلما عند الطالب أو من ضروريات مذهبه. وهذا معنى منع النقل. وإذا ورد عليك طلب النقل فلك أن تثبت نقلك باحضار كتاب ما نقلته منه مثلا إذ قد يكون ما نقلته عنه شخصا فتحضره. وإن التزمت صحته معنى أى مطابقة نسبة المنقول للواقع وذا لا يتصور في المفرد لعدم النسبة فيه ولا في الانشاء إذ نسبته لا تحتمل المطابقة ولا في المركب الناقص الذي ليس بقيد للمركب التام لعدم المطابقة في نسبته بخلاف المركب الناقص الذى هو قيد له والتعريف والتقسيم والتصديق. فيرد عليك الأبحاث السابقة من المنع والمناقشة وأجوبتها في باب التعريف إن كان التعريف وفي باب التقسيم إن كان التقسيم وفي باب التصديق إن كان التصديق إلا أن يجب الإيمان به وهو قول الله وقول انبيائه والممكن الذي أجمع المسلمون عليه وكذا ما استيقن بعقله من أمور الدين أو يكون بديهيا جليا أو معلوما أو مسلما عند السائل. وأما إن التزمت صحة لفظ المنقول وهذا الالتزام في كل ما له نسبة فيرد عليك نقض العبارة بالمخالفة وحسنها بالاستدراك. ودعوى الالتزام ليس بلازم في التزام المنقول. ومن التزام صحة حكمك عليه بأنه صحيح أو تقوية مقالك به مستدلا.
In explanation of debate on the assumption of transmission, whether it is transmission of a definition, division, or assent absolutely or otherwise. If you are a transmitter, then either you commit to the correctness of the transmitted statement or you do not. If you do not commit to its correctness, neither in wording nor meaning, whether that transmitted statement is a single term, a performative, an incomplete compound, a definition, a division, or an assent, then nothing is directed against you except the demand to verify the transmission, i.e., to demonstrate its correctness, if its correctness is not obvious, known, or conceded by the student, or among the necessities of his school. This is the meaning of 'denial of transmission.' And if a demand for transmission is directed against you, you may prove your transmission by presenting a book from which you transmitted, for example; for sometimes the one from whom you transmitted is a person, so you present him. If you commit to its correctness in meaning, i.e., conformity of the transmitted statement's relation to reality, this is not conceivable in a single term due to lack of relation in it, nor in a performative because its relation does not admit of conformity, nor in an incomplete compound that is not a qualifier of a complete compound due to lack of conformity in its relation, unlike the incomplete compound that is a qualifier of it; and also definitions, divisions, and assents. Then the previous investigations are directed against you, such as denial and discussion and their answers, in the chapter of definition if it was a definition, in the chapter of division if it was a division, and in the chapter of assent if it was an assent, unless it is obligatory to believe in it, which is the word of Allah, the word of His prophets, the possible matter upon which Muslims have consensus, and likewise what one is certain of by reason from matters of religion, or it is obvious, known, or conceded by the questioner. As for if you commit to the correctness of the wording of the transmitted statement—and this commitment applies to everything that has a relation—then there is directed against you the invalidation of the expression through contradiction and its improvement through substitution. The claim of commitment is not necessary in committing to the transmitted statement. And from committing to its correctness is your judgment upon it as correct or strengthening your statement by it, using it as evidence.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage from the Waldiyya commentary explains the rules governing transmission (naql) in dialectical debate (munazara). It opens by stating the topic: how should a debater handle transmitted material—whether it is a definition, a division, a proposition (tasdiq), or anything else. The key distinction is whether the transmitter (the person citing the source) commits to the truth of the transmitted content or not. If the transmitter does not commit to its truth at all (neither wording nor meaning), then the only valid objection that can be raised against them is a demand to verify the transmission itself—that is, to prove that the source actually said that. This is called 'denial of transmission' (man‘ al-naql). However, if the transmitter does commit to the truth of the meaning (i.e., they assert that the content corresponds to reality), then they open themselves to the full range of objections that apply to the type of content: denial (man‘) and discussion (munaqasha) and their answers, as detailed in the chapters on definition, division, and assent. The passage notes an exception: if the transmitted matter is something that must be believed (like divine speech, prophetic statements, consensus, or self-evident truths), then the normal objections are not applicable. If the transmitter commits only to the wording (not the meaning), then the objections are about the form: invalidation by contradiction or improvement by substitution. The passage ends by clarifying that the mere claim of commitment is not binding, but if one uses the transmission to strengthen one's own argument, then that implies commitment. The discussion is dialectical: it prescribes what moves are allowed and when, based on the transmitter's stance.

Key Terms
نقل
منع النقل
التزام الصحة
طلب تصحيح النقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a transmitter does not commit to the correctness of the transmitted statement, which of the following is correct?
A) The opponent can deny the meaning.
B) The opponent can demand verification of the transmission.
C) No objection is possible.
D) The opponent must accept the statement.

Key Takeaway
A transmitter's commitment to the truth of what he transmits determines the permissible objections: no commitment means only transmission itself can be questioned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about whether the Quran is created, one debater says: 'Ibn Hanbal said the Quran is uncreated.' He does not explicitly say he agrees. According to the rule, what is the only objection the opponent can raise? Formulate it.
?
If the same debater said: 'Ibn Hanbal's statement is correct: the Quran is uncreated,' what new types of objections become possible?

Commitment to Meaning vs. Wording and Exceptions
Explains the effects of committing to meaning vs. wording and the exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between committing to the meaning of a transmitted statement and committing only to its wording, and identify when objections are not allowed.

Core Idea
Committing to meaning opens the door; committing to wording only invites linguistic objections.

The Waldiyya commentary goes deeper: what if you commit only to the wording, not the meaning? And are there any transmitted statements that are simply immune to objection? This lesson explains the nuances for different types of transmitted material and the exceptions for divinely revealed or agreed-upon truths.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish commitment to meaning vs. wording
If you commit to the meaning (iltizam sihhat al-ma'na), you assert that the content corresponds to reality. If you commit only to the wording (iltizam sihhat al-lafz), you only vouch for the accuracy of the text.
Single terms and performatives cannot have meaning commitment due to lack of a truth-bearing relation.
2
Consequences of commitment to meaning
If you commit to meaning, the opponent can raise denial (man‘) and discussion (munaqasha) according to the type of transmitted content (definition, division, assent).
Unless the content is obligatory to believe.
3
Consequences of commitment to wording
If you commit only to wording, the opponent can raise invalidation of expression through contradiction (naqd al-ibara) and improvement by substitution (husn al-istidrak).
These are formal objections, not about truth.
4
Exceptions: obligatory beliefs
No objections are allowed for transmitted content that is the word of Allah, prophets, matters of consensus, or self-evident truths.
These are considered foundational and not subject to dialectical challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في بيان المناظرة على تقدير النقل سواء كان نقل تعريف أو تقسيم أو تصديق مطلقا أو غيرها. إن كنت ناقلا فإما أن تلتزم صحة المنقول أو لم تلتزم. فان لم تلتزم صحة المنقول لا لفظا ولا معنى سواء كان ذلك المنقول مفردا أو إنشاء أو مركبا ناقصا أو تعريفا أو تقسيما أو تصديقا فلا يرد عليك شيء إلا طلب تصحيح النقل أى بيان صحته إذ لم تكن صحته بديهيا جليا أو معلوما أو مسلما عند الطالب أو من ضروريات مذهبه. وهذا معنى منع النقل. وإذا ورد عليك طلب النقل فلك أن تثبت نقلك باحضار كتاب ما نقلته منه مثلا إذ قد يكون ما نقلته عنه شخصا فتحضره. وإن التزمت صحته معنى أى مطابقة نسبة المنقول للواقع وذا لا يتصور في المفرد لعدم النسبة فيه ولا في الانشاء إذ نسبته لا تحتمل المطابقة ولا في المركب الناقص الذي ليس بقيد للمركب التام لعدم المطابقة في نسبته بخلاف المركب الناقص الذى هو قيد له والتعريف والتقسيم والتصديق. فيرد عليك الأبحاث السابقة من المنع والمناقشة وأجوبتها في باب التعريف إن كان التعريف وفي باب التقسيم إن كان التقسيم وفي باب التصديق إن كان التصديق إلا أن يجب الإيمان به وهو قول الله وقول انبيائه والممكن الذي أجمع المسلمون عليه وكذا ما استيقن بعقله من أمور الدين أو يكون بديهيا جليا أو معلوما أو مسلما عند السائل. وأما إن التزمت صحة لفظ المنقول وهذا الالتزام في كل ما له نسبة فيرد عليك نقض العبارة بالمخالفة وحسنها بالاستدراك. ودعوى الالتزام ليس بلازم في التزام المنقول. ومن التزام صحة حكمك عليه بأنه صحيح أو تقوية مقالك به مستدلا.
In explanation of debate on the assumption of transmission, whether it is transmission of a definition, division, or assent absolutely or otherwise. If you are a transmitter, then either you commit to the correctness of the transmitted statement or you do not. If you do not commit to its correctness, neither in wording nor meaning, whether that transmitted statement is a single term, a performative, an incomplete compound, a definition, a division, or an assent, then nothing is directed against you except the demand to verify the transmission, i.e., to demonstrate its correctness, if its correctness is not obvious, known, or conceded by the student, or among the necessities of his school. When Objections Apply to Transmitted Statements
Rules for applying objections to transmitted statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a dialectical objection (man‘ or munāqaḍa) is applicable to a transmitted statement, based on the type of commitment and exceptions.

Core Idea
Objections are excluded only when belief is obligatory or the statement is self-evident.

In dialectic, when you cite a transmitted statement—like a Qur'anic verse or prophetic hadith—your opponent may object. However, the rules of debate specify when such objections are permitted. This lesson unpacks the conditions under which objections apply and when they are suspended, drawing from the matn's detailed exposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of commitment
First, determine whether the debater committed to the correctness of the meaning (ṣiḥḥat al-ma‘nā) or the wording (ṣiḥḥat al-lafẓ). The passage distinguishes these two forms of commitment, and the applicable objections differ accordingly.
Commitment to meaning opens the transmitted statement to objections in specific chapters; commitment to wording invites objections related to contradiction and correction.
2
Apply the three-chapter rule for meaning commitment
If the commitment is to the meaning, objections apply only in the chapters of definition (ta‘rīf), division (taqsīm), or assent (taṣdīq) — including incomplete composites that qualify a proposition. Check which chapter the transmitted statement belongs to.
For example, a transmitted definition can be objected to; a simple citation for strengthening may not be subject to objection.
3
Check for the exception of obligatory belief
If the transmitted statement is obligatory to believe (e.g., the word of Allah, words of prophets, matters of ijma‘, or certain rational certainties), then objections are not allowed. Also, if the statement is self-evidently obvious (badīhī jalī) or conceded by the questioner, objections are suspended.
Al-Shafi‘i disagreed on the exclusion for rational certainties; the passage notes this difference.
4
Distinguish between commitment and mere strengthening
The passage clarifies that a debater may not be truly committed to the transmitted statement; they may simply use it to strengthen their own argument (e.g., 'The world is temporally originated, as Allah said'). In such cases, the full force of objections does not apply.
This prevents automatic objection when a transmitted statement is cited as supporting evidence rather than as a definitive proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيرد عليك الأبحاث السابقة من المنع والمناقشة وأجوبتها في باب التعريف إن كان المنقول الذي التزم صحته تعريفا، وفي باب التقسيم إن كان تقسيما، وفي باب التصديق إن كان تصديقا أو مركبا ناقصا كان قيدا للقضية، إلا أن يجب الإيمان به. وقيل: يرد عليك الأبحاث المذكورة في جميع الأوقات إلا وقت وجوب الإيمان بمضمون ذلك المنقول، وهو قول الله وقول أنبيائه، والممكن الذي أجمع المسلمون عليه، وكذا ما استيقن بعقله من أمور الدين خلافا للشافعي رحمه الله، أو يكون بديهيا جليا أو معلوما أو مسلما عند السائل. وأما إن التزمت صحة لفظ المنقول، وهذا الالتزام في كل ما له نسبة، فيرد عليك نقض العبارة بالمخالفة وحسنها بالاستدراك، وقد سبق بيان المخلص. هكذا حقق بعض الفضلاء. ودعوى الالتزام ليس بلازم في التزام المنقول، بل من التزام صحة حكمك عليه بأنه صحيح أو تقوية مقالك به، كان تقول: العالم حادث كما قال الله تعالى.
So the previous investigations (objections) of prevention (man'), discussion (munāqaḍa), and their answers apply to you (i.e., to your transmitted statement) in the chapter of definition if the transmitted statement whose correctness you committed to is a definition; and in the chapter of division if it is a division; and in the chapter of assent if it is an assent or an incomplete composite that is a qualification of the proposition, except if belief in it is obligatory. And it is said: the mentioned investigations apply to you at all times except the time of the obligation of belief in the content of that transmitted statement, which is the word of Allah and the word of His prophets, and what is possible that the Muslims have agreed upon, and likewise what is certain by reason from the matters of religion, contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, or it is evidentially obvious, or known, or conceded by the questioner. But if you commit to the correctness of the wording of the transmitted statement, and this commitment is in everything that has a relationship, then the refutation (naqḍ) of the expression by contradiction and its improvement by correction apply to you, and the remedy has been previously explained. Thus some virtuous persons verified. And the claim of commitment is not necessary in committing to the transmitted statement; rather, from the commitment of its correctness is your judgment upon it that it is correct or your strengthening of your statement by it, as you say: 'The world is temporally originated, as Allah the Exalted said.'
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules for applying objections (al-abḥāth al-sābiqa: man‘, munāqaḍa, and their answers) to a transmitted statement (al-manqūl) in dialectic. It first states the general rule: the objections apply to the transmitter's commitment in three cases: if the transmitted statement is a definition (in the chapter of definition), a division (in the chapter of division), or an assent or an incomplete composite qualifying a proposition (in the chapter of assent). The exception is when belief in that transmitted statement is obligatory. The author then elaborates on the exception: the objections do not apply at the time when belief in the content is wajib, such as the words of Allah and His prophets, matters of ijma‘ (consensus), and what is known by certain reason (except for some differences with al-Shafi‘i). Additionally, if the transmitted statement is self-evidently obvious (badīhī jalī) or known (ma‘lūm) or conceded (musallam) by the questioner, then objections are also not applicable. The passage then distinguishes between committing to the meaning (ṣiḥḥat al-ma‘nā) and committing to the wording (ṣiḥḥat al-lafẓ). If one commits to the wording, then objections regarding contradiction (naqḍ al-‘ibāra bil-mukhālafa) and improvement by correction (ḥusnuhā bil-istidrāk) still apply, and the remedy has been previously explained. Finally, the author clarifies that the claim of commitment is not necessary; rather, commitment can be in the form of a judgment of correctness or strengthening one's own argument by citing the transmitted statement as evidence.

مناقشة
التزام
بديهي جلي

D) When the transmitted statement is used in the chapter of assent

Key Takeaway
Objections to transmitted statements are permitted in specific dialectical contexts unless the statement is obligatory to believe or self-evidently obvious.

The Termination of Debate and the Terms Ifhām and Ilzām
Author defines 'bahth', ends debate, and coins ifhām/ilzām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three technical meanings of 'bahth', reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving that every debate must end, and distinguish ifhām from ilzām.

Core Idea
Dialectical debate must end; ifhām and ilzām name the two inevitable outcomes.

This concluding section defines the key term 'bahth' as used in dialectic and then proves that every debate must end in one of two ways: either the respondent is silenced (ifhām) or the questioner is compelled (ilzām). Understanding these definitions and the logical proof equips you to analyze actual debates in Kalam and usul al-fiqh, where these outcomes are the decisive moments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three technical meanings of 'bahth'
Find the three meanings listed in the Arabic: (1) carrying one thing onto another (predication), (2) affirming a positive or negative relation through inference, (3) the dialectical debate itself. Note that the third is the intended one for the context.
The first two meanings are general logical operations; the third is specific to dialectic.
2
Extract the categorical syllogism
Locate the major premise (every debate that ends in one of the two incapacities is terminated), the minor premise (the debate between them ends in one of the two incapacities), and the conclusion (so the debate must terminate). Write them in propositional form.
The author also provides a conditional reconstruction: if the debate did not end in one of the two incapacities, it would go on infinitely, which is impossible.
3
Analyze the conditional reconstruction
The author says the argument can also be framed as a conditional (modus tollens): if the debate does not end in one of the two incapacities, it continues infinitely; but infinite continuation is impossible; therefore it must end in one of the two incapacities. Verify that this is logically equivalent.
This shows the same proof in a different logical form.
4
Distinguish ifhām and ilzām
Ifhām is the incapacity of the mu'allil (respondent) to answer; ilzām is the incapacity of the questioner to object. Recognize that both are passive participial forms: muflıam (silenced) and mulzam (compelled).
The author clarifies that ifhām is added to the mu'allil as an object to avoid the misunderstanding that the mu'allil is the one doing the silencing.
5
Clarify the passive participle usage
Note that ifhām and ilzām are masādir built for the passive (mabni lil-maf‘ūl). Thus 'ifhām al-mu'allil' means 'the silencing of the mu'allil' (he is silenced), not 'the mu'allil silences'. Avoid the common confusion.
This grammatical point prevents misinterpretation of who is active.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة: من عادة المؤلفين إذا فرغوا من أنواع مصنفاتهم أن يوردوا خاتمة لتكون تتمة للكلام وتحسينا للمقاصد والمرام. ثم اعلم أن الخاتمة مقابلة للفاتحة وفيها براعة الاستهلال لأن الفاتحة كما تدل على الأبحاث الآتية إجمالا تدل الخاتمة على الأبحاث الماضية إجمالا. ثم بعد الفراغ من المناظرة (وهي ابتدائية وقيل عاطفة ولا يخفى ضعفه) نقول: إن البحث يطلق في اللغة على التفتيش، وفي العرف على ثلاثة معان: الأول حمل الشيء على الشيء وإثباته له، والثاني إثبات النسبة الإيجابية أو السلبية بالاستدلال، والثالث المناظرة التي هي صفة المناظرين، وهي النظر بالبصيرة من الجانبين في النسبة بين الشيئين إظهارا للصواب، وهو المراد هنا. ثم إن البحث بين المتناظرين إما أن ينتهي إلى عجز المعلل وسكوته عن دفع اعتراض السائل، أو ينتهي إلى عجز السائل عن الاعتراض على جواب المعلل، إذ لا يمكن جريان البحث بينهما إلى غير النهاية. وحاصل الدليل أن البحث بينهما منته إلى أحد العجزين، وكل بحث منته إلى أحد العجزين منقطع، فالبحث بينهما لا بد أن ينقطع. أما الكبرى فبديهية، وأما الصغرى فبينها بقوله إذ لا يمكن الخ. ويمكن أن يكون من الاستثنائي غير المستقيم بأن يقال: إن البحث بينهما منته إلى أحد العجزين وإلا لأمكن جريان البحث إلى غير النهاية، لكن لا يمكن جريان البحث إلى غير النهاية لقصور القوة البشرية عن ترتيب أمور غير متناهية. وعجز المعلل من حيث هو معلل (وكذا عجز المعرف والمقسم) يسمى في عرف المناظرين إفحاما، وعجز السائل من حيث هو سائل إلزاما، وهما مصدران مبنيان للمفعول أو من قبيل تسمية اللازم باسم الملزوم، لكن اللاحق يقتضي السابق ويعمهما التبكيت. ويقال: أفحم السائل المعلل أي أعجزه وأسكته، ويقال: ألزم المعلل السائل أي جعله ملزما. ويقال: المعلل مفحم والسائل ملزم بفتح الحاء والزاي. فإضافة الإفحام إلى المعلل إضافة المصدر المبني للمفعول إلى مفعوله، فلا يرد أن الإفحام يكون عبارة عن إسكات المعلل فكيف يكون عبارة عن عجز المعلل. وكذا الإضافة في إلزام السائل. ثم أن السؤال قد يكون بمعنى الاعتراض (سواء كان منعا أو نقضا أو معارضة) يقال: سألت عليه أي اعترضت عليه، فذا سؤال المناظرين. وقد يكون بمعنى الاستفسار عن.
Conclusion: It is the custom of authors, when they finish the sections of their works, to produce a conclusion so that it is a completion of the speech and an improvement of the aims and objectives. Then know that the conclusion corresponds to the opening and has the beauty of beginning, because just as the opening indicates the upcoming investigations in summary, the conclusion indicates the past investigations in summary. Then after finishing the debate (which is a beginning, and it is said it is a conjunction, and its weakness is not hidden), we say: 'bahth' is used in language for 'searching'; in technical usage it has three meanings: first, carrying one thing onto another and affirming it for it; second, affirming a positive or negative relation through inference; third, the debate which is the characteristic of the disputants, and it is the insight-based consideration from both sides concerning the relation between two things to manifest correctness, and that is what is intended here. Then the debate between the disputants either ends in the incapacity of the debate-respondent (mu'allil) and his silence from repelling the questioner's objection, or ends in the incapacity of the questioner to object to the answer of the respondent, because it is not possible for the debate between them to continue to infinity. The summary of the proof is that the debate between them ends in one of the two incapacities, and every debate that ends in one of the two incapacities is terminated, so the debate between them must necessarily terminate. As for the major premise, it is self-evident; as for the minor premise, it is explained by his saying 'because it is not possible, etc.' And it is possible that it is from the indirect conditional argument, such that it is said: the debate between them ends in one of the two incapacities, otherwise the debate could continue to infinity, but it is not possible for the debate to continue to infinity due to the weakness of human power to arrange infinite matters. The incapacity of the mu'allil (and likewise the incapacity of the definer and the divider) is called in the terminology of the disputants 'ifhām', and the incapacity of the questioner is called 'ilzām', and they are two passive participles or from the category of naming the necessary by the name of the necessitating, but the latter implies the former and both share 'tabkīt'. And it is said: 'the questioner silenced the mu'allil', i.e., made him incapable and silenced him; and it is said: 'the mu'allil compelled the questioner', i.e., made him obligated. And it is said: 'the mu'allil is silenced (muflıam) and the questioner is compelled (mulzam)' with fatḥa on the ḥā' and zāy. So the addition of 'ifhām' to the mu'allil is the addition of the passive participle to its object, so there is no objection that ifhām is the silencing of the mu'allil, so how can it be the incapacity of the mu'allil? Likewise the addition in 'ilzām' of the questioner. Then, 'su'āl' may be in the sense of objection (whether it is prevention, refutation, or opposition); it is said: 'I asked against him', meaning I objected to him; that is the question of the disputants. And it may be in the sense of asking for explanation.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is the closing section (khātima) of a work on dialectic, where the author defines technical terms used by disputants (nuzzār). It begins by explaining the purpose of a conclusion: to summarize past investigations and improve the work's structure, similar to how an opening summarizes what is to come. Then it defines the term 'baḥth' (research/investigation): in language it means 'searching'; in technical usage it has three meanings: (1) carrying one thing onto another and affirming it (which is essentially predication), (2) affirming a positive or negative relation through inference (i.e., reasoning), and (3) the dialectical debate itself, which is the mutual consideration by both sides to discover the truth—this is the intended meaning here. The author then presents a logical argument that every debate must end. The argument is a categorical syllogism: the debate between two disputants must end in one of two incapacities (either the mu'allil cannot respond, or the questioner cannot object), and every debate that ends in such an incapacity is terminated, therefore the debate must terminate. He also provides a conditional reconstruction: if the debate did not end in one of the two incapacities, it would go on infinitely, but it cannot go on infinitely due to human limitation. The two outcomes are named: ifhām (silencing) for the mu'allil's incapacity, and ilzām (compelling) for the questioner's incapacity. Both are passive participial forms (or metonyms). He clarifies that ifhām is added to the mu'allil as an object, not subject, to avoid the confusion that ifhām would mean the mu'allil silences someone else. Finally, he distinguishes two meanings of 'su'āl': objection (i‘tirāḍ) in dialectical contexts, and asking for explanation (istifsār) in general.

Key Terms
بحث
إفحام
إلزام
سؤال

Key Takeaway
Dialectical debate is defined as mutual insight-based consideration to uncover truth, and it must terminate in either ifhām (silencing of the respondent) or ilzām (compelling of the questioner).

The Effect of Man' and Naqd on Proof
Author explains that man' and naqd leave the claim without proof, not necessarily invalidate it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between man' (prevention of a premise) and naqd (refutation of the proof) and explain why neither automatically disproves the claim, using the concepts of malzum (necessary) and lazim (consequent).

Core Idea
Refuting a proof does not refute the claim; the claim may have another proof.

In dialectical debates (jadal), objections often target the premises or the reasoning of an argument. A common mistake is to think that dismantling one proof destroys the claim itself. This lesson clarifies the logical relationship between the proof (malzum) and the claim (lazim), showing that a claim can survive even when a particular proof is invalidated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
Determine whether the objection is man' (denying a premise) or naqd (showing the proof leads to absurdity). The author states that both leave the claim without proof, but they operate differently.
Man' targets a premise; naqd targets the proof's validity.
2
Understand the proof-claim relationship
The proof (dalil) is the malzum (necessary condition) and the claim (da'wa) is the lazim (consequent). Invalidation of the malzum does not entail invalidation of the lazim because there could be alternative malzums. The author explicitly says: 'invalidation of the necessary does not necessitate invalidation of the consequent.'
This is the key logical insight.
3
Analyze the example from Arabic grammar
Work through the example: Claimant says 'daraba is a past tense verb because it indicates time before speech.' The objector refutes (naqd) the proof by showing it entails a contradiction. The claimant then provides an alternative proof: 'daraba by its form indicates past time by convention.' Thus the claim remains supported.
The example demonstrates that a claim can have multiple proofs.
4
Apply to mu'arada (mutual cancellation)
When both sides present proofs and they cancel each other, neither claim is proven. The author describes this as the objector saying 'my proof invalidates your claim, so it invalidates your proof.' This leads to no valid evidence for either side.
Mu'arada is a more comprehensive objection.

فصل في بيان مراتب المنوع في القوة والضعف. اعلم أن حاصل منع مقدمة الدليل ونقضه إبقاء دعوى المعلل بلا دليل. وذلك لأنه لو كانت المقدمة ممنوعة كانت خفية، ولو كانت خفية لم يكن الدليل ثابتا، وإذا لم يكن ثابتا لا يثبت به شيء، فيبقى المدعى بلا دليل. وكذا لو كان الدليل منقوضا. وليس حاصل نقضه إبطالا لدعوى المعلل، إذ الدليل ملزوم للدعوى لزوما قطعيا أو ظنيا. ولا يلزم من إبطال الملزوم إبطال اللازم، إذ يجوز أن يكون للازم ملزوم آخر. فإذا جاز عموم اللازم، فيجوز أن يكون للمدعى دليل آخر. كما إذا قال المعلل: ضرب فعل ماض لأنه يدل على زمان قبل زمان إخبارك، وكل لفظ شأنه كذا فعل ماض. فنقض السائل دليله بأنه باطل لأنه يستلزم ظرفية الشيء لنفسه وهو محال، وكل دليل يستلزم المحال فهو باطل. فإن للمعلل هنا دليلا آخر وهو أن ضرب يدل بهيئته وضعا على زمان مقدم، وكل لفظ شأنه كذا فعل ماض. فإذا بقيت دعوى المعلل عند إبطال دليله بلا دليل، فالبقاء عند منع مقدمة دليله بالطريق الأولى. وكذا حاصل المعارضة المساقطة: أعنى أن يسقط ويبطل دليل المعارض دليل المعلل وبالعكس، وذلك لأن المدعى لازم والدليل ملزوم، فيبطل الملزوم فيبطل لازمه. فكأن المعارض يقول: إن دليلي أبطل دعواك فيبطل دليلك.
Section on explaining the levels of the prevented (objection) in strength and weakness. Know that the outcome of preventing a premise of the proof and refuting it is to leave the claim of the one providing the proof without a proof. That is because if the premise is prevented, it is obscure; if it is obscure, the proof is not established; and if it is not established, nothing is proven by it, so the claimant remains without a proof. Similarly if the proof is refuted. And the outcome of refuting it is not the invalidation of the claim of the one providing the proof, because the proof is necessary for the claim with a necessary or probable necessity. And invalidation of the necessary does not necessitate invalidation of the consequent, because it is possible that the consequent has another necessary. So if the generality of the consequent is possible, then it is possible that the claimant has another proof. As when the one providing the proof says: 'Daraba is a past tense verb because it indicates a time before your time of informing, and every word whose nature is like that is a past tense verb.' Then the questioner refuted his proof by saying it is invalid because it entails the thing being a container of itself, which is impossible, and every proof that entails impossibility is invalid. For the one providing the proof here has another proof, which is that 'daraba' by its form indicates, by convention, a past time, and every word whose nature is like that is a past tense verb. So if the claim of the one providing the proof remains without proof when his proof is invalidated, then it remains even more so when a premise of his proof is prevented. Similarly, the outcome of counter-argument (al-mu'arada) is mutual cancellation: meaning that the proof of the objector invalidates the proof of the one providing the proof, and vice versa. That is because the claim is a consequent and the proof is a necessary; if the necessary is invalidated, its consequent is invalidated. So it is as if the objector says: 'My proof invalidates your claim, so it invalidates your proof.'
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a section (fasl) on the ranks of objections (manu') in strength and weakness. The author explains the effect of two types of objections: man' (prevention of a premise) and naqd (refutation of the proof). The immediate outcome is that the claimant (al-mu'allil) is left without a proof for his claim. However, the author emphasizes that this does not automatically disprove the claim itself. The reason is that the proof serves as a necessary condition (malzum) for the claim (lazim), but invalidation of a particular necessary does not entail invalidation of the consequent, because the claim might have another necessary—i.e., another proof. The author illustrates with a concrete example from Arabic grammar: a claimant argues that the word 'daraba' (he hit) is a past tense verb because it indicates a time before the moment of speech. The objector refutes this proof by showing it leads to a contradiction (a thing being a container of itself), thus invalidating the proof. However, the claimant can provide a different proof: that 'daraba' by its morphological form (hay'a) conventionally indicates a past time, which is a sound proof. Thus, the claim remains intact despite one proof being refuted. The same logic applies when a premise is prevented; the claim may still hold if another proof exists. Finally, the author discusses mu'arada (counter-argument), where both proofs (the original and the objection) are cancelled out, leaving no valid proof for either side. This demonstrates that objections do not automatically disprove a claim; they only remove the specific proof offered.

النقض
المعارضة المساقطة
الملزوم

Key Takeaway
Man' and naqd remove the specific proof but do not disprove the claim; the claimant may have another proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

B objects by naqd: 'This proof would also forbid fermented foods that cause mild intoxication, which is not the case.' Every linguistic revelation free of error is the word of Allah.' Effects of Man' and Naqd in Munazara
Author explains how man' and naqd leave the claim without evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical effects of man' (blocking a premise) and naqd (refuting the whole argument) in a munazara, and explain why neither disproves the claim itself.

Core Idea
Blocking a premise and refuting an argument both remove evidence, but do not disprove the claim.

In Islamic dialectics, objections are categorized into those attacking a single premise (man') and those attacking the entire argument (naqd). Many students mistakenly think that successfully refuting an argument disproves the claim itself. This lesson clarifies the correct position: both types of objections only remove one piece of evidence, leaving the possibility that the claim stands on other grounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objection types
Find where the author defines man' (blocking a premise) and naqd (refuting the entire evidence). Notice that man' targets a premise within the evidence, while naqd attacks the whole argument structure.
2
Understand the consequence for the evidence
The author explains that if a premise is blocked, the evidence becomes unestablished; if the evidence is refuted, it is similarly void. In both cases, the evidence cannot support the claim.
3
Distinguish between removing evidence and disproving the claim
The author emphasizes that neither man' nor naqd invalidates the claim itself. They only leave the claim without evidence, because the claim may have other independent supports.
This is the key insight.
4
Apply to a hypothetical debate
Imagine a jurist argues: 'Wine is forbidden because it is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' If the opponent blocks the premise that wine is an intoxicant, the evidence fails but the ruling might still be proven by another evidence, e.g., a direct text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان مراتب المنع في القوة والضعف. اعلم أن حاصل منع مقدمة الدليل ونقضه إبقاء دعوى المعلل بلا دليل. وذلك لأنه لو كانت المقدمة ممنوعة كانت إذ المنع يفيد خفاء الممنوع، ولو كانت خفية لم يكن الدليل ثابتا، وإذا لم يكن ثابتا لا يثبت به شيء آخر فيبقى المدعى بلا دليل، وكذا لو كان الدليل منقوضا وهو ظاهر. وقيل في وجهه: لأن ما لم يثبت لا يثبت به شيء، وهذا أخصر. وليس حاصل نقضه إبطالا لدعوى المعلل، وليس حاصل المنع أيضا إبطالا لدعوى المعلل، إذ الدليل ملزوم للدعوى لزوما قطعيا وهو الدليل القطعي، والأمارة التي في صورة القياس أو لزوما ظنيا وهو الأمارة التي ليست في صورة القياس وهو الاستقراء والتمثيل. كذا قيل ولا يخفى ما فيه فتأمل فيه. ولا يلزم من إبطال الملزوم ولو بلزوم قطعي إبطال اللازم، إذ يجوز أن يكون لذلك اللازم ملزوم آخر، لجواز عموم اللازم من الملزوم كالحرارة اللازمة للشمس، وإذا جاز عموم اللازم فيجوز أن يكون للمدعى المنقوض دليله دليل آخر، كما إذا قال المعلل: ضرب فعل ماض لأنه يدل على زمان قبل زمان إخبارك وكل لفظ شأنه كذا فعل ماض، ونقض السائل دليله بأنه باطل لأنه يستلزم ظرفية الشيء لنفسه وهو محال وكل دليل يستلزم المحال فهو باطل، فان للمعلل هنا دليلا آخر وهو أن ضرب يدل بهيئته وضعا على زمان مقدم وكل لفظ شأنه كذا فعل ماض. فإذا بقي مدعى المعلل عند إبطال دليله بلا دليل، فالبقاء عند منع مقدمة دليله بالطريق الأولى.
Chapter on explaining the ranks of objection in strength and weakness. Know that the outcome of blocking a premise of the evidence and refuting it is leaving the claim of the evidence-giver without evidence. That is because if the premise were blocked, then the blocking indicates obscurity of the blocked, and if it were obscure, the evidence would not be established, and if it is not established, nothing else can be established by it, so the claim remains without evidence. Similarly if the evidence itself were refuted, and that is apparent. It was said in its explanation: because what is not established cannot establish anything else, and this is more concise. And the outcome of refutation is not invalidation of the claim of the evidence-giver, and the outcome of blocking is also not invalidation of the claim of the evidence-giver, because the evidence is a necessary condition for the claim, either with definitive necessity—which is definitive evidence—or with probable necessity—which is an indication not in the form of a syllogism, namely induction and analogy. Thus it was said, and what is in it is not hidden, so reflect on it. And invalidation of the necessary condition does not entail invalidation of the necessary consequence, even if the necessity is definitive, because it is possible that the necessary consequence has another necessary condition, due to the possibility of the necessary consequence being common to multiple conditions, like heat which is necessary to the sun. And if commonness of the necessary consequence is possible, then it is possible that the claimant whose evidence was refuted has another evidence, as when the evidence-giver said: 'Daraba is a past tense verb because it indicates a time before the time of your utterance, and every word whose property is such is a past tense verb.' And the opponent refuted his evidence that it is invalid because it entails the thing being a container for itself, which is impossible, and every evidence that entails impossibility is invalid. So the evidence-giver here has another evidence, which is that daraba indicates by its form, by convention, a preceding time, and every word whose property is such is a past tense verb. So if the claim of the evidence-giver remains without evidence upon invalidation of his evidence, then remaining upon blocking of his premise is all the more likely.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion of the effects of two types of objections in munazara: man' (blocking or denying a premise) and naqd (refuting the argument itself). The author states that both result in leaving the claim without evidence, not in directly disproving the claim. The reasoning is that if a premise is blocked, the evidence becomes unestablished, and if the evidence is unestablished, it cannot prove anything else; similarly if the argument is refuted, it is void. However, the author emphasizes that this does not invalidate the claim itself, because the evidence is not the only possible ground (luzum) for the claim. The relationship between evidence and claim is that of necessary condition (malzum) and necessary consequence (lazim). Invalidation of the necessary condition does not necessarily invalidate the necessary consequence, because the consequence may have another condition. The author illustrates with a linguistic example from Arabic grammar: the claim that 'daraba' (he struck) is a past tense verb because it indicates a time before the utterance; the opponent refutes this by arguing that it would entail a self-contradiction (something being a container for itself). The evidence-giver then provides another evidence: that 'daraba' by its form conventionally indicates a preceding time. Thus, even if one evidence is refuted, the claim may still be supported by another. The passage concludes that the same reasoning applies even more strongly to man' (blocking a premise) as opposed to naqd (refuting the whole argument).

Key Terms
منع
نقض
ملزوم
لازم

The Principle of Multiple Evidences (Lazim and Malzum)
Author explains the malzum-lazim relationship and multiple conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why invalidating a necessary condition (malzum) does not necessarily invalidate the necessary consequence (lazim), using the concept of multiple conditions.

Core Idea
A consequence can have multiple conditions; removing one does not negate it.

A key insight in Islamic dialectics is that a claim (the necessary consequence) may be connected to more than one evidence (necessary condition). If one evidence is refuted, the claim may still stand on another. This lesson unpacks the logical relationship between malzum and lazim as presented by the author, with the sun-and-heat analogy and the grammar example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the malzum-lazim relationship
The author states that the evidence (dalil) is a 'malzum' (necessary condition) for the claim (mad'ua), which is the 'lazim' (necessary consequence). This means if the evidence is true, the claim must be true. But the converse is not necessarily true: the claim can be true even if that particular evidence is false.
2
Understand the possibility of multiple malzums
The author says 'invalidation of the malzum does not entail invalidation of the lazim' because the lazim may have another malzum. He gives the example of heat: heat is a lazim of the sun, but heat also occurs from fire. So if someone argues that heat comes from the sun, and that argument is refuted, heat still exists from fire.
This is the core logical principle.
3
Apply to the grammar example
In the example, the claim that 'daraba' is past tense (lazim) has two possible malzums: (1) it indicates a past time by implication, and (2) its form conventionally indicates past time. The first malzum is refuted, but the second remains, so the claim still stands.
4
Generalize to Islamic legal debates
In usul al-fiqh, a ruling (hukm) may have multiple proofs: a verse, a hadith, or an analogy. If one proof is refuted, the ruling may still be established by another. This prevents premature defeat in munazara.

فصل في بيان مراتب المنع في القوة والضعف. اعلم أن حاصل منع مقدمة الدليل ونقضه إبقاء دعوى المعلل بلا دليل. وذلك لأنه لو كانت المقدمة ممنوعة كانت إذ المنع يفيد خفاء الممنوع، ولو كانت خفية لم يكن الدليل ثابتا، وإذا لم يكن ثابتا لا يثبت به شيء آخر فيبقى المدعى بلا دليل، وكذا لو كان الدليل منقوضا وهو ظاهر. وقيل في وجهه: لأن ما لم يثبت لا يثبت به شيء، وهذا أخصر. وليس حاصل نقضه إبطالا لدعوى المعلل، وليس حاصل المنع أيضا إبطالا لدعوى المعلل، إذ الدليل ملزوم للدعوى لزوما قطعيا وهو الدليل القطعي، والأمارة التي في صورة القياس أو لزوما ظنيا وهو الأمارة التي ليست في صورة القياس وهو الاستقراء والتمثيل. كذا قيل ولا يخفى ما فيه فتأمل فيه. ولا يلزم من إبطال الملزوم ولو بلزوم قطعي إبطال اللازم، إذ يجوز أن يكون لذلك اللازم ملزوم آخر، لجواز عموم اللازم من الملزوم كالحرارة اللازمة للشمس، وإذا جاز عموم اللازم فيجوز أن يكون للمدعى المنقوض دليله دليل آخر، كما إذا قال المعلل: ضرب فعل ماض لأنه يدل على زمان قبل زمان إخبارك وكل لفظ شأنه كذا فعل ماض، ونقض السائل دليله بأنه باطل لأنه يستلزم ظرفية الشيء لنفسه وهو محال وكل دليل يستلزم المحال فهو باطل، فان للمعلل هنا دليلا آخر وهو أن ضرب يدل بهيئته وضعا على زمان مقدم وكل لفظ شأنه كذا فعل ماض. فإذا بقي مدعى المعلل عند إبطال دليله بلا دليل، فالبقاء عند منع مقدمة دليله بالطريق الأولى.
Chapter on explaining the ranks of objection in strength and weakness. Know that the outcome of blocking a premise of the evidence and refuting it is leaving the claim of the evidence-giver without evidence. That is because if the premise were blocked, then the blocking indicates obscurity of the blocked, and if it were obscure, the evidence would not be established, and if it is not established, nothing else can be established by it, so the claim remains without evidence. Similarly if the evidence itself were refuted, and that is apparent. It was said in its explanation: because what is not established cannot establish anything else, and this is more concise. And the outcome of refutation is not invalidation of the claim of the evidence-giver, and the outcome of blocking is also not invalidation of the claim of the evidence-giver, because the evidence is a necessary condition for the claim, either with definitive necessity—which is definitive evidence—or with probable necessity—which is an indication not in the form of a syllogism, namely induction and analogy. Thus it was said, and what is in it is not hidden, so reflect on it. And invalidation of the necessary condition does not entail invalidation of the necessary consequence, even if the necessity is definitive, because it is possible that the necessary consequence has another necessary condition, due to the possibility of the necessary consequence being common to multiple conditions, like heat which is necessary to the sun. And if commonness of the necessary consequence is possible, then it is possible that the claimant whose evidence was refuted has another evidence, as when the evidence-giver said: 'Daraba is a past tense verb because it indicates a time before the time of your utterance, and every word whose property is such is a past tense verb.' And the opponent refuted his evidence that it is invalid because it entails the thing being a container for itself, which is impossible, and every evidence that entails impossibility is invalid. So the evidence-giver here has another evidence, which is that daraba indicates by its form, by convention, a preceding time, and every word whose property is such is a past tense verb. So if the claim of the evidence-giver remains without evidence upon invalidation of his evidence, then remaining upon blocking of his premise is all the more likely.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion of the effects of two types of objections in munazara: man' (blocking or denying a premise) and naqd (refuting the argument itself). The author states that both result in leaving the claim without evidence, not in directly disproving the claim. The reasoning is that if a premise is blocked, the evidence becomes unestablished, and if the evidence is unestablished, it cannot prove anything else; similarly if the argument is refuted, it is void. However, the author emphasizes that this does not invalidate the claim itself, because the evidence is not the only possible ground (luzum) for the claim. The relationship between evidence and claim is that of necessary condition (malzum) and necessary consequence (lazim). Invalidation of the necessary condition does not necessarily invalidate the necessary consequence, because the consequence may have another condition. The author illustrates with a linguistic example from Arabic grammar: the claim that 'daraba' (he struck) is a past tense verb because it indicates a time before the utterance; the opponent refutes this by arguing that it would entail a self-contradiction (something being a container for itself). The evidence-giver then provides another evidence: that 'daraba' by its form conventionally indicates a preceding time. Thus, even if one evidence is refuted, the claim may still be supported by another. The passage concludes that the same reasoning applies even more strongly to man' (blocking a premise) as opposed to naqd (refuting the whole argument).

Key Terms
ملزوم
لازم
عموم اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's analogy, what does heat from the sun illustrate?
A) The sun is the only source of heat.
B) Heat can have multiple causes (malzums).
C) Refuting the sun as a cause disproves all heat.
D) The sun's heat is definitive.

Key Takeaway
A claim (lazim) may have multiple evidences (malzums); refuting one evidence does not destroy the claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example from Islamic jurisprudence where a ruling (e.g., the obligation of zakat on silver) has multiple proofs (Qur'an, Sunnah, consensus). Explain how refuting one proof (e.g., questioning a hadith's authenticity) does not invalidate the ruling because other proofs still stand.
?
Construct a simple logical diagram showing how a single lazim (conclusion) can have multiple malzums (premises). Label each malzum as a different evidence for the same claim.

Grammar Example: Refuting and Replacing Evidence
Author illustrates multiple evidences with a grammar debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the grammar example from the text, identifying how the claimant's first evidence is refuted and how a second evidence is provided to sustain the original claim.

Core Idea
A refuted argument can be replaced by a new argument for the same conclusion.

The author provides a concrete example from Arabic grammar to illustrate the principle of multiple evidences. A claimant argues that the word 'daraba' is a past tense verb based on one indication; the opponent refutes that indication; the claimant then provides a different, valid indication. This lesson breaks down the logical structure of that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the first evidence
The claim: 'daraba is a past tense verb.' The first evidence: 'It indicates a time before the time of your utterance, and every word that indicates a past time is a past tense verb.'
2
Understand the opponent's refutation (naqd)
The opponent refutes the first evidence by showing it entails an impossibility: that the word is a container for itself. This makes the evidence invalid because any evidence leading to impossibility is false.
The opponent does not block a premise; he attacks the whole argument's logical consequence.
3
See how the claimant provides a second evidence
The claimant says: 'daraba indicates by its form, by convention, a preceding time.' This is a different basis for the same claim. The opponent's refutation targeted the first evidence, not the claim itself.
4
Apply the principle to a fiqh example
Consider the claim: 'Riba al-fadl (excess in barter) is prohibited.' First evidence: 'The Prophet prohibited exchanging wheat for wheat with excess.' Opponent might refute that hadith as weak. Second evidence: 'Consensus of scholars on the prohibition.' The claim remains supported.
